Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
Principle of Firar: Divorce During Terminal Illness
Definition and expansion of firar with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of firar to determine whether a divorced wife inherits when the husband divorces during a life-threatening situation.

Core Idea
Divorce during terminal illness triggers inheritance if death is the dominant expectation – this is firar.

The principle of firar (فرار) is a special rule in Hanafi law that prevents a husband from using divorce to deprive his wife of inheritance when he is terminally ill. The key is not just the illness but the likelihood of death. This lesson unpacks the cause (ʿillah) of firar, contrasts it with safety-dominant scenarios, and applies it to various examples from the classical texts, including drowning, battle, and chronic illness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and legal question
The act is a divorce pronounced by a husband who is seriously ill or in a life-threatening situation. The legal question: does the wife inherit despite the divorce?
2
Understand the cause (ʿillah) of firar
The effective cause is the dominant expectation of death (ghalabat al-halāk) at the time of divorce. If death is more likely than survival, the divorce is considered a flight from inheritance, so the wife still inherits.
3
Contrast the safety-dominant scenario
If the husband divorces when safety (salāma) is dominant, e.g., being besieged in a fortress or in a battle line, there is no firar and the wife does not inherit.
4
Apply the principle to new cases
The text lists examples: drowning, shipwreck, falling into a beast's mouth, chronic worsening illness (first category – death dominant); minor ailments not confining to bed are like healthy (no firar).
5
Note source authority and cross-madhhab difference
This principle is derived from Hanafi sources (Al-Hidayah, Al-Binayah). Other schools may not recognize firar, so the wife’s inheritance depends on whether death was dominant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
میں طلاق دی ہے کہ اس کی موت غالب تھی اور یہی فرار کی علت ہے، لہذا جب فرار کی علت پائی گئی تو فرار کا حکم ثابت ہوگا اور عورت وارث ہوگی ، اور اگر اسی حالت میں طلاق دی جس میں سلامتی غالب تو پھر عورت وارث نہ ہوگی اور اس طلاق دینے سے فرار ثابت نہیں ہوگا، جیسے محصور شخص اور قتال کی صف میں موجود شخص کے حق میں سلامتی غالب رہتی ہے، کیونکہ قلعہ دشمن کے حملوں سے حفاظت کے لئے تعمیر کیا جاتا ہے، اسی طرح جنگ کی صف بندی میں پورالشکر ہوتا ہے جو ہمہ وقت اپنے ساتھیوں کی حفاظت میں لگا رہتا ہے، چونکہ ان دونوں کے حق میں سلامتی غالب ہے، لہذا ایسے وقت میں طلاق دینے سے فرار ثابت نہیں ہو گا۔

والأصل المذكور هو ثبوت حكم الفرار بما هو في معنى المرض في توجه الهلاک الغالب وعدم ثبوته فيما كان الغالب منه السلامة، فمن الأول النازل في السبعة والراكب في السفينة وبقى على لوح، وكذا في المحيط وفى جوامع الفقه كتان في سفينة فاضطربت الأمواج، وكان الغالب منه العرق فهو كمرض الموت، وكذا الواقع في فم السبع والمسلول والمفلوج والمقعد ما دام يزاد ما به فهو من الثانى وإلا فهو من الأول، وصاحب جرح وقرحة أو وجع لم يصبه على الفراش بمنزلة الصحيح في الطلاق وغيره.

البناية: كتاب الطلاق، باب المريض، ج ۵ ص ۴۴۷، ط: دار الكتب العلمية
الهداية كتاب الطلاق، باب الرجعة ج ۲ ص ۴۰۵، ط: رحمانیه
He divorced her when his death was dominant and that is the cause of firar; so when the cause of firar is found, the ruling of firar is established and the woman inherits. And if he divorced her in a state where safety was dominant, then the woman does not inherit and firar is not established from this divorce, like the besieged person and the person present in the battle line - safety is dominant for them, because a fort is built for protection from enemy attacks, and similarly in battle formation the entire army is constantly engaged in protecting its companions. Since safety is dominant for both, firar is not established by divorcing at such a time.

The mentioned principle is: the ruling of firar is established in a condition analogous to illness where destruction is dominant, and it is not established where safety is dominant. From the first category: the one descending into water, the rider on a ship, and the one remaining on a plank, similarly in the ocean and in compilations of fiqh: like one in a ship whose waves are turbulent and sweating is dominant - it is like the illness of death; likewise the one falling into the mouth of a beast, the consumptive, the paralyzed, and the crippled as long as his condition worsens - that is from the second category, otherwise from the first. The owner of a wound or ulcer or pain that did not confine him to bed is like the healthy person in divorce and other matters.

Al-Binayah: Book of Divorce, Chapter of the Sick, Vol. 5 p. 447, Dar al-Kutub al-Ilmiyyah
Al-Hidayah: Book of Divorce, Chapter of Raj'ah, Vol. 2 p. 405, Rahmaniyyah
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling concerning the principle of firar (flight from inheritance) in divorce. It begins by explaining the cause of firar: when a husband divorces his wife while he is terminally ill, the dominant expectation is death, so the divorce is considered a means to deprive her of inheritance, and she still inherits. If he divorces in a situation where safety is dominant (like being besieged or in battle), the wife does not inherit because there is no intent to escape inheritance. The passage then lists examples from the primary texts: those facing imminent death (drowning, shipwreck, falling into a beast's mouth, severe chronic illness) are treated as 'death-dominant' and firar applies; those with minor ailments not confining to bed are like healthy persons. This principle is derived from the Hanafi understanding of intent and dominant probability.

Key Terms
فرار (firār): Flight from inheritance by divorcing during terminal illness to deprive wife of inheritance.
علت (ʿillah): The effective cause that determines the ruling of firar, i.e., the dominance of death at the time of divorce.
سلامتی غالب (salāmatī ghālib): The situation where survival is more likely, thus no firar.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the principle of firar, which of the following scenarios would result in the wife inheriting after divorce?
A) A husband divorces his wife while healthy.
B) A husband divorces his wife while lying in bed with a minor headache.
C) A husband divorces his wife while sinking in a ship with no hope of rescue.
D) A husband divorces his wife while standing in a well-defended fortress.

Key Takeaway
Firar protects a wife's inheritance when a husband divorces while the dominant expectation is death, but not when safety is dominant.

Definition and Condition of Raj'ah
Defines raj'ah and states its single essential condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of raj'ah and identify the essential condition for its validity.

Core Idea
Raj'ah is the continuation of marital ownership, valid only during the 'iddah.

Raj'ah, or revocation of divorce, is a unique legal mechanism in Islamic family law that restores the marriage during the waiting period without a new contract. To understand when it is valid, we must first grasp its nature as a continuation of ownership, not a fresh beginning. The source passage lays down this foundational definition and immediately ties it to the survival of the 'iddah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and definition
Read the first sentence: 'Raj'ah is the continuation of ownership.' Recognize that raj'ah is not a new marriage but a revival of the existing bond.
The term 'ownership' (milk) here means the right to marital intimacy.
2
Pinpoint the essential condition
The text states that for raj'ah to be valid, the 'iddah must still be in effect. If the 'iddah has ended, the marriage is fully terminated and cannot be revived by raj'ah.
This is a clear condition derived from the definition itself.
3
Apply the logical link
Since raj'ah is continuation, it can only apply to something that still exists. The 'iddah period is the time when the marital ownership remains in a suspended state, allowing revocation.
Consider: if ownership is completely gone, continuation is impossible.

Source Text

بدائع الصنائع: كتاب الطلاق، فصل فى شرائط جواز الرجعة، ج ۳ ص ۱۸۳، ط: دار الكتب العلمية الهداية كتاب الطلاق، باب الرجعة ج ۲ ص ۴۰۸، ط: رحمانیه
Chapter of Raj'ah
(85) Indeed, raj'ah is the continuation of ownership.
Translation: It is called keeping the marriage bond remaining.
Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that raj'ah means seeking the continuity and preservation of ownership. For the validity of raj'ah, the existence of the 'iddah is necessary, because raj'ah is called keeping the marriage bond remaining. For the validity of raj'ah, the existence of the 'iddah is necessary; after the 'iddah, raj'ah cannot occur, because the marriage ends completely. While for the validity of raj'ah, the existence of the 'iddah is a condition and necessary.

The mentioned principle is: the ruling of firar is established in a condition analogous to illness where destruction is dominant, and it is not established where safety is dominant. From the first category: the one descending into water, the rider on a ship, and the one remaining on a plank, similarly in the ocean and in compilations of fiqh: like one in a ship whose waves are turbulent and sweating is dominant - it is like the illness of death; likewise the one falling into the mouth of a beast, the consumptive, the paralyzed, and the crippled as long as his condition worsens - that is from the second category, otherwise from the first. The owner of a wound or ulcer or pain that did not confine him to bed is like the healthy person in divorce and other matters.
Al-Binayah: Book of Divorce, Chapter of the Sick, Vol. 5 p. 447, Dar al-Kutub al-Ilmiyyah
Al-Hidayah: Book of Divorce, Chapter of Raj'ah, Vol. 2 p. 405, Rahmaniyyah

[PAGE_097]

Author of Hidayah mentions the aforementioned principle and explains that for revocation, the existence of 'iddah is necessary, because when 'iddah remains, the ownership also remains in one sense, so the meaning of revocation is realized. After the 'iddah passes, revocation is not established, because after the 'iddah passes, the marriage ownership completely ends. When ownership completely ends, then the meaning of continuation and preservation is not established, so revocation is also not valid.
(86) Indeed, divorce in a confirmed ownership results in raj'ah.
Translation: After divorce in confirmed ownership, raj'ah occurs.
Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that if a person divorces in a confirmed ownership, i.e., stable ownership, he may revoke, and ownership becomes stable through consummation. Therefore, after consummation in the case of revocable divorce, revocation is valid. Author of Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that if a person divorced his wife while she was pregnant or had given birth, and the husband says he did not consummate, then revocation is established for him, because in this case the shari'ah rejects his statement of not having intercourse, due to the hadith 'the child belongs to the bed.' Similarly, her pregnancy indicates consummation. So when consummation is established, ownership becomes confirmed by consummation, and in that case revocation after divorce is established. Therefore, in this case also, revocation is established for the husband. Due to pregnancy, after divorce before delivery, the wife is in the husband's 'iddah, and during 'iddah, revocation is valid. This is: 'As for the conditions for the permissibility of raj'ah, among them is the existence of the 'iddah; raj'ah is not valid after the completion of the 'iddah, because raj'ah is the continuation of ownership, and ownership ends after the completion of the 'iddah, so continuation cannot be conceived because continuation is for that which exists, to protect it from removal.'
Bada'i' al-Sana'i': Book of Divorce, Section on Conditions of Permissibility of Raj'ah, Vol. 3 p. 183, Dar al-Kutub al-Ilmiyyah
Al-Hidayah: Book of Divorce, Chapter of Raj'ah, Vol. 2 p. 408, Rahmaniyyah
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This lesson defines raj'ah (revocation of divorce) as the continuation of marital ownership, and explains its essential condition: the 'iddah must still be in effect. It begins by quoting the principle from the matn: 'Raj'ah is the continuation of ownership.' The commentary expands that revocation means preserving the marriage bond; therefore, if the 'iddah expires, the marriage ends and revocation is impossible. The text then discusses a second principle: if divorce occurs in a confirmed ownership (i.e., after consummation), then revocation is possible. An important application: if a husband divorces his pregnant wife and denies consummation, the shari'ah still considers it a confirmed marriage because of the hadith 'al-waladu lil-firash' (the child belongs to the bed), so revocation is valid. The passage concludes with a quotation from Bada'i' al-Sana'i' reiterating that 'iddah is a condition for raj'ah. This is classic Hanafi fiqh: revocation is a legal resumption of marriage, not a new contract, and it is only valid during the 'iddah period.

Key Terms
رجعة (rajʿah): Revocation of a revocable divorce during the waiting period, restoring marital relations without a new contract.
عدت (ʿiddah): The waiting period after divorce during which the wife cannot remarry, and the husband may revoke.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the necessary condition for the validity of raj'ah?
A) The wife's consent.
B) The presence of two witnesses.
C) The existence of the 'iddah period.
D) A new marriage contract.

Key Takeaway
Raj'ah is a continuation of marriage, and it is only valid while the 'iddah exists; once 'iddah ends, the marriage cannot be revived by revocation.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate

Raj'ah in Confirmed Ownership: Effect of Consummation and Pregnancy
States that raj'ah follows divorce in confirmed ownership and uses pregnancy as proof of consummation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why raj'ah is valid after consummation (milk mu'akkad) and apply the hadith 'al-waladu lil-firash' to cases where the husband denies consummation but the wife is pregnant.

Core Idea
Raj'ah is valid only after confirmed ownership (consummation), and pregnancy proves consummation even if the husband denies it.

Building on the definition, the source text now introduces a second principle: raj'ah occurs after divorce in a confirmed marital ownership. This confirms that revocation is only possible after consummation, and that consummation can be proven by pregnancy or childbirth, overriding the husband's denial. The hadith 'the child belongs to the bed' is key to this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second principle
The text states: 'Divorce in confirmed ownership results in raj'ah.' Understand that confirmed ownership (milk mu'akkad) is established by valid consummation.
Before consummation, divorce is final (ba'in) and no raj'ah is possible.
2
Recognize the role of consummation
The commentary explains that after consummation, raj'ah is valid. The text then addresses an edge case: if the husband divorces a pregnant wife and denies consummation, the law still considers the marriage confirmed due to the pregnancy.
This applies whether the wife is currently pregnant or has already given birth.
3
Apply the hadith 'al-waladu lil-firash'
The hadith means the child is attributed to the husband as the legal father, so pregnancy is evidence of intimacy. Therefore, the husband's denial is not accepted, and the marriage is treated as confirmed.
This is a rule of evidence, not a substantive condition of raj'ah.
4
Derive the ruling
Since confirmed ownership is established, the husband can revoke during 'iddah (which continues until delivery if pregnant, or up to three menstrual cycles post-delivery if not).
The ruling protects the wife's rights and the legitimacy of the child.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ رجوع ملک کے دوام اور ابقاء طلب کرنے کو کہتے ہیں، صحت رجعت کے لئے عدت کی بقاء ضروری ہے، اس لئے کہ ملک نکاح کو باقی رکھنے کا نام رجوع ہے، صحت رجعت کے لئے عدت کی بقاء ضروری ہے ، عدت کے بعد رجوع نہیں ہوسکتا ، اس
لئے کہ نکاح بالکلیہ ختم ہو جاتا ہے، جبکہ صحت رجعت کے لئے بقائے عدت شرط اور ضروری ہے۔

والأصل المذكور هو ثبوت حكم الفرار بما هو في معنى المرض في توجه الهلاک الغالب وعدم ثبوته فيما كان الغالب منه السلامة، فمن الأول النازل في السبعة والراكب في السفينة وبقى على لوح، وكذا في المحيط وفى جوامع الفقه كتان في سفينة فاضطربت الأمواج، وكان الغالب منه العرق فهو كمرض الموت، وكذا الواقع في فم السبع والمسلول والمفلوج والمقعد ما دام يزاد ما به فهو من الثانى وإلا فهو من الأول، وصاحب جرح وقرحة أو وجع لم يصبه على الفراش بمنزلة الصحيح في الطلاق وغيره.
البناية: كتاب الطلاق، باب المريض، ج ۵ ص ۴۴۷، ط: دار الكتب العلمية
الهداية كتاب الطلاق، باب الرجعة ج ۲ ص ۴۰۵، ط: رحمانیه

اصول ہدایہ جلد ثانی صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ رجوع کرنے کے لئے عدت کا باقی ہونا ضروری ہے، اس لئے کہ جب عدت باقی ہو گی تو من وجہ ملک بھی باقی ہوگی ، تو رجوع کا معنی متحقق ہوگا ، اور عدت گزرنے کے بعد رجوع ثابت نہ ہوگا، اس لئے کہ عدت گزرنے کے بعد ملک نکاح بالکلیہ ختم ہو جاتا ہے، جب ملک بالکلیہ ختم ہوگئی تو پھر استدامت اور ابقاء کا معنی ثابت نہ
ہوگا ،لہذا رجوع بھی درست نہ ہوگا۔
(٨٦) إِنَّ الطَّلَاقَ فِى مِلْكِ مُتَأَكَّدٍ يُعَقِّبُ الرَّجْعَةَ .
ترجمہ: ملک مؤکد میں طلاق کے بعد رجعت واقع ہوتی ہے۔
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ ملک مؤکد یعنی پختہ ملک میں اگر کوئی آدمی طلاق دیتا ہے تو ہ رجوع کر سکتا ہے اور ملک دخول سے پختہ ہو جاتا ہے، لہذ ابعد الدخول طلاق رجعی کی صورت میں رجوع درست ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر کسی شخص نے اپنی بیوی کو طلاق دی اس حال میں کہ وہ حاملہ تھی یاوہ مولود لہا تھی، یعنی اس نے بچہ جنا تھا اور شوہر کہتا ہے کہ میں نے اس کے ساتھ دخول نہیں کیا، تو شوہر کے لئے رجوع ثابت ہوگا کیونکہ مذکورہ صورت میں شریعت شوہر کے اس قول کے میں نے جماع نہیں کیا اس کی مکذب ہے ، اس لئے کہ حدیث میں ہے الْوَلَدُ لِلْفِرَاشِ “ اور اسی طرح عورت کا حاملہ ہونا یہ بھی دخول پر دال ہے، لہذا مذکورہ صورت میں جب دخول ثابت ہوا تو دخول کی وجہ سے ملک مؤکد ہو جاتی ہے اور اس میں طلاق کے بعد رجوع ثابت ہوتا ہے، لہذا مذکورہ صورت میں بھی شوہر کے لئے رجوع ثابت ہو گا۔ حاملہ ہونے کی وجہ سے طلاق کے بعد وضع حمل سے پہلے پہلے بیوی شوہر کی عدت میں ہے اور دورانِ عدت رجعت کرنا صحیح ہے، یہ وَأَمَّا شَرَائِطُ جَوَازِ الرَّجُعَةِ فَمِنْهَا قِيَامُ الْعِدَّةِ، فَلا تَصِحُ الرَّجْعَةُ بَعْدَ انْقِضَاءِ الْعِدَّةِ، لِأَنَّ الرَّجُعَةَ اسْتِدَامَةُ الْمِلْكِ، وَالْمِلْكُ يَزُولُ بَعْدَ انْقِضَاءِ الْعِدَّةِ، فَلَا تُتَصَوَّرُ الاسْتِدَامَةُ إِذْ الاسْتِدَامَةُ لِلْقَائِمِ لِصِيَانَتِهِ عَنْ الزَّوَالِ.
بدائع الصنائع: كتاب الطلاق، فصل فى شرائط جواز الرجعة، ج ۳ ص ۱۸۳، ط: دار الكتب العلمية الهداية كتاب الطلاق، باب الرجعة ج ۲ ص ۴۰۸، ط: رحمانیه
Chapter of Raj'ah
(85) Indeed, raj'ah is the continuation of ownership.
Translation: It is called keeping the marriage bond remaining.
Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that raj'ah means seeking the continuity and preservation of ownership. For the validity of raj'ah, the existence of the 'iddah is necessary, because raj'ah is called keeping the marriage bond remaining. For the validity of raj'ah, the existence of the 'iddah is necessary; after the 'iddah, raj'ah cannot occur, because the marriage ends completely. While for the validity of raj'ah, the existence of the 'iddah is a condition and necessary.

The mentioned principle is: the ruling of firar is established in a condition analogous to illness where destruction is dominant, and it is not established where safety is dominant. From the first category: the one descending into water, the rider on a ship, and the one remaining on a plank, similarly in the ocean and in compilations of fiqh: like one in a ship whose waves are turbulent and sweating is dominant - it is like the illness of death; likewise the one falling into the mouth of a beast, the consumptive, the paralyzed, and the crippled as long as his condition worsens - that is from the second category, otherwise from the first. The owner of a wound or ulcer or pain that did not confine him to bed is like the healthy person in divorce and other matters.
Al-Binayah: Book of Divorce, Chapter of the Sick, Vol. 5 p. 447, Dar al-Kutub al-Ilmiyyah
Al-Hidayah: Book of Divorce, Chapter of Raj'ah, Vol. 2 p. 405, Rahmaniyyah

[PAGE_097]

Author of Hidayah mentions the aforementioned principle and explains that for revocation, the existence of 'iddah is necessary, because when 'iddah remains, the ownership also remains in one sense, so the meaning of revocation is realized. After the 'iddah passes, revocation is not established, because after the 'iddah passes, the marriage ownership completely ends. When ownership completely ends, then the meaning of continuation and preservation is not established, so revocation is also not valid.
(86) Indeed, divorce in a confirmed ownership results in raj'ah.
Translation: After divorce in confirmed ownership, raj'ah occurs.
Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that if a person divorces in a confirmed ownership, i.e., stable ownership, he may revoke, and ownership becomes stable through consummation. Therefore, after consummation in the case of revocable divorce, revocation is valid. Author of Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that if a person divorced his wife while she was pregnant or had given birth, and the husband says he did not consummate, then revocation is established for him, because in this case the shari'ah rejects his statement of not having intercourse, due to the hadith 'the child belongs to the bed.' Similarly, her pregnancy indicates consummation. So when consummation is established, ownership becomes confirmed by consummation, and in that case revocation after divorce is established. Therefore, in this case also, revocation is established for the husband. Due to pregnancy, after divorce before delivery, the wife is in the husband's 'iddah, and during 'iddah, revocation is valid. This is: 'As for the conditions for the permissibility of raj'ah, among them is the existence of the 'iddah; raj'ah is not valid after the completion of the 'iddah, because raj'ah is the continuation of ownership, and ownership ends after the completion of the 'iddah, so continuation cannot be conceived because continuation is for that which exists, to protect it from removal.'
Bada'i' al-Sana'i': Book of Divorce, Section on Conditions of Permissibility of Raj'ah, Vol. 3 p. 183, Dar al-Kutub al-Ilmiyyah
Al-Hidayah: Book of Divorce, Chapter of Raj'ah, Vol. 2 p. 408, Rahmaniyyah
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This lesson defines raj'ah (revocation of divorce) as the continuation of marital ownership, and explains its essential condition: the 'iddah must still be in effect. It begins by quoting the principle from the matn: 'Raj'ah is the continuation of ownership.' The commentary expands that revocation means preserving the marriage bond; therefore, if the 'iddah expires, the marriage ends and revocation is impossible. The text then discusses a second principle: if divorce occurs in a confirmed ownership (i.e., after consummation), then revocation is possible. An important application: if a husband divorces his pregnant wife and denies consummation, the shari'ah still considers it a confirmed marriage because of the hadith 'al-waladu lil-firash' (the child belongs to the bed), so revocation is valid. The passage concludes with a quotation from Bada'i' al-Sana'i' reiterating that 'iddah is a condition for raj'ah. This is classic Hanafi fiqh: revocation is a legal resumption of marriage, not a new contract, and it is only valid during the 'iddah period.

Key Terms
ملک مؤکد (milk mu'akkad): Confirmed marital ownership, established by valid consummation, after which only irrevocable divorce or death can sever the marriage.
الْوَلَدُ لِلْفِرَاشِ (al-waladu lil-firash): Prophetic hadith meaning 'the child belongs to the bed' (i.e., the husband is the legal father if born during marriage).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the proof that a marriage is in 'confirmed ownership' when the husband denies consummation but the wife is pregnant?
A) The wife's testimony alone.
B) The presence of witnesses to the wedding.
C) The hadith 'al-waladu lil-firash' and the fact of pregnancy.
D) A medical certificate.

Key Takeaway
Raj'ah is valid only after consummation, and consummation can be proven by pregnancy via the hadith 'al-waladu lil-firash,' even if the husband denies it.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
Divorce in Confirmed Ownership Permits Revocation (Raj'a)
States the principle that divorce after consummation allows revocation, with an applied case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the principle of milk mu'akkad and apply it to determine the validity of raj'a in cases of disputed consummation.

Core Idea
After consummation, divorce is revocable during iddah – the child or pregnancy confirms the marriage.

This lesson explores the Hanafi principle that divorce in a confirmed marriage (milk mu'akkad) allows the husband to revoke (raj'a) during iddah. The author of al-Hidayah illustrates this with a case where a husband denies consummating, but the wife is pregnant or has a child. Islamic law overrides his claim, establishing consummation and thus confirming the marriage, making raj'a valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal principle
The principle is that divorce in milk mu'akkad (confirmed ownership after consummation) is followed by the right of raj'a. Understand that milk mu'akkad is established by actual consummation (dukhul).
2
Recognize the condition of iddah
Raj'a is only valid during the iddah period. Once iddah ends, the marriage ownership ceases completely, and raj'a is not possible.
The text emphasizes that raj'a is a continuation of ownership, which requires that ownership still exists.
3
Analyze the contested case
Apply the principle to a scenario where the husband denies consummation, but the wife is pregnant or has given birth. The Sharia rejects the husband's denial based on the hadith 'al-walad lil-firash' and the evidentiary value of pregnancy.
This shows how the principle overrides a contradictory claim.
4
Derive the ruling
Since consummation is established legally, the marriage becomes milk mu'akkad. Therefore, a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) during iddah permits raj'a, even if the husband physically denies consummation.
The ruling is consistent across Hanafi sources and is considered mufta bihi.

Source Text

بدائع الصنائع: كتاب الطلاق، فصل فى شرائط جواز الرجعة، ج ۳ ص ۱۸۳، ط: دار الكتب العلمية الهداية كتاب الطلاق، باب الرجعة ج ۲ ص ۴۰۸، ط: رحمانیه
اصول ہدایہ جلد ثانی اصول کہ ملک مؤکد میں طلاق رجعت کو ثابت کرتی ہے، اس کی وضاحت اس بات سے ہوتی ہے کہ شوہر کی مذکورہ ولی سے احصان ثابت ہو جائے گا اور سزا اور عقوبت وغیرہ کے وجوب میں احصان مؤثر ہے، لہذا جب اس ولی سے احصان ثابت ہورہا ہے تو رجعت بدرجہ اولی ثابت ہوگی۔
Principles of Hidayah Volume Two: The author of Hidayah mentions the mentioned principle and says that for revocation, the remaining of the iddah is necessary, because when the iddah remains, then in some respects ownership also remains, so the meaning of revocation is realized. After the iddah passes, revocation is not established because after the iddah, the ownership of marriage is completely terminated. When ownership is completely terminated, then the meaning of continuation and preservation is not established, so revocation is also not valid. (86) Indeed, divorce in a confirmed ownership is followed by revocation. Translation: After divorce in confirmed ownership, revocation occurs. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that if a person gives divorce in confirmed ownership, i.e., firm ownership, then he can revoke, and ownership becomes firm through consummation. Therefore, after consummation, in the case of revocable divorce, revocation is valid. The author of Hidayah mentions the mentioned principle to explain that if a person divorces his wife while she is pregnant or has given birth, and the husband says that he did not consummate with her, then the husband has the right of revocation because in this situation, the Shariah denies his statement of not having intercourse, because in the hadith it is said: 'The child belongs to the bed.' Similarly, the woman being pregnant also indicates consummation. Therefore, when consummation is established in the mentioned case, due to consummation the ownership becomes confirmed, and after divorce in it, revocation is established. So in the mentioned case too, the husband's revocation is established. Due to pregnancy, after divorce and before delivery, the wife is in the husband's iddah, and during the iddah, revocation is valid. And the conditions for the permissibility of revocation include the existence of the iddah. Therefore, revocation is not valid after the completion of the iddah, because revocation is the continuation of ownership, and ownership ceases after the completion of the iddah. So continuation is not conceivable, since continuation is for what exists, to protect it from removal. (Bada'i' al-Sana'i': Book of Divorce, Chapter on Conditions of Revocation, Vol. 3, p. 183, Dar al-Kutub al-Ilmiyyah; Al-Hidayah: Book of Divorce, Chapter of Revocation, Vol. 2, p. 408, Rahmaniyyah). Principles of Hidayah Volume Two: The principle that divorce in confirmed ownership establishes revocation is clarified by the fact that from the aforementioned consummation, the husband's ihsan (being in a valid marriage) is established, and ihsan is effective in the obligation of punishment and penalty. So when ihsan is established from this consummation, then revocation is established a fortiori.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It states the principle that divorce in confirmed ownership (milk mu'akkad, i.e., after consummation) allows the husband to revoke the divorce (raj'a). The author explains that the validity of raj'a depends on the iddah remaining; once the iddah ends, the marriage ownership completely terminates, and raj'a is impossible. He then applies this principle to a specific case: if a man divorces his pregnant wife or a wife who has given birth, and he claims he never consummated, his claim is rejected by Islamic law because the child is attributed to the marriage bed (al-walad lil-firash) and pregnancy itself indicates consummation. Thus, the marriage is considered confirmed, and raj'a is valid. The passage also quotes from Bada'i' al-Sana'i' and al-Hidayah to support the condition that iddah must be in effect. Finally, it mentions that the ruling has implications for ihsan (moral probity) in criminal law, but the primary point is the validity of raj'a.

Key Terms
رجعة (raj'a): The husband's return to his wife during iddah after a revocable divorce, reestablishing the marriage without a new contract.
ملک مؤکد (milk mu'akkad): The marital authority that becomes firm and confirmed after consummation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does a husband have the right to revoke a divorce according to the principle of milk mu'akkad?
A) Only if he admits consummation
B) Only during iddah after any divorce
C) During iddah after consummation
D) Only if wife agrees

Key Takeaway
The principle of milk mu'akkad ensures that divorce after consummation is revocable, even if the husband denies consummation when pregnancy or birth confirms the marriage.

Definition of Muli: The One Who Swears an Oath of Ila
Defines the legal term muli and applies it to an example with an exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of muli and apply it to an oath with an exception to determine whether ila is formed.

Core Idea
A husband is a muli only if he cannot approach his wife for four months without incurring kaffarah; an exception that avoids kaffarah negates muli status.

This lesson defines the key term muli, central to the chapter of Ila. The definition hinges on the impossibility of approaching without penalty. An important nuance emerges when an exception is made: if the husband can approach within four months without penalty due to an exception, he is not considered a muli. We will examine the definition and an illustrative example from the text.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
Read the Arabic definition: 'إن المولى من لا يمكنه القربان أربعة أشهر إلا بشيء يلزمه'. Translate it: 'The muli is one for whom approaching is not possible for four months except with something that becomes obligatory upon him.' Note the condition: he cannot approach without incurring kaffarah.
2
Identify the key condition
The phrase 'إلا بشيء يلزمه' (except with something that becomes obligatory) means that if he approaches, he must pay kaffarah. Thus, a muli is someone who is unable to approach without penalty.
3
Apply to the example
Consider the oath: 'By Allah, I will not approach you for a year except one day.' The exception (one day) is unspecified. The husband can choose to approach on any day within the first four months. Since he can approach that day without penalty, he is not a muli.
4
Contrast with a true muli
If the oath were 'for four months' without exception, he would be a muli because he cannot approach at all without kaffarah within that period.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الإيلاء
(۸۷) إِنَّ الْمُوْلِى مَنْ لَا يُمْكِنُهُ الْقِرُبَانُ أَرْبَعَةَ أَشْهُرٍ إِلَّا بِشَيْءٍ يَلْزَمُهُ. ترجمہ: ایلاء کرنے والا وہ شخص کہلاتا ہے جسے کسی چیز کے لزوم کے بغیر چار ماہ تک بیوی سے قربت کرنا ممکن نہ ہو، ہومگر ایسی شے کے ساتھ جو اس پر لازم ہو۔
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ مولی یعنی ایلاء کرنے والا وہ شخص کہلائے گا جس کو اپنی بیوی کے پاس چار ماہ تک بغیر لزوم کفارہ کے جانا ممکن نہ ہو، یعنی اگر بیوی کے پاس جائے تو کفارہ لازم ہوگا۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر کوئی اپنی بیوی سے یہ کہتا ہے وَاللَّهِ لَا أَقْرَبُكِ سَنَةٌ إِلَّا يَوْمًا “ کہ اللہ کی قسم میں آپ کے قریب نہیں آؤں گا ایک سال تک مگر ایک دن، تو یہ شخص مولی نہیں کہلائے گا، کیونکہ مولی وہ ہوتا ہے جس کے لئے قربت کرنا چار مہینوں تک بغیر لزوم کفارہ کے ممکن نہ ہو۔ مذکورہ صورت میں اس شخص کے لئے چار ماہ تک قربت بغیر لزوم کفارہ کے ممکن ہے کیونکہ اس نے ایک دن کا استثناء کیا ہے، وہ دن غیر متعین ہے اور پورے سال کے ہر ہر دن میں شوہر کو وطی کرنے کا اختیار ہے اور مولی اس شخص کو کہا جاتا ہے جولز وم کفارہ کے بغیر چار ماہ تک بیوی سے وطی نہ کر سکے، حالانکہ صورتِ مسئلہ میں یوم کے استثناء کی وجہ سے ہر
(قَوْلُهُ وَلَوْ طَلَّقَ ذَا حَمْلٍ أَوْ وَلَدٍ، وَقَالَ لَمْ أَطَأْهَا رَاجَعَ) يَعْنِي لَوْ طَلَّقَ امْرَأَتَهُ، وَهِيَ حَامِلٌ أَوْ بَعْدَ مَا وَلَدَتْ فِي عِصْمَتِهِ، وَقَالَ لَمْ أُجَامِعْهَا فَلَهُ الرَّجْعَةُ لأَنَّهَا مَبْنِيَّةٌ عَلَى الدُّخُولِ، وَقَدْ ثَبَتَ حُكْمًا لِقُبُوتِ النَّسَب لأنَّهُ يَثْبُتُ بِظُهور الْحَمْل بأنْ وَلَدَتْ لِأقَلَّ مِنْ سِتَّةِ أَشْهُرٍ فَلَمْ يُلْتَفَتْ إِلَى قَوْلِهِ لَمُ أَطَاهَا لأَنَّهُ صَارَ مُكَذَّبًا شَرُعًا، وَمَنْ صَارَ مُكَذَّبًا شَرُعًا بَطَلَ زَعْمُهُ مَا لَمْ يَتَعَلَّقُ بِإِقْرَارِهِ حَقُّ الْغَيْرِ.
البحر الرائق: كتاب الطلاق، باب الرجعة، ج ۴ ص ۵۸، ط: دار الكتاب الإسلامي
الهداية: كتاب الطلاق، باب الإيلاء، ج ۲ ص ۳۹۲، ط: رحمانیه
Chapter of Ila. (87) Indeed, the muli is the one for whom approaching (his wife) for four months is not possible except with something that becomes obligatory upon him. Translation: The one who performs ila is called a person for whom it is not possible to approach his wife for four months without something becoming obligatory, except with something that becomes obligatory upon him. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that a muli, i.e., one who performs ila, is called a person for whom it is not possible to go to his wife for four months without the obligation of kaffarah. That is, if he goes to his wife, kaffarah becomes obligatory. The author of Hidayah, by mentioning the mentioned principle, explains that if someone says to his wife: 'By Allah, I will not approach you for a year except one day,' then this person is not called a muli, because the muli is the one for whom approaching is not possible for four months without the obligation of kaffarah. In the mentioned case, for this person, approaching for four months is possible without the obligation of kaffarah because he made an exception of one day. That day is unspecified, and the husband has the option to have intercourse on any day of the whole year. And the muli is called a person who cannot have intercourse with his wife for four months without kaffarah, whereas in the case in question, due to the exception of a day, every... (His statement: 'And if he divorces a pregnant woman or one with a child, and said I did not have intercourse with her, he can revoke') means if he divorces his wife while she is pregnant or after she gave birth while in his protection, and he said I did not have intercourse with her, then he has the right of revocation because it is based on consummation, and it is established legally due to the confirmation of lineage because it is established by the appearance of pregnancy if she gave birth in less than six months. So his statement 'I did not have intercourse with her' is not heeded because he has become falsified legally, and whoever becomes falsified legally, his claim is void as long as no right of another is attached to his acknowledgment. (Al-Bahr al-Raiq: Book of Divorce, Chapter of Revocation, Vol. 4, p. 58, Dar al-Kitab al-Islami; Al-Hidayah: Book of Divorce, Chapter of Ila, Vol. 2, p. 392, Rahmaniyyah)
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling. It defines the term 'muli' (one who swears an oath of ila, i.e., to abstain from intercourse with his wife for four months or more). The definition states that a muli is someone who cannot approach his wife for four months without incurring a penalty (kaffarah) if he does. The author then applies this definition to an example: if a man swears by Allah not to approach his wife for a year except one day, he is not a muli. This is because he can choose to approach her on the excepted day within the first four months without incurring any penalty. The excepted day is unspecified, giving him full flexibility. The passage also includes an Arabic quote from al-Bahr al-Raiq that seems to repeat the ruling from the previous lesson about a pregnant woman and revocation, which may be a marginal note. However, the main lesson is the definition of muli.

Key Terms
مُولِي (mūlī): A husband who takes an oath to abstain from sexual relations with his wife for a period of four months or more, such that he cannot approach without incurring kaffarah.
إيلاء (īlā'): An oath sworn by a husband to abstain from sexual relations with his wife for four months or more, with legal consequences if the oath is kept or broken.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man swears not to approach his wife for a year except one day, is he considered a muli?
A) Yes, because the oath is for a year
B) No, because he can choose the one day within four months
C) Yes, because he still avoids her for most of the year
D) No, because ila requires a specific wording

Key Takeaway
A husband is a muli only if he cannot approach his wife within four months without incurring kaffarah; an exception that allows approach without penalty removes him from the definition.

Raj'ah Despite Denial of Intercourse: The Principle of Mukadhdhab Shar'an
Ruling with proof on raj'ah and denial of intercourse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a husband may return to his pregnant wife despite his denial of intercourse, and explain the concept of mukadhdhab shar'an as it overrides personal testimony.

Core Idea
Pregnancy legally proves consummation, overriding the husband's denial for the right of return.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a critical conflict: a husband divorces his pregnant wife but swears he never had intercourse, hoping to block the right of return. The Hanafi school resolves this using a powerful legal presumption: pregnancy or childbirth establishes consummation by law, because lineage is confirmed through birth. The husband's denial is thus legally contradicted (mukadhdhab shar'an), and he may still take his wife back. This lesson unpacks the proof structure and the underlying maxim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and the Husband's Claim
The husband divorces his wife while she is pregnant or after childbirth, then denies consummation. He wants to deny his right of return (raj'ah).
Raj'ah is permitted only if the divorce is revocable (talaq raj'i) and requires prior consummation (dukhul).
2
Understand the Basis of Raj'ah
Raj'ah depends on prior consummation. The husband must have had intercourse with his wife during the marriage for the right of return to exist.
3
Recognize the Legal Proof of Consummation
Pregnancy or childbirth legally proves consummation, as lineage is established by birth occurring within six months of marriage. This proof overrides the husband's subjective denial.
If birth occurs after six months, it may not prove consummation under the marriage, and the denial might be considered.
4
Apply the Principle of Mukadhdhab Shar'an
The husband becomes 'legally contradicted' because objective evidence (pregnancy) contradicts his claim. The law disregards his statement unless it affects third-party rights.
Exception: If the denial involves another's right (e.g., paternity contest), it may be heard.
5
Derive the Ruling and Its Application
The husband may return (raj'ah) to his wife. The ruling is based on the maxim that evidence overrides personal testimony.
This ruling is mukhtalaf fih (disputed); other schools may require admission of intercourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْ طَلَّقَ ذَا حَمْلٍ أَوْ وَلَدٍ، وَقَالَ لَمْ أَطَأْهَا رَاجَعَ. يَعْنِي لَوْ طَلَّقَ امْرَأَتَهُ، وَهِيَ حَامِلٌ أَوْ بَعْدَ مَا وَلَدَتْ فِي عِصْمَتِهِ، وَقَالَ لَمْ أُجَامِعُهَا فَلَهُ الرَّجْعَةُ لأَنَّهَا مَبْنِيَّةٌ عَلَى الدُّخُولِ، وَقَدْ ثَبَتَ حُكْمًا لِثُبُوتِ النَّسَبِ لأَنَّهُ يَثْبُتُ بِظُهُورِ الْحَمْلِ، بِأَنْ وَلَدَتْ لِأَقَلَّ مِنْ سِتَّةِ أَشْهُرٍ، فَلَمْ يُلْتَفَتْ إِلَى قَوْلِهِ لَمْ أَطَأْهَا لأَنَّهُ صَارَ مُكَذَّبًا شَرْعًا، وَمَنْ صَارَ مُكَذَّبًا شَرْعًا بَطَلَ زَعْمُهُ مَا لَمْ يَتَعَلَّقْ بِإِقْرَارِهِ حَقُّ الْغَيْرِ.
And if he divorces her while she is pregnant or after she has given birth, and he says, 'I did not have intercourse with her,' he may take her back. That is, if he divorces his wife while she is pregnant or after she has given birth while still under his authority, and he says, 'I did not have intercourse with her,' he has the right of return because [return] is based on [prior] consummation. And it has been established by law (hukman) due to the confirmation of lineage, for lineage is established by the appearance of pregnancy, if she gives birth in less than six months. So his statement 'I did not have intercourse' is not considered, because he has become legally contradicted (mukadhdhab shar'an). And whoever becomes legally contradicted, his claim becomes void unless the right of others is attached to his admission.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It establishes that a husband who divorces his wife while she is pregnant or after childbirth can revoke the divorce (raj'a), even if he denies having consummated the marriage. The reason given is that the right of return (raj'a) is based on prior consummation (dukhul). In this case, consummation is legally proven by the existence of pregnancy or childbirth, as lineage is confirmed by birth occurring less than six months after marriage (indicating intercourse before divorce). Therefore, the husband's denial is legally contradicted (mukadhdhab shar'an) because the evidence of pregnancy establishes intercourse. The principle is that when the law contradicts a person's claim, that claim is disregarded, unless it would adversely affect the rights of a third party (e.g., if paternity is contested by someone else). The passage relies on the legal maxim that external evidence can override personal testimony.

Key Terms
رَجْعَة (raj'ah): The husband's right to take back his wife during her 'iddah without a new marriage contract, applicable in revocable divorces (talaq raj'i).
مُكَذَّب شَرْعًا (mukadhdhab shar'an): A person whose statement is legally contradicted by clear evidence, such that the law disregards his claim.
الدُّخُول (al-dukhūl): Consummation of marriage (intercourse).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the husband have the right of return (raj'ah) despite denying intercourse with his pregnant wife?
A) Because his denial is a form of admission.
B) Because pregnancy legally establishes previous consummation.
C) Because the wife's consent is required.
D) Because the divorce was invalid.

Key Takeaway
The husband's denial of intercourse is legally ineffective when pregnancy or childbirth establishes consummation; he may return to his wife despite the denial.

Definition of Muli (One Who Makes Ila')
Defines muli and applies the rule to an exception case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of muli and apply it to determine whether a husband is considered a muli when his oath includes a valid indefinite exception.

Core Idea
A husband is a muli only if he cannot approach his wife for four months without incurring kaffarah.

This lesson defines the key term 'muli' in Hanafi law of ila'. The definition hinges on impossibility of approach without expiation, not merely the duration of the oath. We then examine a case where an exception makes approach possible without liability, showing that the husband is not a muli.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of muli
Read the first sentence: 'Indeed, the one who makes ila' is he for whom it is not possible to approach for four months except with something that binds him.' Note that the key is impossibility without liability.
The 'something that binds him' is kaffarah (expiation) for breaking the oath.
2
Understand the condition of impossibility without liability
The definition requires that the husband cannot approach within four months without incurring kaffarah. If he can approach without kaffarah, he is not a muli.
This distinguishes ila' from a mere oath of abstinence.
3
Analyse the exception case
The passage presents a case: the husband says 'By Allah, I will not approach you for a year except a day.' The excepted day is indefinite (nakirah), so he can choose any day to approach without breaking the oath.
Because the exception is valid, approaching on that day does not break the oath, hence no kaffarah.
4
Apply the definition to conclude
Since the husband can approach within four months (by choosing an early day) without incurring kaffarah, he does not meet the definition of muli. Therefore, he is not a muli.
The ruling: the husband is not a muli.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ الْمُوْلِيَ مَنْ لَا يُمْكِنُهُ الْقُرْبَانُ أَرْبَعَةَ أَشْهُرٍ إِلَّا بِشَيْءٍ يَلْزَمُهُ. وَأَمَّا الثَّانِيَةُ وَهُوَ مَا إِذَا قَالَ وَاللَّهِ لَا أَقْرَبُكَ سَنَةً إِلَّا يَوْمًا فَإِنَّ الْمُولِيَ مَنْ لَا يُمْكِنُهُ الْقُرْبَانَ فِي أَرْبَعَةِ أَشْهُرٍ إِلَّا بِشَيْءٍ يَلْزَمُهُ، وَهُنَا يُمْكِنُهُ الْقُرْبَانُ مِنْ غَيْرِ شَيْءٍ يَلْزَمُهُ، لِأَنَّ الْمُسْتَثْنَى يَوْمٌ مُنَكَّرٌ فَلَهُ أَنْ يَجْعَلَهُ أَيَّ يَوْمٍ شَاءَ فَلَا يَمُرُّ عَلَيْهِ يَوْمٌ مِنْ أَيَّامِ السَّنَةِ إِلَّا وَيُمْكِنُهُ أَنْ يَجْعَلَهُ هُوَ الْمُسْتَثْنَى.
Indeed, the one who makes ila' is he for whom it is not possible to approach [his wife] for four months except with something that binds him. As for the second [case], which is when he says: 'By Allah, I will not approach you for a year except a day,' then indeed the one who makes ila' is he for whom it is not possible to approach in four months except with something that binds him. Here, it is possible for him to approach without anything binding him, because the excepted day is indefinite, so he can make it any day he wishes. Thus no day of the year passes except that it is possible for him to make it the excepted day.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling statement. It defines the term 'muli' (one who makes ila') as a husband who cannot approach his wife for four months without incurring an expiatory obligation. The 'thing that binds him' (shay' yalzamuhu) refers to kaffarah (expiation) that becomes wajib if he breaks his oath by approaching. The passage then applies this definition to a specific case: a husband swears, 'By Allah, I will not approach you for a year except one day.' Since the excepted day is indefinite (nakirah), the husband can choose any day to approach without breaking his oath and without incurring kaffarah because the exception is valid. Therefore, he is not a muli, as he can approach within four months without any obligation. This illustrates that the definition hinges on the impossibility of approach without liability, not on the mere length of time.

Key Terms
الْمُوْلِي (al-mūlī): The husband who makes ila' (vow of abstinence); legally, one who cannot approach his wife for four months without incurring an obligation (kaffarah).
الإيلاء (al-īlā'): A vow (usually by Allah's name) to abstain from marital intercourse for a period of four months or more.
الْمُسْتَثْنَى (al-mustathnā): The excepted item in an exception construction; here, the indefinite day that allows the husband to approach without breaking the oath.
كفارة (kaffārah): Expiation for breaking a valid oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a husband says to his wife, 'By Allah, I will not approach you for a year except one day,' is he considered a muli (one who makes ila')?
A) Yes, because he swore for more than four months.
B) No, because he can approach on any day without incurring kaffarah.
C) Yes, because the exception is invalid.
D) No, because the oath is void.

Key Takeaway
A husband is not a muli if he can approach his wife within four months without incurring kaffarah, and an indefinite exception in his oath allows him to do so.

Absolute Statement Admits Restriction: Zihar by Intention
Intra-Hanafi dispute on zihar through comparison to non-mahram objects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a statement comparing a wife to a non-human object can be zihar with intention, and apply the principle 'al-mutlaq yahtamil al-muqayyad'.

Core Idea
An absolute prohibition can be restricted by intention to form zihar.

This lesson examines a classical Hanafi dispute: does a husband's statement 'You are like a donkey' constitute zihar if he intends it? The two senior imams, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, argue yes, based on the principle that an absolute term can be qualified by intention. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees, insisting that the pillar of comparison to a prohibited woman is missing. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply juristic reasoning and the concept of mutlaq and muqayyad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Statement in Question
Note the husband's words: 'You are to me like a donkey, or a pig, or anything forbidden in itself.' This is an absolute statement of prohibition (hurma).
The statement does not explicitly mention zihar.
2
State the Position of the Shaykhayn
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that if the husband intends zihar, the statement becomes zihar. Their evidence: the absolute prohibition can be restricted by intention to the specific type of prohibition in zihar.
They rely on the usul principle 'al-mutlaq yahtamil al-muqayyad'.
3
State the Position of Imam Muhammad
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani argues it is not zihar regardless of intention, because zihar requires comparison to a prohibited woman (e.g., mother, sister), which is a pillar (rukn) missing here.
He emphasizes the necessity of the pillar over intention.
4
Analyse the Application of al-Mutlaq yahtamil al-Muqayyad
The Shaykhayn apply the principle: the absolute word 'haram' includes all types of prohibition, and intention specifies it to zihar. Muhammad objects that the pillar requirement cannot be overridden by intention.
This highlights a tension between textual form and substantive pillars.
5
Determine the Practical Consequence and Mufta Bihi
If the Shaykhayn's view is followed, the wife becomes prohibited until expiation. If Muhammad's view is followed, no zihar occurs. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of the Shaykhayn.
The passage does not explicitly state tarjih, but the citation of the principle supports the Shaykhayn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَقَوْلُهُ أَنْتِ عَلَيَّ كَالْحِمَارِ أَوِ الْخِنْزِيرِ أَوْ مَا كَانَ مُحَرَّمَ الْعَيْنِ فَهُوَ كَقَوْلِهِ أَنْتِ عَلَيَّ حَرَامٌ كَمَا فِي الْبَزَّازِيَّةِ. قَوْلُهُ وَظِهَارٌ إِنْ نَوَاهُ) أَىِ الطَّهَارَ، وَهَذَا عِنْدَهُمَا، وَقَالَ مُحَمَّدٌ لَيْسَ بِظِهَارٍ لِانْعِدَامِ التَّشْبِيهِ بِالْمُحَرَّمَةِ، وَهُوَ رُكْنُ فِيهِ، وَلَهُمَا أَنَّهُ أَطْلَقَ الْحُرْمَةَ، وَفِى الظِّهَارِ نَوْعُ حُرُمَةٍ، وَالْمُطْلَقُ يَحْتَمِلُ الْمُقَيَّدَ.
And his statement: 'You are to me like a donkey, or a pig, or anything that is forbidden in itself (muharram al-'ayn),' then it is like his statement: 'You are unlawful to me (haram),' as in al-Bazzaziyyah. His statement: 'And it is zihar if he intends it' — i.e., zihar — and this is according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). And Muhammad said: It is not zihar due to the absence of comparison to a forbidden woman, and that is a pillar in it. And for the two (their proof is) that he uttered absolute prohibition (hurma), and in zihar there is a type of prohibition, and the absolute admits the restricted.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether a husband's statement comparing his wife to a donkey, pig, or other inherently forbidden thing constitutes zihar if he intends it. The two (al-Shaykhayn: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) hold that such a statement is like saying 'You are haram to me,' and if the husband intends zihar, it becomes zihar. Their reasoning (dalil) is based on the principle 'al-mutlaq yahtamil al-muqayyad' (the absolute admits the restricted). Here, the husband has made an absolute statement of prohibition (hurma), and zihar is a specific type of prohibition. Therefore, his intention (niyyah) can restrict the absolute to the specific meaning of zihar. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani opposes, arguing that zihar requires a comparison to a prohibited woman (e.g., 'like my mother's back'), which is a pillar (rukn). Comparing to a donkey or pig does not fulfill this pillar, so intention cannot create zihar. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of the Shaykhayn, as indicated by the principle cited. The passage illustrates the principle that absolute statements can be qualified by the speaker's intention.

Key Terms
الظِّهَار (al-zihār): A pre-Islamic form of divorce where a husband says to his wife, 'You are to me like my mother's back,' which makes her prohibited until expiation.
الْمُطْلَق (al-muṭlaq): A word without any qualification or restriction; here, the word 'haram' used absolutely.
الْمُقَيَّد (al-muqayyad): A word with a qualification; here, the specific type of prohibition (zihar) intended.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), why does the statement 'You are to me like a donkey' become zihar if the husband intends it?
A) Because a donkey is a forbidden animal, so it implies prohibition.
B) Because the husband uttered an absolute prohibition, which can be restricted to the type of prohibition in zihar by intention.
C) Because the principle of 'al-muqayyad yahtamil al-mutlaq' applies.
D) Because Imam Muhammad agreed with them.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position holds that an absolute prohibition can be narrowed by intention to create zihar, illustrating the principle al-mutlaq yahtamil al-muqayyad.

The Absolute Can Be Qualified by Intention
Explains the principle of mutlaq and muqayyad with application to Ila' and Zihar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle 'al-mutlaq yahtamil al-muqayyad' to determine whether a husband's statement 'You are forbidden to me' constitutes zihar based on his intention.

Core Idea
The absolute can be qualified by intention—what a husband intends specifies the ruling.

In Hanafi fiqh, the principle 'al-mutlaq yahtamil al-muqayyad' (the absolute implies the qualified) is a powerful tool for interpreting unqualified statements. This lesson explores its application to a common marital scenario: when a husband says 'You are forbidden to me.' Does this automatically become zihar (a pre-Islamic form of divorce requiring expiation)? Or does it depend on his intention? The Hidayah presents the view of the Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) that intention qualifies the absolute, while Imam Muhammad disagrees. Understanding this principle and the intra-Hanafi dispute will sharpen your ability to derive rulings from text.

Guided Path
1
State the principle and its meaning
Articulate the principle 'al-mutlaq yahtamil al-muqayyad' in your own words. Understand that an absolute ruling or term can be restricted by context, evidence, or intention.
2
Identify the contested case
The case is a husband saying 'You are forbidden to me' without specifying the type of prohibition. Determine whether this statement can be considered zihar if the husband intends it.
The wife can become prohibited by divorce, zihar, or other means; thus the prohibition is ambiguous.
3
Present the Shaykhayn's position and proof
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that if the husband intends zihar, it becomes zihar. Their proof: the absolute prohibition can be qualified by intention, and zihar involves a type of prohibition.
The principle directly supports this: al-mutlaq yahtamil al-muqayyad.
4
Present Muhammad's objection
Imam Muhammad argues that zihar requires explicit comparison to a prohibited woman (like the mother). Saying 'like a donkey' lacks this pillar (rukn) of similitude, so it cannot be zihar.
Muhammad focuses on the literal meaning of the statement, not the intention alone.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The later Hanafi authorities (mufta bihi) adopt the Shaykhayn's view. Understand why: the principle of qualifying the absolute by intention is well-established, and the majority followed them.
This shows the practical effect: in fatwa, intention determines the ruling.

وَهُنَا يُمْكِنُهُ الْقُرْبَانُ مِنْ غَيْرِ شَيْءٍ يَلْزَمُهُ، لِأَنَّ الْمُسْتَثْنَى يَوْمٌ مُنَكَّرٌ فَلَهُ أَنْ يَجْعَلَهُ أَيَّ يَوْمٍ شَاءَ فَلَا يَمُرُّ عَلَيْهِ يَوْمٌ مِنْ أَيَّامِ السَّنَةِ الظِّهَارَ، وَهَذَا عِنْدَهُمَا، وَقَالَ مُحَمَّدٌ لَيْسَ بِظِهَارِ لِانْعِدَامِ التَّشْبِيهِ بِالْمُحَرَّمَةِ، وَهُوَ رُكْنٌ فِيهِ، وَلَهُمَا أَنَّهُ أَطْلَقَ الْحُرْمَةَ، وَفِى الظِّهَارِ نَوْعُ حُرْمَةٍ، وَالْمُطْلَقُ يَحْتَمِلُ الْمُقَيَّدَ. البحر الرائق: كتاب الطلاق، باب الإيلاء، ج ۴ ص ۷۴، ط: دار الكتاب الإسلامي
(88) The absolute can be qualified. Translation: The absolute implies the qualified. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that when something is absolute, i.e., free from restrictions, it is valid to qualify that ruling because it possesses the possibility of being qualified. The author of Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that if a husband says to his wife 'You are forbidden to me' and then is asked about his intention: if he intends zihar, then according to the two shaykhs it will be zihar, because he uttered absolute prohibition and the speaker's intention can qualify the absolute. Since a wife can become prohibited in several ways—e.g., by irrevocable divorce or by zihar—the prohibition has multiple aspects; thus the husband's intention specifies it. If he intends zihar, then zihar is established, because zihar contains a type of prohibition, so the absolute prohibition is qualified by the prohibition of zihar, and every absolute can be qualified. As for the second case, when he says 'By Allah, I will not approach you for a year except one day,' then the one making Ila' is he who cannot have intercourse within four months except by something that incurs a liability, but here he can have intercourse without any liability, because the excepted day is indefinite; he can make it any day he wishes, so no day of the year passes except he can make it the excepted day. Tabyin al-Haqa'iq, Kitab al-Talaq, Bab al-Ila', vol. 2, p. 225, al-Matba'a al-Kubra al-Amiriyya; al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Talaq, Bab al-Ila', vol. 2, p. 413, Rahmaniyya. And his saying 'You are to me like a donkey or a pig or anything that is forbidden in itself' is like his saying 'You are forbidden to me' as in al-Bazzaziyya. His saying 'and it is zihar if he intends it' means zihar, and this is according to them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). Muhammad said it is not zihar due to the absence of similitude with a prohibited woman, which is a pillar in it. Their proof is that he uttered absolute prohibition, and in zihar there is a type of prohibition, and the absolute can be qualified. Al-Bahr al-Raiq: Kitab al-Talaq, Bab al-Ila', vol. 4, p. 74, Dar al-Kitab al-Islami.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage presents a foundational legal principle: al-mutlaq yahtamil al-muqayyad (the absolute implies the qualified). This is a definition-ruling passage. The commentator explains that when a ruling is stated in absolute terms, it can be restricted or specified by context or intention. The principle is then applied to two cases. The first case: a husband saying 'You are forbidden to me' without specifying the type of prohibition. According to the Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), if the husband intends zihar, it becomes zihar. This is because the absolute word 'forbidden' is capable of being qualified by intention. The second case from Tabyin al-Haqa'iq involves Ila' (vow of abstinence). If a husband swears not to approach his wife for a year except one day, he is not considered a muli because he can fulfill the oath without any required expiation; the excepted day is indefinite, so he can choose any day. Both cases illustrate the maxim. The passage also includes an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Muhammad holds that saying 'You are to me like a donkey' does not constitute zihar because zihar requires resemblance to a prohibited woman (like a mother or sister), and animals are not such. The Shaykhayn respond that the absolute prohibition includes zihar's type of prohibition. The relied-upon position (which is mufta bihi) is that of the Shaykhayn.

Key Terms
مطلق (mutlaq): A term or ruling without any qualifying restrictions, applicable to all cases unless specified otherwise.
مقيد (muqayyad): A term or ruling restricted by a condition, context, or intention, narrowing its application.
ظہار (zihar): A pre-Islamic form of divorce where the husband declares his wife like his mother, requiring expiation before resuming relations.
إيلاء (ila'): A vow by the husband to abstain from marital relations for a period, typically four months, triggering legal consequences.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is not zihar because the word 'forbidden' is absolute.
B) It is zihar because the absolute can be qualified by intention.
C) It is a binding divorce without possibility of revocation.
D) It is merely a promise without legal effect.

Key Takeaway
The absolute can be qualified by intention; in zihar, the Shaykhayn's view that intention specifies the ruling is the relied-upon position.

Compensability of Marital Authority in Khul'
Explains that marital authority can be compensated even though it is not wealth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that marital authority (milka al-nikah) is compensable in khul' and explain its analogy to qisas.

Core Idea
Marital authority is not wealth, but can be compensated in khul', just like qisas.

This passage from al-Hidayah establishes a key principle of khul': the husband's right over the marriage (milka al-nikah) can be exchanged for compensation even though it is not material wealth. The author uses an analogy to qisas to demonstrate that non-wealth rights can be compensable. Understanding this principle is essential to grasp the validity of khul' agreements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal principle
Read the opening statement: 'Indeed, the ownership of marriage is among things for which compensation is permissible even though it is not wealth.' This is the principle being established.
The principle directly counters the notion that only wealth can be exchanged.
2
Understand the nature of milka al-nikah
Marital authority is a non-wealth right. The author clarifies that it is not 'mal' (wealth) but still can be compensated because it is a right that can be given up.
Hanafi fiqh distinguishes between property rights and personal rights; the latter can sometimes be compensable.
3
Analyze the qiyas to qisas
The analogy is made to the right of qisas (retaliation). Qisas is not wealth either, but it can be commuted for monetary compensation. Similarly, milka al-nikah can be bought out in khul'.
The illa (effective cause) is that both are non-wealth rights that can be voluntarily relinquished for compensation.
4
Apply the principle to a concrete case
Consider the husband's statement: 'I divorce you for one thousand dirhams.' If the wife accepts, the divorce takes effect and the wife must pay. This is valid because the husband receives compensation for his marital authority.
The compensation becomes binding upon acceptance, just as in qisas the compensation becomes binding upon agreement.
5
Recognize the ruling's consequences
The khul' is valid (sahih), and the wife owes the agreed compensation. There is no dispute among the Hanafi school on this point.
The authority weight is zahir al-riwayah, and it is mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۸۹) إِنَّ مِلْكَ النِّكَاحِ مِمَّا يَجُوزُ الْاعْتِيَاضُ عَنْهُ وَإِنْ لَّمْ يَكُنْ مَالًا. ترجمہ: ملک نکاح ان چیزوں میں سے ہے جن کا عوض لینا جائز ہے اگر چہ یہ مال نہیں ہے۔ تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ ملک نکاح ان اشیاء کے قبیل سے ہے جن کے عوض مال لینا جائز ہے، جیسے قصاص وغیرہ ۔ مملک نکاح مال نہیں ہے لیکن بصورت خلع نکاح کے بدلے میں عوض لینا درست ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلارہے ہیں کہ اگر شوہر نے بیوی کو مال کے بدلے میں طلاق دیدی، مثلاً یہ کہا طَلَّقْتُكِ عَلَى أَلْفِ دِرْهَم “ میں نے ایک ہزار درہم کے عوض تجھے طلاق دی اور بیوی نے اسے قبول کیا تو طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی اور بیوی پر ایک ہزار درہم کی ادائیگی لازم ہوگی، کیونکہ مذکورہ صورت میں شوہر نے ملک نکاح کے بدلے میں عوض یعنی مال لیا اور نکاح ان اشیاء میں سے ہے جن کے بدلے عوض لینا درست ہے، نکاح اگر چہ مال نہیں ہے مگر اس کو ختم کرنے کے لئے عوض لینا بصورت خلع جائز ہے، جیسے قصاص کے عوض معاوضہ لینا جائز ہے ، حالانکہ قصاص مال نہیں ہے۔ الهداية كتاب الطلاق، باب الخلع، ج ۲ ص ۴۱۴، ط: رحمانیه (وَلَزِمَهَا الْمَالُ لِأَنَّهُ لَمْ يَرْضَ بِخُرُوجِ الْبُضْعِ عَنْ مِلْكِهِ إِلَّا بِهِ وَهُوَ يَجُوزُ الْاعْتِيَاضُ عَنْهُ وَإِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ مَالًا كَحَقِّ الْقِصَاصِ فَوَجَبَ بِالْتِزَامِهَا لَهُ. تبيين الحقائق كتاب الطلاق، باب الخلع، ج ۲ ص ۲۲۹، ط: المطبعة الكبرى الأميرية الهداية كتاب الطلاق، باب الخلع، ج ۲ ص ۴۱۴، حاشیہ نمبر ۹، ط: رحمانیه
(89) Indeed, the ownership of marriage is among things for which compensation is permissible even though it is not wealth. Translation: The ownership of marriage is among the things for which taking compensation is permissible even though it is not wealth. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that marital authority belongs to the category of things for which taking wealth in exchange is permissible, like qisas, etc. The ownership of marriage is not wealth, but in the case of khul', taking compensation for the marriage is valid. The author of Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that if the husband divorced his wife for wealth, for example saying 'I divorce you for one thousand dirhams' and the wife accepts, then the divorce takes effect and the wife is obligated to pay one thousand dirhams, because in this case the husband took compensation for the marital authority, and marriage is among those things for which taking compensation is valid. Although marriage is not wealth, taking compensation to terminate it in khul' is valid, just as it is valid to take compensation for qisas, despite qisas not being wealth. Al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Talaq, Bab al-Khul', Vol. 2, p. 414, Rahmaniyya. (And the wealth is binding upon her because he did not consent to the exit of the marriage right from his ownership except with it, and it is permissible to take compensation for it even if it is not wealth, like the right of qisas; thus it becomes obligatory by her assumption of it. Tabyin al-Haqa'iq, Kitab al-Talaq, Bab al-Khul', Vol. 2, p. 229, al-Matba'a al-Kubra al-Amiriyya; al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Talaq, Bab al-Khul', Vol. 2, p. 414, footnote 9, Rahmaniyya.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage establishes another legal principle: milka al-nikah (marital authority) is among those rights that can be exchanged for compensation, even though it is not physical wealth. The commentator explains that just as qisas (retaliation) can be commuted for monetary compensation, so too can marital rights be bought out in khul'. The application: if a husband says 'I divorce you for a thousand dirhams' and the wife accepts, the divorce is valid and the wife must pay. The principle is supported by an analogy (qiyas) to qisas. The Arabic quotes from Hidayah and Tabyin al-Haqa'iq affirm that compensation becomes binding because the husband only consented to the dissolution of marriage upon receiving it, and such compensation is permissible by analogy to qisas.

Key Terms
ملک نکاح (milka al-nikah): The husband's right to enjoy the marriage, which is compensable in khul'.
خلع (khul'): Divorce initiated by the wife with payment of compensation to the husband.
قصاص (qisas): Retaliation in kind for murder or injury; can be commuted for compensation (diyah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the permissibility of taking compensation in khul' according to the passage?
A) Because the husband owns the wife and can sell her.
B) By analogy to qisas, where a non-wealth right can be compensated.
C) Because the wife must always pay to get divorced.
D) It is not permissible; the text is only theoretical.

Key Takeaway
Marital authority is a non-wealth right that can be compensated in khul' by analogy to qisas.

Compensation for Marriage Rights in Khul'
States a principle about compensation for marriage rights with proof by qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that marriage rights can be compensated and explain the qiyas to qisas.

Core Idea
Marriage rights are not property but can be exchanged for compensation, like qisas.

This lesson examines a foundational Hanafi principle from Al-Hidayah: in khul', the husband's marital right (milk al-nikah) can be exchanged for compensation, even though it is not property per se. The author proves this by analogy to the right of qisas (retaliation), which also is not property but can be waived for compensation. Understanding this principle clarifies the validity of khul' and the binding nature of the compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The passage addresses whether the ownership of marriage (milk al-nikah) can be subject to compensation (iwad). The Hanafi position is that it can, even though it is not property (mal).
The issue arises in khul' where the wife pays to obtain divorce.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The proof is qiyas (analogy) to the right of qisas (retaliation). Just as qisas is not property but can be compensated, so too can milk al-nikah.
Qisas is a personal right, not a property right, yet it can be waived for compensation.
3
Understand the analogy (wajh al-dalalah)
The common 'illa (effective cause) is that both milk al-nikah and qisas are non-property rights that can be exchanged for property because the law permits compensation for them. Thus, the ruling of permissibility transfers from qisas to marriage rights.
The 'illa is not explicitly stated but is implied: both are rights that the owner can relinquish in exchange for property.
4
Apply the ruling
The husband's statement 'I divorce you for 1000 dirhams' with the wife's acceptance results in a valid divorce and a binding obligation for her to pay the compensation.
The compensation becomes wajib upon her acceptance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ مِلْكَ النِّكَاحِ مِمَّا يَجُوزُ الْاعْتِيَاضُ عَنْهُ وَإِنْ لَّمْ يَكُنْ مَالًا. وَلَزِمَهَا الْمَالُ لِأَنَّهُ لَمْ يَرْضَ بِخُرُوجِ الْبُضْعِ عَنْ مِلْكِهِ إِلَّا بِهِ وَهُوَ يَجُوزُ الْاعْتِيَاضُ عَنْهُ وَإِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ مَالًا كَحَقِّ الْقِصَاصِ فَوَجَبَ بِالْتِرَامِهَا لَهُ.
Indeed, the ownership of marriage is among that for which compensation is permissible, even if it is not property. And the property is binding on her because he was not satisfied with the exit of the conjugal right from his ownership except with it, and it is permissible to give compensation for it even if it is not property, like the right of qisas, so it is binding due to her undertaking of it.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage from Al-Hidayah states a key principle in the chapter of Khul': the marital right (milk al-nikah) is not property in itself, but it can be exchanged for property when dissolving the marriage. The commentary provides the proof by analogy to qisas (retaliation), which also is not property but can be compensated. The ruling establishes that if a husband says 'I divorce you for 1000 dirhams' and the wife accepts, the divorce is valid and she must pay the sum.

Key Terms
خلع (khul'): Divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for compensation to the husband.
ملک نکاح (milk al-nikah): The marital authority and right to the marriage bond.
متقوم (muttaqawwim): Having monetary value in Islamic law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for allowing compensation in khul' even though marriage rights are not property?
A) It is allowed by explicit Quranic text.
B) Analogy to qisas, which also is not property but can be compensated.
C) The wife's consent makes it valid.
D) It is permitted by ijma'.

Key Takeaway
Marriage rights can be compensated in khul' by analogy to qisas, even though they are not property.
