Deception in Contract: Liability for Mahr in Khul'
Principle and intra-Hanafi dispute on liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that deception within a contract creates liability (daman) to determine the required compensation in khul' and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Deception within a contract obligates compensation regardless of whether the deceived item is property.

This passage introduces a foundational Hanafi principle: deception (taghrir) that occurs within the framework of a contract imposes liability (daman). It then applies this principle to a khul' scenario where the wife offers a specific vinegar as her compensation, but the vinegar turns out to be wine—a non-property item. The application leads to an intra-Hanafi dispute: does she return the exact mahr she received, or does she give an equivalent amount of medium-quality vinegar? Let's unpack the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle and the Case
First, extract the general rule: 'التغرير في ضمن العقد يوجب الضمان' (deception within a contract imposes liability). Then note the specific case: a wife in khul' offers a specified vinegar as compensation, but it is actually wine.
The case involves a contract of khul' where the wife pays compensation to be released from marriage.
2
State Abu Hanifa's Position and Evidence
Abu Hanifa rules that she must return the exact mahr (dower) she had previously received. His evidence: the deception makes her liable; the husband's loss is precisely the mahr he gave.
His reasoning follows the principle directly: taghrir → daman = return of what was received.
3
State the Sahibayn's Position and Evidence
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani rule that she must provide a measure of medium-quality vinegar equivalent to what she promised. Their evidence: the compensation should resemble the specified property, not the mahr.
They focus on the subject of the contract (the vinegar) rather than the mahr as the measure of liability.
4
Analyse the Legal Reasoning
Both positions apply the principle of taghrir, but differ on what constitutes the ‘loss’. Abu Hanifa sees the mahr as the actual loss; the Sahibayn see the promised vinegar as the benchmark for compensation.
The dispute highlights how the same principle can yield different outcomes when determining the measure of daman.
5
Determine the Practical Consequence (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
In practice, if the wife deceived, under Abu Hanifa she returns exactly what she took (the mahr); under the Sahibayn she gives an amount of medium vinegar equal to what she promised. The mufta bihi (relied-upon) position is Abu Hanifa's view.
The tarjih is based on the direct application of the principle to the actual loss suffered.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ التَّغْرِيرَ فِى ضِمْنِ الْعَقْدِ يُوجِبُ الضَّمَانَ. فَإِنَّهُ يَلْزَمُهُ عَلَيْهَا رَدُّ الْمَهُرِ الَّذِي أَخَذَتْهُ عِندَ أَبي حَنِيفَةَ. وَعِنْدَهُمَا كَيْلٌ مِثْلُ ذَلِكَ مِنْ خَلْ وَسَطٍ، وَهَذَا وَالصَّدَاقُ سَوَاءٌ لِأَنَّهَا سَمَّتُ مَالًا وَغَرَّتْهُ بِذَلِكَ فَكَانَتْ ضَامِنَةً لَّانَّ التَّغْرِيرَ فِي ضِمُنِ الْعَقْدِ يُوجِبُ الضَّمَانَ.
Indeed, deception within the contract imposes liability. So it is binding upon her to return the mahr that she took, according to Abu Hanifa. And according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), a measure of medium vinegar like that (is binding). And this and the dower are the same because she specified property and deceived him by that, so she is liable because deception within the contract imposes liability.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage introduces the principle that deception within a contract creates liability (daman). It then applies it to a khul' case where the wife offers a specific vinegar as compensation, but it turns out to be wine. Abu Hanifa ruled that she must return the actual mahr she had received from the husband. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) ruled that she must give an equivalent amount of medium-quality vinegar. The underlying reason is that she deceived him by calling it property (vinegar) when it was not (wine). This principle is used to protect against fraud in transactions.

Key Terms
تغرير (taghrir): Deception or misrepresentation in a contract.
ضمان (daman): Liability to compensate for loss or damage.
مهر (mahr): The dower given by the husband to the wife in marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what must the wife return if she deceived the husband in khul' by offering vinegar that was actually wine?
A) The exact mahr she had received.
B) An equivalent amount of vinegar.
C) Nothing because wine is not property.
D) The value of the wine.

Key Takeaway
Deception within a contract obligates compensation; in khul', the relied-upon Hanafi position requires the wife to return the actual mahr received.

Principle: Milk al-Bud' is Non-Property During Exit
States that the right to intercourse is non-property when marriage ends

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why milk al-bud' is non-mutaqawwim during exit and distinguish the ruling of khul' compensation between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn when deception occurs.

Core Idea
Milk al-bud' loses its property status at divorce, so khul' can be made with non-property but deception triggers return of mahr.

In this lesson, we examine a key Hanafi distinction: the conjugal right (milk al-bud') is considered property (mutaqawwim) when entering marriage, but it ceases to be property when the marriage ends. This distinction has practical consequences for khul' (divorce initiated by the wife). We will explore the general rule and the exception when the wife deceives by promising vinegar that turns out to be wine, leading to an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Guided Path
1
State the general principle
Identify the ruling that milk al-bud' is non-property during exit (khuruj) as opposed to entry (dukhul).
This is the core legal premise of the passage.
2
Apply to khul' with non-property compensation
Understand that if khul' is exchanged for a non-property item (e.g., wine), the khul' is valid but the compensation is not binding.
Because the conjugal right is not property, it can be exchanged for non-property.
3
Identify the exceptional case
Note the contrapositive: when the wife specifies a particular vinegar (property) but it turns out to be wine, deception occurs.
The phrase 'بخلاف' signals the exception.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's ruling
According to Abu Hanifa, the wife must return the mahr she took because she deceived the husband.
This is based on the principle of taghrir (deception).
5
Analyze the Sahibayn's ruling
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, she must provide an equivalent amount of medium vinegar.
They consider the compensation as a contractual obligation despite the defect.
6
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference is the form of restitution: return of mahr vs. specific performance of vinegar equivalent.
The author favors Abu Hanifa's view as the mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ مِلْكَ الْبُضَعِ فِي حَالَةِ الْخُرُوجِ غَيْرُ مُتَقَوَّمٍ بِخِلَافِ حَالَةِ الدُّخُولِ. بِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَا خَالَعَ عَلَى خَلْ بِعَيْنِهِ فَظَهَرَ خَمْرًا فَإِنَّهُ يَلْزَمُهُ عَلَيْهَا رَدُّ الْمَهُرِ الَّذِي أَخَذَتْهُ عِندَ أَبي حَنِيفَةَ. وَعِنْدَهُمَا كَيْلٌ مِثْلُ ذَلِكَ مِنْ خَلْ وَسَطٍ.
Indeed, the ownership of the vulva (conjugal right) in the state of exit is not considered property (ghayr mutaqawwim), contrary to the state of entry. Contrary to when he made khul' on a specific vinegar and it appeared to be wine, so it is binding upon her to return the mahr that she took according to Abu Hanifa, and according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) a measure of medium vinegar like that.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage establishes a key distinction: the conjugal right (milk al-bud') is considered property (mutaqawwim) when entering marriage (e.g., for mahr), but it is not considered property when the marriage ends (e.g., in khul' or talaq). This means that if khul' is done in exchange for wine or pork (non-property items), the khul' is valid but the compensation is not binding as property. However, the text gives a counter-example: if the wife specifies a particular vinegar (which is property) but it turns out to be wine, then since she deceived him, she must either return the mahr (Abu Hanifa) or give an equivalent of vinegar (Sahibayn). The exception is because of deception (taghrir).

Key Terms
ملک بضع (milk al-bud'): The right to sexual intercourse with the spouse, a marital right.
متقوم (mutaqawwim): Having monetary value in Islamic law.
دخول/خروج (dukhul/khuruj): Entry into marriage and exit from marriage (divorce/khul').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if a wife in khul' specifies a certain vinegar as compensation, but it turns out to be wine?
A) The khul' is invalid.
B) She must return the mahr according to Abu Hanifa.
C) She must give wine according to both.
D) She does nothing because the compensation is non-property.

Key Takeaway
Milk al-bud' is non-property during exit, making khul' valid with non-property compensation, but deception in specified property triggers restitution according to the intra-Hanafi preference of Abu Hanifa.

Khul' with Deceptive Consideration: Vinegar Turns Out to be Wine
Ruling when khul' is on vinegar but it is wine

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khul' where the wife provides wine instead of vinegar, and distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the compensation.

Core Idea
If the wife deceives by naming a valuable item but delivers a non-valuable one, she must compensate: Abu Hanifa says return the dower; Sahibayn say pay average vinegar.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in khul' where the wife offers vinegar (a valuable commodity) as consideration, but it turns out to be wine (non-valuable). The Hanafi school unanimously holds the khul' valid, but differs on the compensation owed due to the wife's deception (taghrir). We will explore the underlying principle that milk al-bud' (ownership of the vulva) is non-valuable in the context of divorce, and how taghrir creates liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario and the issue
The wife says she will give vinegar (a valuable good) for khul', but it turns out to be wine (non-valuable). The khul' is valid, but what compensation does the wife owe?
The khul' is not invalidated because the consideration is non-valuable; the marriage is dissolved.
2
Recall the principle of milk al-bud' in exit vs entry
In the state of khul' (exit), milk al-bud' is not considered valuable property (ghayr mutaqawwim). This means the wife does not owe the wine itself, but rather compensation for deception.
Contrast with marriage (entry) where milk al-bud' is valuable, so if wine is specified as dower, the proper dower (mahr al-mithl) is owed.
3
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa rules that the wife must return the exact dower (mahr) she received from the husband. Reason: she deceived him by naming a valuable item, so she is liable to return what she took.
The dower is the actual wealth she obtained from the husband.
4
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad rule that the wife must pay a measure of average vinegar equivalent to the value of the dower. Reason: she deceived him about the vinegar, so she must give what she promised (vinegar), but the actual object was wine, so she pays the value of average vinegar.
This is based on the principle that the deceived party is entitled to the object he thought he was receiving.
5
Analyse the reasoning using taghrir
Both views are based on taghrir (deception) creating daman (liability). The difference lies in what is considered the proper remedy: returning the actual consideration received (Abu Hanifa) or providing the promised item's equivalent (Sahibayn).
The text states: 'because she named wealth and deceived him, so she is liable; deception in a contract necessitates liability.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اصول ہدایہ جلد ثانی صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر بیوی نے شوہر سے کہا کہ میں اس سر کے متعین ملکے پر آپ سے خلع کرتی ہوں، شوہر نے خلع کر دیا، بعد میں دیکھا تو وہ شراب تھی، تو امام صاحب کے نزدیک بیوی پر وہ مہر دینا واجب ہے جو مہر شوہر نے اس کو دیا تھا، کیونکہ مذکورہ صورت میں بیوی نے شوہر کو عقد خلع میں دھوکا دیا ہے، کیونکہ اس نے (سرکہ) مال کا نام لیا ہے جبکہ وہ مال نہیں تھا بلکہ شراب تھی اور عقد میں دھوکا دینا یہ ضمان کو ثابت کرتا ہے، لہذا مذکورہ صورت میں عورت کے دھوکہ دینے کی وجہ سے اس پر مہر سمی کے بقدر مال واجب ہوگا۔ (۹۱) إِنَّ مِلْكَ الْبُضَعِ فِي حَالَةِ الْخُرُوجِ غَيْرُ مُتَقَوَّمٍ بِخِلَافِ حَالَةِ الدُّخُولِ . ) ترجمہ: بے شک ملک بضع حالت خروج میں غیر متقوم ہے برخلاف حالت دخول کے (اس میں متقوم ہے )۔ تشریح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ ملک بضہ حالت خروج یعنی طلاق خلع وغیرہ کی صورت میں غیر متقوم ہے برخلاف حالت دخول ( بحالت نکاح ) اس میں متقوم ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر شراب یا خنزیر کے عوض ضلع کیا گیا تو ضلع ہو جائے گا لیکن شراب وغیرہ واجب نہیں ہوگی ، اس لئے ملک بضع حالت خروج یعنی طلاق خلع وغیرہ کی صورت میں مالِ متقوم نہیں ہے۔ برخلاف اگر شخص نے شراب یا خنزیر کے عوض کسی عورت سے نکاح کیا تو نکاح صحیح ہوگا اور مہر مسمی کے بدل مہر مثل واجب ہوگا، اس لئے کہ ملک بضع حالت دخول ( بحالت نکاح ) میں مال متقوم ہے اس لئے اس کے عوض میں مہر سمی یعنی شراب وغیرہ کا بدل بشکل مہر مثل واجب ہو گا۔ (بِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَا خَالَعَ عَلَى خَلْ بِعَيْنِهِ فَظَهَرَ خَمْرًا) فَإِنَّهُ يَلْزَمُهُ عَلَيْهَا رَدُّ الْمَهُرِ الَّذِي أَخَذَتْهُ عِندَ أَبي حَنِيفَةَ. وَعِنْدَهُمَا كَيْلٌ مِثْلُ ذَلِكَ مِنْ خَلْ وَسَطٍ، وَهَذَا وَالصَّدَاقُ سَوَاءٌ لِأَنَّهَا سَمَّتُ مَالًا وَغَرَّتْهُ بِذَلِكَ فَكَانَتْ ضَامِنَةً لَّانَّ التَّغْرِيرَ فِي ضِمُنِ الْعَقْدِ يُوجِبُ الضَّمَانَ. العناية: كتاب الطلاق، باب الخلع، ج ۴، ص ۲۲۰، ط: دار الفكر الهداية كتاب الطلاق، باب الخلع، ج ۲ ص ۴۱۴، ط: رحمانیه إِذَا خَالَعَهَا أَوْ طَلَّقَهَا وَهُوَ مُسْلِمٌ عَلَى خَمْرٍ أَوْ خِنْزِيرٍ أَوْ مَيْتَةٍ) أَوْ غَيْرِهَا مِمَّا لَا قِيمَةً =
Principles of Hidayah Volume Two: The author of Hidayah mentions the following principle and explains that if the wife said to the husband, 'I divorce myself from you for this specified vinegar,' and the husband divorced (khul'), and later it was seen that it was wine, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the wife must pay the dower that the husband had given her, because in this case the wife deceived the husband in the khul' contract, since she named it as wealth (vinegar) while it was not wealth but wine, and deception in a contract proves liability, so in this case because of the wife's deception, she owes wealth equivalent to the specified dower. (91) 'Indeed, ownership of the vulva in the state of exit is not valuable, unlike the state of entry.' Translation: Indeed, milk al-bud' in the state of exit (divorce, khul') is not valuable, unlike the state of entry (marriage) where it is valuable. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that milk al-bud' in the state of exit—i.e., divorce, khul', etc.—is not valuable, unlike the state of entry (marriage) where it is valuable. The author of Hidayah mentions this principle and explains that if khul' is done in exchange for wine or pork, the khul' is valid, but the wine etc. is not obligatory, because milk al-bud' in the state of exit (divorce, khul') is not valuable property. In contrast, if a man marries a woman in exchange for wine or pork, the marriage is valid and the proper dower (mahr al-mithl) is obligatory instead of the specified dower, because milk al-bud' in the state of entry (marriage) is valuable property, so in exchange for it, the specified dower (i.e., wine etc.) is replaced by the proper dower. (Contrary to when he made khul' on specific vinegar and it turned out to be wine): then according to Abu Hanifa, she must return the dower that she took. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), she must pay a measure of average vinegar of the same amount. This is equal to the dower because she named wealth and deceived him by that, so she is liable because deception in the contract necessitates liability. Al-Inayah: Kitab al-Talaq, Bab al-Khul', vol. 4, p. 220, Dar al-Fikr. Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Talaq, Bab al-Khul', vol. 2, p. 414, Rahmaniyyah. 'If he makes khul' with her or divorces her while he is a Muslim in exchange for wine or pork or carrion' or other things that have no value =
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling of khul' when the wife gives vinegar but it turns out to be wine, and the related principle of milk al-bud' (ownership of the vulva) being valuable only in marriage, not in divorce. The author first states the principle: 'In the state of exit (divorce/khul'), milk al-bud' is not valuable; in the state of entry (marriage), it is valuable.' He then applies it: if khul' is done for wine or pork, the khul' is valid but the wine/pork is not owed because the consideration is non-valuable. However, if the wife deceives the husband by claiming she is giving vinegar (a valuable good) but it is actually wine (non-valuable), she is liable to return the dower she received (according to Abu Hanifa) or pay an equivalent amount of average vinegar (according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). The reason is that she named wealth and deceived him, and deception in a contract creates liability. The passage contrasts this with marriage: if a man marries a woman in exchange for wine, the marriage is valid and the proper dower is owed because milk al-bud' in marriage is valuable.

Key Terms
ملک بضع (milk al-bud'): Ownership of the vulva — the right to sexual enjoyment, which is the subject of marriage and khul'.
متقوم (mutaqawwim): Legally valuable property that can be sold and compensated.
تغرير (taghrir): Deception or misrepresentation in a contract that leads to liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

6 min
Khul' on Unspecified Dirhams: The Meaning of 'Min'
Ruling on khul' with unspecified dirhams using linguistic principle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a wife offers khul' for unspecified dirhams and apply the Hanafi principle distinguishing 'min' as tab'id (partiality) vs bayan (explanation).

Core Idea
When 'min' is needed for sentence completion, it explains, not divides; thus the plural triggers the minimum of three.

This lesson explores a fascinating intersection of Arabic linguistics and fiqh. The word 'min' can indicate partiality ('some of') or explanation ('namely'). The Hanafi school applies a simple test: if the sentence is correct without 'min', then it indicates partiality; otherwise, it is explanatory. This test determines how much a wife must pay in khul' when she says 'my hand of dirhams' but has nothing. The ruling: three dirhams, because the plural noun 'dirhams' (without partiality) obliges the minimum of three.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wife's statement and the factual scenario
The wife says: 'Khul' me for what is in my hand of dirhams' (خَالِعْنِي عَلَى مَا فِي يَدِيَ مِنْ دَرَاهِمَ). After khul', it is discovered that she has no dirhams at all.
2
Apply the linguistic rule for 'min'
Determine whether 'min' is for partiality (tab'id) or explanation (bayan). Test: Is the phrase 'what is in my hand dirhams' (without 'min') correct? No, it is grammatically incomplete. Therefore, 'min' is explanatory, meaning 'namely' or 'which are'. Thus, 'dirhams' remains a plural noun without partiality.
The Quranic verse 'fajtanibū al-rijsa mina al-awthān' is cited as an example where 'min' is explanatory: 'avoid the abomination, namely idols.'
3
Determine the obligation from the plural noun
Since the wife committed to pay 'dirhams' (plural), and no specific number is mentioned, the Hanafi rule applies: the minimum of a plural in Arabic is three. Therefore, she must pay at least three dirhams.
4
State the final ruling
If she has nothing, she owes three dirhams. If she has one dirham, she must complete to three. If she has more, the husband takes that amount.
The ruling is based on the linguistic principle and the default that the plural minimum is three.
5
Distinguish from cases where 'min' indicates partiality
If the sentence is correct without 'min' (e.g., 'I have three of dirhams'), then 'min' indicates partiality, and only the specified portion is owed. In this khul' case, the sentence is incomplete without 'min', so it is explanatory, not partial.
An example from al-Jami': 'If in my hand of dirhams there are three, then his slave is free' – here 'min' is for partiality because the sentence is correct without it (the number specifies).

Commentary
This passage discusses a linguistic-legal principle regarding the word 'min' and applies it to a khul' case. The principle: If a sentence is complete and correct without 'min', then 'min' indicates partiality (tab'id, meaning 'some of'); otherwise, if the sentence is incorrect or incomplete without 'min', then 'min' is explanatory (bayani, meaning 'which is' or 'namely'). The author applies this to a wife who says, 'Khul' me for what is in my hand of dirhams' (خَالِعْنِي عَلَى مَا فِي يَدِيَ مِنْ دَرَاهِمَ). If she has nothing, she owes three dirhams because 'dirhams' is plural, and the minimum plural is three. The word 'min' here is explanatory, not partial, because without 'min' the phrase 'what is in my hand dirhams' would be incorrect. Thus, it specifies that the thing in her hand is dirhams. Since she mentioned a plural term, the minimum applies. The passage also references another principle: since milk al-bud' in exit is non-valuable, if khul' were for a non-valuable item like wine, nothing would be owed; but here the consideration is dirhams (valuable), so they are owed. The Arabic quote from al-Jami' provides a counterexample where 'min' is for partiality. The overall ruling is that she must pay at least three dirhams.

Key Terms
تبع یض (tab'id): Partiality; indicating a part of something ('some of').
بیان (bayan): Explanation; 'min' used to explain or specify the preceding noun.
اقل جمع (aqall al-jam'): The minimum number for a plural noun, which in Arabic is three.

B) One dirham.
C) Three dirhams.
D) The proper dower.

Key Takeaway
When a wife offers khul' for 'what is in my hand of dirhams' and has nothing, she must pay three dirhams due to the linguistic rule that 'min' is explanatory and the plural minimum is three.

The Rule of 'Min': Tab'id vs Bayan in Khul' Context
Linguistic rule for 'min' and its application

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul rule to determine whether the particle 'min' indicates partiality (tab'id) or explanation (bayan) in a given khul' or similar statement, and derive the resulting financial obligation.

Core Idea
If the sentence is correct without 'min', it indicates partiality; if not, it's explanatory—and in khul', that decides the due amount.

The particle 'min' is common in Arabic, but its legal effect depends on context. The Hanafi school has a precise linguistic rule to determine whether 'min' picks out a part (tab'id) or is merely an explanatory connector (bayan). This distinction has direct financial consequences, especially in khul' settlements. This lesson unpacks that rule and its application as recorded in al-Hidayah.

Guided Path
1
State the rule
The rule: in any place where speech is correct without 'min', then 'min' is for partiality (tab'id); otherwise it is for explanation (bayan/sillah).
2
Apply to the khul' example
Wife says: 'khali'ni 'ala ma fi yadi min darahima'. Test: remove 'min' → 'khali'ni 'ala ma fi yadi darahima' – grammatically defective (incomplete). Therefore 'min' is bayaniyyah.
3
Derive the amount owed
Since 'min' is bayaniyyah, 'darahim' retains its plural meaning. In Hanafi fiqh, the minimum of a plural (jam') is three. Hence, the wife owes three dirhams.
This holds even if she had nothing in hand.
4
Consider the alternative (Jami' example)
If the sentence is correct without 'min', e.g., 'fi yadi min al-darahim thalathah' (without 'min': 'fi yadi al-darahim thalathah' is correct), then 'min' is tab'id, and the specified number binds.

Every place where speech is correct without 'min', then 'min' is for partiality (tab'id), otherwise it is for explanation (bayan). Translation: Every place where speech is correct without the word 'min', then 'min' is for partiality; otherwise it is for explanation. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that where speech is correct without the word 'min', i.e., no disruption occurs in the speech, then the word 'min' there is for partiality. And where disruption occurs in speech without 'min', then 'min' is for explanation. The author of Hidayah, after mentioning this principle, explains that if a wife says to her husband: 'Divorce me (khul') in exchange for what is in my hand of dirhams,' and the husband gives khul', but later it is found that there was nothing in her hand, then three dirhams become obligatory upon the wife. This is because the wife mentioned dirhams in the plural, and the minimum of the plural is three. And 'min' in 'min darahima' is not for partiality but for explanation, because in this case without 'min' speech is disrupted, and where speech is not correct without 'min', 'min' is for explanation. When 'min' is explanatory, the word 'darahim' remains in the plural form, and due to the minimum of the plural being specified, three dirhams become obligatory upon the wife. He has a basis: because the ownership of the marital right (buda') is not compensable (ghayr mutaqawwim) at the time of exit, so nothing is obligatory in exchange for it, unlike the case of marriage and kitaba (contract of manumission) with wine, because the ownership of the master is compensable, and likewise the marital right at the time of entrance. (From Majma' al-Anhur and al-Hidayah). And if she says: 'Divorce me in exchange for what is in my hand of dirhams,' and the situation is that there is nothing in her hand, three dirhams are binding upon her. And if there is one dirham in her hand, she is ordered to complete three dirhams. And if there is more, then he has that. It cannot be said that he should not have the three dirhams, because 'min' is for partiality, as was said in al-Jami': 'If in my hand there are three dirhams, then his slave is free.' And if in his hand there are four dirhams, he would be [something]. Because 'min' may be for partiality and may be as a connector (sillah), as in the Almighty's saying: 'So avoid the abomination of idols.' So in every place where speech is correct without it, it is for partiality, as in the issue of al-Jami'. And in every place where speech is not correct without it, it is a connector, as in the issue of khul'. Because if she said: 'Divorce me in exchange for what is in my hand dirhams,' the speech would be defective. (Majma' al-Anhur: Kitab al-Talaq, Bab al-Khul', vol. 1, p. 761, Dar Ihya' al-Turath al-‘Arabi; al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Talaq, Bab al-Khul', vol. 2, p. 415, Rahmaniyyah)
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents a linguistic rule from usul al-fiqh concerning the particle 'min'. The principle is that if a sentence remains grammatically correct and meaningful without 'min', then 'min' indicates partiality (tab'id), i.e., it picks out a part of something. If the sentence is correct only with 'min', then 'min' is for explanation (bayan) or as a connector (sillah). The author applies this to a khul' case from al-Hidayah: A wife says 'khali'ni 'ala ma fi yadi min darahima' (divorce me for what is in my hand of dirhams). If she has nothing, she must pay three dirhams because 'min' here is bayaniyyah (explanatory), so the word 'darahim' retains its plural sense, and the minimum of the plural is three. The passage also includes a note that the ownership of the wife's marital right is not considered compensable (ghayr mutaqawwim) upon dissolution, so no equivalent is due for that, unlike in marriage or kitaba. Then it quotes a similar ruling from al-Jami' to reinforce the principle.

Key Terms
مِن (min): A preposition that can indicate partiality (tab'id) or serve as an explanatory connector (bayan/sillah).
تبعض (tab'id): Indicating a part of a whole; partiality.
بيان (bayan): Explanation; in this context, 'min' serves as an explanatory connector (sillah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the khul' case, why does the wife owe three dirhams even though she had nothing?
A) Because she agreed to pay three.
B) Because 'min' is tab'id, so she owes part of three.
C) Because 'min' is bayan, so the word 'darahim' is treated as plural and its minimum is three.
D) Because the husband can demand any amount.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule for 'min': its function (tab'id or bayan) hinges on syntactic completeness, with direct financial implications in khul'.

Khul' Is Not Invalidated by Invalid Conditions
States ruling and proof by analogy with marriage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that khul' remains valid despite invalid conditions and apply it to scenarios involving conditions contrary to the contract.

Core Idea
Khul' remains valid even with an invalid condition; only the condition is void.

In Hanafi fiqh, khul' is a dissolution of marriage with consideration. The school holds that it is a commutative contract, similar to marriage, and thus invalid conditions do not nullify the contract itself. This lesson examines the principle from Al-Hidayah and its application through a detailed case where a wife stipulates exemption from liability for a runaway slave.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? What is the hukm (valid/invalid)? The act is khul' with an invalid condition. The ruling is that the khul' is valid (sahih) and the condition is invalid (batil).
2
Locate the dalil
What proof does the author cite? The author cites analogy (qiyas) with marriage: just as marriage remains valid despite invalid conditions, so does khul'. Additionally, the nature of khul' as a commutative contract requires the safety of consideration, making an exemption condition invalid.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the dalil prove the ruling? The analogy with marriage shows that contracts can tolerate invalid conditions. The requirement of safety of consideration (salāmat al-‘iwaḍ) means that any condition negating this safety is void, but the contract remains binding.
4
Apply to the given case
Apply the principle to the example: wife says 'I make khul' with you for my runaway slave on condition that I am exempt from delivering him.' The condition exempts her from consideration, contradicting the contract's nature. The khul' is valid, but the condition is void. She must deliver the slave or its value.
5
Note the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical effect) is that the wife cannot avoid paying consideration despite the invalid condition. The khul' dissolves the marriage, and the husband is entitled to the agreed consideration or its equivalent.

ترجمه : خلع شروط فاسدہ سے باطل نہیں ہوتا۔
تشریح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ اگر خلع میں شرط فاسد لگائی جائے یعنی ایسی شرط جو مقتضائے عقد کے خلاف ہو تو ضلع درست ہوگا اور وہ شرط باطل ہوگی، اس لئے کہ ضلع اور نکاح شروط فاسدہ سے فاسد نہیں ہوتے بلکہ شرط فاسد خود فاسد ہو جاتی ہے۔

بِدُونِهِ كَانَ لِلتَّبْعِيضِ كَمَا فِي مَسْأَلَةِ الْجَامِعِ وَفِي كُلِّ مَوْضِعٍ لَا يَصِحُ بِدُونِهِ كَانَ صِلَةً كَمَا فِي مَسْأَلَةِ الْخُلْعِ فَإِنَّهَا لَوْ قَالَتْ خَالِعُنِي عَلَى مَا فِي يَدِى دَرَاهِمَ كَانَ الْكَلَامُ مُخْتَلًا. مجمع الأنهر : كتاب الطلاق، باب الخلع، ج ا ص ۷۶۱، ط: دار إحياء التراث العربي الهداية: كتاب الطلاق، باب الخلع ج ۲ ص ۴۱۵، ط: رحمانیه
قَوْلُهُ فَإِنْ خَالَعَهَا عَلَى عَبْدِ أَبَقَ لَهَا عَلَى أَنَّهَا بَرِيَّةٌ مِنْ ضَمَانِهِ لَمْ تَبْرَأَ لِأَنَّهُ عَقْدُ مُعَاوَضَةٍ فَيَقْتَضِى سَلَامَةَ الْعِوَضِ، وَاشْتِرَاطُ الْبَرَاءةِ شَرُطٌ فَاسِدٌ فَبَطَلَ فَكَانَ عَلَيْهَا تَسْلِيمُ عَيْنِهِ إِنْ قَدَرَتْ، وَتَسْلِيمُ قِيمَتِهِ إِنْ عَجَزَتْ أَشَارَ إِلَى أَنَّ الْخُلُعَ لَا يَبْطُلُ بِالشُّرُوطِ الْفَاسِدَةِ كَالنِّكَاحِ، وَلِذَا قَالَ فِي الْعِمَادِيَّةِ لَوْ خَالَعَهَا عَلَى أَنْ يُمْسِكَ الْوَلَدَ عِنْدَهُ صَحَ الْخُلْعُ، وَبَطَلَ الشَّرُطُ .
البحر الرائق: كتاب الطلاق، باب الخلع، ج ۴، ص ۸۷، ط: دار الكتاب الإسلامي
(93) Khul' is not invalidated by invalid conditions. Translation: Khul' does not become invalid by invalid conditions. Explanation: The meaning of the stated principle is that if an invalid condition is placed in khul', i.e., a condition contrary to the requirement of the contract, then the khul' will be valid and that condition will be invalid, because khul' and marriage are not invalidated by invalid conditions; rather, the invalid condition itself becomes invalid. The author of Al-Hidayah, by mentioning this principle, explains that if a wife says to her husband, 'I make khul' with you for my runaway slave on condition that I am free from liability for his delivery,' then the khul' is valid, and if the wife is able to deliver that slave, she must deliver the very same slave, and if she is unable, she must pay its value. Because in this scenario, the woman placed an invalid condition (of not delivering the slave), and khul' is not invalidated by an invalid condition. However, it becomes obligatory upon the woman to deliver the slave or its value to the husband, because khul' is a commutative contract: on one side, the woman gives consideration, and on the other side, the husband transfers ownership of his marriage to the wife. In a commutative contract, the safety of the consideration is necessary; stipulating exemption from liability is contrary to the safety of the consideration and is an invalid condition. Therefore, this condition is invalid, and the khul' is valid. When the khul' is valid, the consideration becomes obligatory upon her, whether she delivers the slave or its value. 'Without it, it is for division as in the issue of Al-Jami', and in every place where it is not valid without it, it is a particle as in the issue of khul': if she says, 'Divorce me for what is in my hand of dirhams,' the speech would be disordered.' (Majma' al-Anhur: Kitab al-Talaq, Bab al-Khul', vol. 1, p. 761, Dar Ihya' al-Turath al-Arabi. Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Talaq, Bab al-Khul', vol. 2, p. 415, Rahmaniyyah.) 'His statement: If he makes khul' with her for her runaway slave on condition that she is free from liability for it, she does not become free, because it is a commutative contract, and it requires safety of the consideration; stipulating exemption is an invalid condition, so it is invalid. Thus, she must deliver its entity if she is able, and its value if she is unable. This indicates that khul' is not invalidated by invalid conditions, like marriage. Therefore, Al-Imadiyyah says: If he makes khul' with her on condition that he keeps the child with him, the khul' is valid and the condition is invalid.' (Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: Kitab al-Talaq, Bab al-Khul', vol. 4, p. 87, Dar al-Kitab al-Islami.)
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi school. The principle 'Khul' is not invalidated by invalid conditions' is stated and then explained. The author uses the analogy with marriage: just as marriage remains valid despite invalid conditions, so too does khul'. The specific case given is where a wife makes khul' for a runaway slave on condition that she is not liable for its delivery. The ruling is that the khul' is valid, but the condition is invalid; thus she must still deliver the slave or its value. The proof is from Al-Hidayah, which emphasizes that khul' is a commutative contract requiring the safety of the consideration, so stipulating exemption from liability is invalid. The commentary clarifies that the Hanafi stance aligns with the principle that conditions contrary to the contract's requirements are null, but the contract itself stands.

Key Terms
خلع (khul'): A divorce initiated by the wife in return for compensation (consideration) paid to the husband.
شرط فاسد (shart fāsid): A condition that is contrary to the requirements of the contract, rendering the condition itself invalid but not the contract.
عقد معاوضة (ʿaqd muʿāwaḍa): A commutative contract where each party gives and receives something of value.
سلامة العوض (salāmat al-ʿiwaḍ): The safety of the consideration; i.e., the consideration must be free from defects or conditions that negate its delivery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Both the khul' and the condition are valid.
B) The khul' is valid but the condition is invalid, so she must still deliver the slave or its value.
C) The khul' is invalid because of the invalid condition.
D) The khul' is valid and the condition is valid, so she is exempt from delivering the slave.

Key Takeaway
Khul' remains valid despite an invalid condition because it is analogous to marriage in tolerating such conditions, and only the condition is void.

The Consideration Is Divided Over the Thing Considered
States a principle of contract division and applies it to khul' cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that consideration is divided proportionally over the subject matter in khul' contracts and calculate the owed amount when partial performance occurs.

Core Idea
Consideration in khul' is divisible: each divorce gets a proportional share of the lump sum.

The Hanafi school treats a khul' agreement for multiple divorces as a divisible contract. This lesson explores a key maxim: 'The consideration is divided over the thing considered.' We will see how this applies when a wife offers a lump sum for three divorces but the husband only divorces once, understanding the legal reasoning behind proportional obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
The maxim 'al-‘iwaḍ yanqasimu ‘ala al-mu‘awwaḍ' means consideration is divided over the thing considered. This is the foundational rule.
2
Understand the scenario
The wife says: 'Divorce me three times for 1000 dirhams.' The husband divorces her only once. The question is: how much does she owe?
The husband's action is partial fulfilment of the requested divorces.
3
Apply the principle
Since the consideration is divisible, each divorce is worth one-third of 1000 dirhams. Thus, she owes 333.33 dirhams.
The principle applies even though the wife only gets one divorce instead of three.
4
Consider the linguistic proof
The letter 'ba' in 'bi-alf' (with one thousand) indicates substitution; the consideration is associated with each individual divorce.
This linguistic analysis supports the divisibility.
5
Note the benefit to the wife
By giving one divorce, the husband benefits the wife because she only owes a fraction but gains freedom from marriage.
The husband's action is treated as an act of kindness, strengthening the validity of the partial performance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۹۴) الْعِوَضُ يَنْقَسِمُ عَلَى الْمُعَوَّضِ .
ترجمہ : عوض معوض پر تقسیم ہوتا ہے۔
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ عوض کو معوض کے اجزاء پر تقسیم کرنا درست ہے، یعنی عوض کو معوض کے اجزاء پر تقسیم کیا جائے گا۔
صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر بیوی نے شوہر سے یہ کہا طَلَّقْنِي ثَلَاثًا بِأَلْفِ “ کہ مجھے ہزار کے عوض تین طلاقیں دو، پس شوہر نے اس کو ایک طلاق دی تو عورت پر ہزار کا ثلث یعنی تین سو تینتیس (۳۳۳) درا ہم لازم ہوں گے، اس لئے کہ مذکورہ صورت میں ہزار عوض ہے اور تین طلاق معوض ہے اور عوض کو معوض پر تقسیم کیا جائے گا، گویا ہر طلاق کا عوض (۳۳۳) در اہم ہوں گی ، اب شوہر جتنی طلاقیں دے گا اتنے عوض کا مستحق ہوگا، حرف با عوضوں پر داخل ہوتا ہے، گویا الف ثلاث کا عوض ہے اور عوض معوض پر تقسیم ہوتا ہے، اس لئے الف ثلاث پر تقسیم ہو گا، اور ہر ہر طلاق کا حصہ ثلث الف ہوگا۔
الهداية: كتاب الطلاق، باب الخلع ج ۲ ص ۴۱۵، ط: رحمانیه
فَإِن قَالَتْ: طَلِّقْنِي ثَلاثًا بِأَلْفِ دِرُهَم فَطَلَّقَهَا وَاحِدَةً فَلَهُ قُلْتُ الْأَلْفِ لِأَنَّ حَرُفَ الْبَاءِ يَصْحَبُ الْأَبْدَالَ، وَالْأَعْوَاضَ، وَالْعِوَضُ يَنْقَسِمُ عَلَى الْمُعَوَّضِ، فَهِيَ لَمَّا الْتَمَسَتْ الثُّلْتَ بِأَلْفِ فَقَدْ جَعَلَتْ بِإِزَاءِ كُلِّ تَطْلِيقَةٍ ثُلُثَ الْأَلْفِ ، ثُمَّ فِيمَا صَنَعَ الزَّوْجُ مَنْفَعَةً لَهَا، لِأَنَّهَا رَضِيَتْ بِوُجُوبِ جَمِيعِ الْألْفِ عَلَيْهَا بِمُقَابَلَةِ التَّخَلُّصِ مِنْ زَوْجِهَا فَتَكُونُ أَرْضَى بِوُجُوبِ ثُلُثِ الْأَلْفِ عَلَيْهَا إِذَا تَخَلَّصَتْ مِنْ زَوْجِهَا، وَبِالْوَاحِدَةٍ تَتَخَلَّصُ مِنْهُ.
المبسوط للسرخسى كتاب الطلاق، باب الخلع، ج ۶ ص ۱۷۳، ط: دار المعرفة الهداية: كتاب الطلاق، باب الخلع، ج ۲ ص ۴۱۵، ط: رحمانیه
(94) The consideration is divided over the thing considered. Translation: The consideration is divided over the thing considered. Explanation: The meaning of the stated principle is that it is valid to divide the consideration over the parts of the thing considered, i.e., the consideration will be divided over the parts of the thing considered. The author of Al-Hidayah, by mentioning this principle, explains that if a wife says to her husband: 'Divorce me three times for one thousand,' i.e., give me three divorces for one thousand dirhams, and then the husband divorces her once, then the woman owes one-third of one thousand, i.e., three hundred and thirty-three dirhams. Because in this case, one thousand is the consideration and three divorces are the thing considered, and the consideration will be divided over the thing considered. So each divorce's consideration is 333 dirhams. So the husband will be entitled to consideration for as many divorces as he gives. The letter ba enters upon considerations, so the thousand is the consideration for three, and the consideration is divided over the thing considered. Thus, the thousand will be divided over three, and each divorce's share will be one-third of the thousand. (Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Talaq, Bab al-Khul', vol. 2, p. 415, Rahmaniyyah. 'If she says: Divorce me three times for one thousand dirhams, and he divorces her once, then he gets one-third of the thousand, because the letter ba accompanies compensations and considerations, and the consideration is divided over the thing considered. So when she sought three divorces for one thousand, she made in exchange for each divorce one-third of the thousand. Moreover, in what the husband did, there is benefit for her, because she consented to the obligation of the entire thousand upon her in exchange for being freed from her husband, so she would be more content with the obligation of one-third of the thousand upon her if she is freed from her husband, and with one divorce she becomes freed from him.' (Al-Mabsut li al-Sarakhsi, Kitab al-Talaq, Bab al-Khul', vol. 6, p. 173, Dar al-Ma'rifah. Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Talaq, Bab al-Khul', vol. 2, p. 415, Rahmaniyyah.)
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof applying a general contractual principle to family law. The principle 'The consideration is divided over the thing considered' is exemplified in the context of khul' or divorce by talaq with consideration. The Hanafi ruling explains that if a wife offers a lump sum for multiple divorces, the husband only receives a proportionate share if he divorces fewer. The proof is from Al-Hidayah and Al-Mabsut, which use linguistic analysis: the letter 'ba' in 'bi-alf' indicates substitution, and the consideration is divisible. The commentary by the author of the current book clarifies that this principle applies even if the wife benefits from a lesser obligation. The practical consequence is that each divorce is treated as a separate transaction.

Key Terms
عوض (‘iwaḍ): Consideration or compensation paid in a contract, here the thousand dirhams.
معوض (mu‘awwaḍ): The thing for which consideration is given, here the three divorces.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) 1,000 dirhams.
B) Nothing, because he did not fulfill the contract.
C) 333.33 dirhams.
D) 500 dirhams.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle that consideration is divisible over the subject matter ensures fairness in partial performance of khul' contracts.

The Conditioned Does Not Divide Over Parts of the Condition
Explaining the maxim that the conditioned is not apportioned over parts of the condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-mashrut la yatawazza' 'ala ajza' al-shart' to determine the wife's liability in conditional divorce proposals.

Core Idea
The conditioned does not divide over parts of the condition – a single condition cannot be apportioned.

This passage introduces a key Hanafi usul principle applied to divorce: the conditioned (mashrut) does not split across parts of the condition. When a wife offers a conditional divorce proposal, the word 'ala signals a condition, not an exchange. Imam Abu Hanifa and his two students differ on whether proportional payment is due if only part of the condition is fulfilled. Understanding this maxim clarifies when a wife owes nothing despite a partial divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the dispute
Understand the wife's proposal: 'Divorce me three times on condition of 1000 dirhams.' The husband divorces once. What, if anything, does she owe?
The dispute arises over the interpretation of 'ala.
2
State Imam Abu Hanifa's position and his proof
Imam holds that 'ala is a particle of condition (shart), not exchange. Since the conditioned (three divorces) is indivisible, and only one divorce occurred, the condition is not met. No payment is due.
His proof is the maxim and Quranic usage.
3
State the Sahibayn's alternative view
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad treat 'ala like 'ba' (an exchange particle). They argue that payment should be proportional to the divorces given.
Thus they would require 1/3 of 1000 dirhams.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response to the opposing view
Imam distinguishes 'ala from 'ba' based on linguistic evidence. The conditioned does not divide because the condition is a single unit.
This is a text-based linguistic argument.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The difference is whether the wife pays nothing (Imam) or 333 dirhams (Sahibayn).
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is Imam's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۹۵) إِنَّ الْمَشْرُوطَ لَا يَتَوَزَّعُ عَلَى أَجْزَاءِ الشَّرْطِ .
فِي قَوْلِهَا طَلَّقَنِي ثَلَاثًا عَلَى أَلْفٍ أَوْ عَلَى أَنَّ لَكَ عَلَى أَلْفًا فَطَلَّقَهَا وَاحِدَةً وَقَعَ رَجُعِيًّا بِغَيْرِ شَيْءٍ عَلَيْهَا عِنْدَ الْإِمَامِ خِلَافًا لَهُمَا فَهُمَا جَعَلَاهَا كَالْبَاءِ، وَهُوَ جَعَلَهَا لِلشَّرْطِ، وَالْمَشْرُوطُ لَا يَتَوَزَّعُ عَلَى أَجْزَاءِ الشَّرْطِ.
(95) The conditioned does not divide over parts of the condition. In her saying: 'Divorce me three times on condition of a thousand' or 'on condition that you have a thousand' and he divorced her once, it occurs as a revocable divorce without anything due from her according to Imam [Abu Hanifa], contrary to the two [Sahibayn]. They made it like 'ba', and he made it for condition, and the conditioned does not divide over parts of the condition.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute within the Hanafi school regarding the interpretation of the particle 'ala (on condition) in the wife's proposal. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, when the wife says 'Divorce me three times on condition of a thousand dirhams,' the word 'ala establishes a condition. The principle 'the conditioned does not divide over parts of the condition' means that the conditioned event (three divorces) is not apportioned over the condition (payment). Thus, if the husband divorces her only once, the condition is not fully met, so no payment is due, and a single revocable divorce occurs. The two students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, analogize 'ala to 'ba' (which indicates exchange), so they would require proportional payment. Imam's position is the relied-upon view in the school.

Key Terms
مشروط (mashrut): The conditioned thing; here, the three divorces that are dependent on the condition.
شرط (shart): The condition; here, the payment of a thousand dirhams.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a wife says 'Divorce me three times on condition of 1000 dirhams' and the husband divorces her once, what does she owe?
A) 333 dirhams
B) 1000 dirhams
C) Nothing
D) 500 dirhams

Key Takeaway
The conditioned does not divide over the parts of the condition: if the condition is not fully fulfilled, no obligation arises.

A Complete Sentence Is Independent
Explaining the maxim that a complete sentence is independent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim that a complete sentence is independent and apply it to determine whether a talaq utterance is self-contained or conditional.

Core Idea
A complete sentence is independent — it needs no external reference to be valid.

In Hanafi fiqh, the interpretation of utterances in divorce often hinges on whether the sentence is complete or incomplete. This lesson introduces the maxim 'al-asl fi al-jumlat al-tammat al-istiqlal' — the principle that a complete sentence is independent. Understanding this maxim helps determine when a condition is integral to the utterance and when the utterance requires external completion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim
Read the Arabic maxim and its English translation. Commit it to memory as a foundational rule.
The maxim is: 'al-asl fi al-jumlat al-tammat al-istiqlal'.
2
Define 'jumla tammah'
Understand that a jumla tammah is a complete sentence that conveys a full meaning independently.
In Arabic grammar, a complete sentence typically has subject and predicate.
3
Understand 'istiqlal' (independence)
Independence means the sentence does not require another sentence or external context to complete its legal meaning.
This is a default presumption; it can be overcome by evidence of dependence.
4
Apply to talaq utterances
Practice applying the maxim to a conditional divorce statement. Recognize that a complete conditional sentence is treated as independent.
Example: 'You are divorced if you enter the house' is a jumla tammah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۹۶) إِنَّ الْأَصْلَ فِي الْجُمْلَةِ التَّامَّةِ الاستقلال.
(96) The principle regarding a complete sentence is independence.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This is a concise legal maxim: a complete sentence (jumla tammah) is by default independent and does not require another sentence to complete its meaning. In the context of divorce, this applies to the interpretation of utterances. For example, if a wife says 'Divorce me three times on condition of a thousand,' the sentence is considered complete and the condition is part of its meaning; it does not need external reference. This maxim supports Imam Abu Hanifa's view that 'ala introduces a condition, making the sentence self-contained.

Key Terms
جملة تامة (jumla tammah): A complete sentence that conveys a full meaning independently.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A complete sentence in talaq is independent; conditions are integral, not external.

The Conditioned is Not Apportioned Over Parts of the Condition
Explaining the principle that a conditioned matter is not divided upon parts of the condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the mashrut is indivisible to cases of conditional divorce and distinguish between the interpretations of 'ala as shart vs mu'awada.

Core Idea
A condition is indivisible; if only part is met, the conditioned does not take effect partially.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi principle regarding conditional acts, specifically in divorce. When a wife says 'divorce me three times for a thousand dirhams' and the husband gives only one divorce, does she owe anything? The answer hinges on how we interpret the word 'ala. Imam Abu Hanifa holds it indicates a condition, not an exchange, leading to a distinct ruling compared to his two companions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core legal scenario
The wife says 'طلقني ثلاثًا على ألف' (divorce me three times for a thousand) and the husband divorces her once. Determine the legal effect.
Focus on the presence of 'ala and the partial fulfillment.
2
Understand the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Abu Hanifa interprets 'ala as a particle of condition (shart); the Sahibayn interpret it as exchange (mu'awada) like the 'ba'. Contrast the two readings.
This linguistic disagreement drives the difference in ruling.
3
Apply the principle 'al-mashrut la yatawazza'
If 'ala is a condition, the conditioned (three divorces) cannot be split over parts of the condition (the payment). Since only one divorce is given, the full condition (1000 for three divorces) is not met, so no payment is due.
The existence of a single divorce does not satisfy the condition for three.
4
Trace the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa: one revocable divorce, nothing owed. Under Sahibayn: one revocable divorce, one-third of the thousand owed (proportionate). Note which is mufta bihi.
The difference matters in financial liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَفِي عَلَى وَقَعَ رَجُعِيٌّ مَجَّانًا) أَى فِى قَوْلِهَا طَلَّقَنِي ثَلَاثًا عَلَى أَلْفٍ أَوْ عَلَى أَنَّ لَكَ عَلَى أَلْفًا فَطَلَّقَهَا وَاحِدَةً وَقَعَ رَجُعِيًّا بِغَيْرِ شَيْءٍ عَلَيْهَا عِنْدَ الْإِمَامِ خِلَافًا لَهُمَا فَهُمَا جَعَلَاهَا كَالْبَاءِ، وَهُوَ جَعَلَهَا لِلشَّرْطِ، وَالْمَشْرُوطُ لَا يَتَوَزَّعُ عَلَى أَجْزَاءِ الشَّرْطِ.
And regarding [the word] 'ala, it occurred as a revocable divorce gratis, meaning in her saying 'divorce me three times for a thousand' or 'on the condition that you owe me a thousand', so he divorced her once, it occurred as a revocable divorce without anything due from her according to Imam [Abu Hanifa], contrary to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), for they made it like the 'ba', and he made it for the condition, and the conditioned is not apportioned over parts of the condition.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof from the Hidayah regarding the principle 'al-mashrut la yatawazza' 'ala ajza' al-shart' (the conditioned is not apportioned over parts of the condition). The case is a wife saying to her husband 'divorce me three times in exchange for a thousand dirhams' and he gives her one divorce. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the one divorce is effective as a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) and nothing is due from the wife. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad disagree, considering the 'ala as equivalent to the 'ba' of exchange, thus requiring payment for the single divorce proportionally. Abu Hanifa holds that 'ala indicates a condition (shart), and a condition attaches to the action as a whole; it cannot be divided. Since only one part of the condition (the full price) is not met, the conditioned (three divorces) is not partially fulfilled. Hence only one revocable divorce falls without financial obligation.

Key Terms
المشروط (al-mashrut): The conditioned matter that is dependent on a condition
على ('ala): A particle that can indicate condition or exchange, depending on context

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A wife says to her husband: 'طلقني ثلاثًا على ألف درهم' and he gives one divorce. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling?
A) One revocable divorce occurs and she owes nothing.
B) One revocable divorce occurs and she owes one-third of the thousand.
C) Three divorces occur and she owes nothing.
D) No divorce occurs because the condition is not met.

Key Takeaway
The conditioned (mashrut) is indivisible; if only part of a condition (shart) is fulfilled, the conditioned does not take effect at all.

A Complete Sentence is Independent
Explaining that a complete sentence is independent and not linked to another without evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle of sentence independence and apply it to distinguish between linked and unlinked contractual phrases in talaq.

Core Idea
A complete sentence is legally independent; divorce and financial obligation are separate unless explicitly linked.

This passage from al-Hidayah introduces a key linguistic principle in Hanafi fiqh: a complete sentence (jumla tamma) is independent unless evidence shows connection. The principle is illustrated through a classic divorce scenario where the husband says 'You are divorced and you owe a thousand.' Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the divorce stands alone, with no financial obligation, while his two students (the Sahibayn) argue that acceptance by the wife creates a binding debt. Understanding this principle helps you analyze how contractual language is interpreted in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle and its scope
Locate the statement 'إِنَّ الْأَصْلَ فِي الْجُمْلَةِ التَّامَّةِ الاستقلال' (the original rule regarding a complete sentence is independence). This is the core legal maxim governing the example.
The principle applies to any complete sentence unless there is a connecting particle or contextual evidence.
2
Analyze the divorce example
Examine the husband's statement: 'أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ وَعَلَيْكِ أَلْفٌ' (You are divorced and you owe a thousand). Break it into two clauses: 'anti taliq' (complete sentence) and 'wa 'alayki alf' (another complete sentence joined by 'wa').
The conjunction 'wa' (and) does not by itself create a legal connection; it merely coordinates two independent statements.
3
Apply Abu Hanifa's reasoning
Abu Hanifa argues that since divorce can occur without consideration (unlike sale or lease), and there is no particle linking the two sentences, the second clause has no effect on the first. Divorce is valid; no debt arises.
Key distinction: in sale (bay') and lease (ijara), consideration is integral — they are inseparable from the obligation of money. Talaq is not.
4
Evaluate the Sahibayn's position
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that the phrase 'wa 'alayki alf' is commonly used in exchange contexts (e.g., 'do this and you have a dirham'), so if the wife accepts, she owes the thousand. They treat the statement as one conditional sentence.
Their position relies on 'urf (customary usage) rather than the strict linguistic principle of independence.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah states that Abu Hanifa's view is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi). The same ruling applies to emancipation with a condition of payment.
The preferred view: no obligation arises even if the wife accepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ الْأَصْلَ فِي الْجُمْلَةِ التَّامَّةِ الاستقلال. وَلَوْ قَالَ لَهَا: أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ وَعَلَيْكِ أَلْفٌ فَقَبِلَتْ طَلْقَتْ وَلَا شَيْءٍ عَلَيْهَا وَكَذَلِكَ إِنْ لَمْ تَقْبَلْ وَقَالَا: إِنْ قَبِلَتْ فَعَلَيْهَا الْأَلْفُ، وَإِلَّا لَا شَيْء عَلَيْهَا، لِأَنَّ هَذَا الْكَلَامَ يُسْتَعْمَلُ لِلْمُعَاوَضَةِ، يُقَالُ : اعْمَلْ هَذَا وَلَكَ دِرْهَمْ كَقَوْلِهِ بِدِرْهَم وَلَهُ أَنَّ قَوْلَهُ وَعَلَيْكِ أَلْفٌ لَا ارْتِبَاطَ لَهُ بِمَا قَبْلَهُ، إِذِ الْأَصْلُ ذَلِكَ وَلَا دَلَالَةَ عَلَى الارْتِبَاطِ، لأنَّ الطَّلَاقَ يُوجَدُ بِدُونِ الْمَالِ، بِخِلَافِ الْبَيْعِ وَالْإِجَارَةِ فَإِنَّهُمَا لَا يَنْفَكَّانِ عَنْ وُجُوبِ الْمَالِ ، وَلَوْ قَالَ لِعَبْدِهِ : أَنْتَ حُرِّ وَعَلَيْكَ أَلْفٌ فَعَلَى الْخِلَافِ.
Indeed the original rule regarding a complete sentence is independence. And if he said to her: 'You are divorced and you owe a thousand,' then she accepted, she is divorced and nothing is due from her. Similarly if she did not accept. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: If she accepted, the thousand is due from her; otherwise nothing is due from her because this expression is used for exchange. It is said: 'Do this and you have a dirham' similar to saying 'with a dirham.' And his (Abu Hanifa's) argument is that his statement 'and you owe a thousand' has no connection with what precedes it, because the original rule is that [a complete sentence is independent] and there is no evidence of connection, because divorce occurs without money, contrary to sale and lease, for they are not separable from the obligation of money. And if he said to his slave: 'You are free and you owe a thousand,' the same difference applies.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage presents the principle 'the original rule regarding a complete sentence is independence' (al-jumla al-tamma mustaqilla binafsiha). The example is a husband saying to his wife 'you are divorced and you owe a thousand' (anti taliq wa 'alayki alf). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the divorce takes effect regardless of acceptance, and she owes nothing, because 'anti taliq' is a complete sentence independent of the second sentence 'wa 'alayki alf'. There is no particle linking them to indicate a contractual exchange. Divorce can exist without consideration, unlike sale or lease. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that the phrase 'wa 'alayki alf' is used in exchange contexts, so if she accepts, she owes the thousand. But Abu Hanifa's view is preferred in the Hidayah. The same rule applies to emancipation with a condition of payment.

Key Terms
الجملة التامة (al-jumla al-tamma): A complete sentence that gives a full meaning independently
الاستقلال (al-istiqlal): Independence; not needing another sentence for completion
المعاوضة (al-mu'awada): Exchange; a transaction where consideration is given

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says to his wife: 'أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ وَعَلَيْكِ أَلْفٌ' and she accepts. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what happens?
A) She is divorced and owes nothing.
B) She is divorced and owes a thousand.
C) She is not divorced unless she pays.
D) It depends on her acceptance.

Key Takeaway
A complete sentence is legally independent; divorce and financial obligation are separate unless explicitly linked by a particle like 'bi' or 'ala'.

The Dual Nature of Khul': Sale for the Wife, Oath for the Husband
Definition of the dual nature of khul'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle on the dual nature of khul' and apply it to determine the legal consequences for each party.

Core Idea
Khul' is a sale for the wife and an oath for the husband, each with distinct legal effects.

This short but foundational principle from the Hidayah encapsulates the Hanafi view of khul' (divorce at the wife's request with compensation). The analogy to a sale on the woman's side explains why she must pay the compensation and can exercise options like khiyar al-shart. The analogy to an oath on the man's side explains why his pronouncement becomes immediately binding and subject to rules of oaths. Understanding this dual nature is key to grasping the entire Hanafi law of khul'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two analogies
The text says khul' is bi-manzilat al-bay' (like a sale) for the woman and bi-manzilat al-yamin (like an oath) for the man. Note each side separately.
These are not mere metaphors; they are legal analogies that determine applicable rulings.
2
Understand the rationale for each analogy
The woman gives compensation to obtain her freedom, resembling a buyer who pays a price. The man releases her with a pronouncement that becomes binding, like an oath that cannot be retracted once uttered.
Think of the consequences: as in a sale, the woman can stipulate conditions (khiyar); as in an oath, the man's word is final.
3
Recognize the legal implications
From the sale analogy: the wife's obligation to pay, the permissibility of khiyar al-shart, and the prohibition of najash (inflating compensation). From the oath analogy: the husband cannot revoke his acceptance, and any conditions he attaches are treated like conditions in oaths (e.g., if you pay by noon, the khul' is effective).
This principle is a key to deriving many furu' (detailed rulings) in the chapter of khul'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ الْخُلْعَ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْبَيْعِ فِي جَانِبِ الْمَرْأَةِ وَبِمَنْزِلَةِ الْيَمِينِ فِي جَانِبِ الرَّجُلِ.
Indeed khul' is like a sale on the woman's side and like an oath on the man's side.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This is a fundamental principle in Hanafi fiqh regarding khul' (divorce at the wife's request with compensation). The woman is akin to a seller because she gives compensation (usually part of the mahr or other property) to obtain her freedom. The husband is akin to one who swears an oath because his pronouncement of divorce is akin to fulfilling an oath; he cannot revoke it once accepted, and options like khiyar al-shart apply similarly to oaths. This principle guides rulings on conditions in khul', acceptance, and revocation.

Key Terms
الخلع (al-khul'): Divorce at the wife's request in exchange for compensation.
بمنزلة البيع (bi-manzilat al-bay'): Like a sale: the woman's act of paying compensation is akin to paying a price.
بمنزلة اليمين (bi-manzilat al-yamin): Like an oath: the husband's pronouncement becomes binding like an oath; he cannot take it back.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A lease
B) A sale
C) An oath
D) A gift

Key Takeaway
Khul' is legally analyzed as a sale from the wife's perspective and an oath from the husband's perspective, determining the rights and obligations of each party.

Divorce with a Monetary Condition: The Disconnected Sentence
Explains the ruling when divorce is pronounced with a financial demand

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute on divorce with a monetary condition and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Divorce and money are disconnected sentences unless a clear link is proven.

When a husband says 'You are divorced and upon you is a thousand,' does the wife owe money if she accepts? This lesson unpacks the internal Hanafi disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. The ruling turns on whether the two clauses are connected or separate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the utterance
The husband says: 'You are divorced and upon you is a thousand.' Note that this is a single utterance with two parts.
The conjunction 'and' (wa) does not inherently create a condition.
2
State the default rule for complete sentences
In Hanafi usul, a complete sentence (jumla tammah) is independent. It does not connect to another sentence without evidence.
3
Apply the rule to the first clause
The phrase 'You are divorced' is a complete sentence. It stands alone. The divorce takes effect immediately upon utterance.
No acceptance is needed for the divorce to occur.
4
Analyse the second clause
The phrase 'upon you is a thousand' is another complete sentence. It has no grammatical link to the first. Therefore, no money is owed unless a connection is proven.
5
Examine the Sahibayn's objection
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that such speech is customarily used for exchange (mu'awadah), so if the wife accepts, she owes the money. Assess which side has stronger evidence.
The author of Al-Ikhtiyar sides with Abu Hanifa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ناقص کہلائے گا، تو جملہ تامہ کے لئے ضروری ہے کہ وہ اپنے مفہوم میں غیر کا محتاج نہ ہو۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر شوہر نے اپنی بیوی سے یہ کہا انتِ طَالِقٌ وَعَلَيْكِ ألف “ کہ تجھے طلاق ہے اور تجھ پر ہزار لا زم ہے، اب بیوی قبول کرے یا نہ کرے طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی اور بیوی پر کچھ بھی لازم نہ ہوگا ، اس لئے کہ انتِ طَالِق “یہ جملہ تامہ ہے اس کا مابعد سے کچھ تعلق نہیں ہے اور جملہ تامہ میں اصل یہ ہے کہ وہ مستقل ہو، اور عَلَيْكِ ألق “ مبتدا خبر ہے اور جو دوسرا جملہ تامہ ہے، اس کا ماقبل انتِ طَالِق “ سے کوئی تعلق ہی نہیں جملہ تامہ دلیل کے بغیر ماقبل سے مربوط نہیں ہوتا اور یہاں ماقبل سے مربوط ہونے کی کوئی دلیل نہیں ہے، اس لئے کہ طلاق و عتاق دونوں مال سے جدا ہوتے ہیں ، اس لئے بیوی نے یا غلام نے مال دینے کو قبول نہ کیا ہو تو ان پر کچھ واجب نہ ہوگا۔
(وَلَوْ قَالَ لَهَا: أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ وَعَلَيْكِ أَلْفٌ فَقَبِلَتْ طَلْقَتْ وَلَا شَيْءٍ عَلَيْهَا وَكَذَلِكَ إِنْ لَمْ تَقْبَلْ وَقَالَا: إِنْ قَبِلَتْ فَعَلَيْهَا الْأَلْفُ، وَإِلَّا لَا شَيْء عَلَيْهَا، لِأَنَّ هَذَا الْكَلَامَ يُسْتَعْمَلُ لِلْمُعَاوَضَةِ، يُقَالُ : اعْمَلْ هَذَا وَلَكَ دِرْهَمْ كَقَوْلِهِ بِدِرْهَم وَلَهُ أَنَّ قَوْلَهُ وَعَلَيْكِ أَلْفٌ لَا ارْتِبَاطَ لَهُ بِمَا قَبْلَهُ، إِذِ الْأَصْلُ ذَلِكَ وَلَا دَلَالَةَ عَلَى الارْتِبَاطِ، لأنَّ الطَّلَاقَ يُوجَدُ بِدُونِ الْمَالِ، بِخِلَافِ الْبَيْعِ وَالْإِجَارَةِ فَإِنَّهُمَا لَا يَنْفَكَّانِ عَنْ وُجُوبِ الْمَالِ ، وَلَوْ قَالَ لِعَبْدِهِ : أَنْتَ حُرِّ وَعَلَيْكَ أَلْفٌ فَعَلَى الْخِلَافِ. الاختيار لتعليل المختار: كتاب الطلاق، باب الخلع، ج ۳ ص ۱۵۹، ط: مطبعة المجلس القاهرة الهداية: كتاب الطلاق، باب الخلع ج ۲ ص ۴۱۶، ط: رحمانیه
It will be called incomplete. So for a complete sentence, it is necessary that it does not need others in its meaning. The author of Hidayah, after mentioning the above principle, is stating that if a husband said to his wife: 'You are divorced and upon you is a thousand' meaning you are divorced and you owe a thousand, now whether the wife accepts or not, divorce will occur and nothing will be obligatory upon the wife, because 'You are divorced' is a complete sentence; it has no connection to what follows. And in a complete sentence, the principle is that it is independent. And 'upon you a thousand' is a subject and predicate, and it is another complete sentence; it has no connection to the preceding 'You are divorced'. A complete sentence is not connected to the preceding without a proof, and here there is no proof of connection, because divorce and manumission are both separate from wealth. Therefore, if the wife or the slave did not accept giving the wealth, nothing will be obligatory upon them. (And if he said to her: 'You are divorced and upon you is a thousand,' and she accepted, she is divorced and nothing is upon her, and likewise if she did not accept. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: If she accepts, then upon her is the thousand; otherwise nothing upon her, because this speech is used for exchange; it is said: 'Do this and you have a dirham' like his saying 'with a dirham'. And his (Abu Hanifa's) argument is that his statement 'and upon you is a thousand' has no connection to what is before it, because the principle is that, and there is no indication of connection, because divorce occurs without wealth, unlike sale and lease, for they are inseparable from the obligation of wealth. And if he said to his slave: 'You are free and upon you is a thousand,' then it is based on the difference. Al-Ikhtiyar li Ta'lil al-Mukhtar: Book of Divorce, Chapter of Khul', vol. 3, p. 159, Cairo Council Press; Al-Hidayah: Book of Divorce, Chapter of Khul', vol. 2, p. 416, Rahmaniyyah Press.)
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of pronouncing divorce together with a financial condition. The author begins by explaining the grammatical principle of a complete sentence (جملة تامہ) — a sentence that is independent in meaning. The ruling is: if a husband says 'You are divorced and upon you is a thousand,' the divorce takes effect immediately upon utterance, regardless of acceptance, and no wealth becomes obligatory on the wife. The reason is that 'You are divorced' is a complete sentence with no grammatical or legal link to the financial demand. The second part 'upon you a thousand' is an independent sentence. Without a clear indication (قرینہ) connecting them, they remain separate. The author then cites the Arabic text from Al-Ikhtiyar, which presents the view of Imam Abu Hanifa (the author of the passage: 'wa lahu' refers to Abu Hanifa) and the view of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). According to Abu Hanifa, the divorce occurs but no money is owed unless the wife accepts; in fact, even if she accepts, no money is owed according to the stronger view because the sentence remains disconnected. The Sahibayn argue that this speech is customarily used for exchange (معاوضہ), similar to saying 'Do this and you have a dirham,' and thus if she accepts, she owes the thousand. Abu Hanifa's proof is that divorce is valid without consideration, unlike sale and lease, which are inseparable from wealth. The analogy with slave manumission ('You are free and upon you a thousand') follows the same difference. The passage concludes with references to the Ikhtiyar and Hidayah. The key point for the student is that in Hanafi law, the mere conjunction of a divorce statement with a financial demand does not create a binding obligation without a specific intent or acceptance; the default is that divorce is unconditional.

Key Terms
جملہ تامہ (jumla tammah): A complete sentence that is independent in meaning and does not need another sentence for completion.
معاوضہ (mu'awadah): Exchange contract where two obligations (property and countervalue) are linked.
خلع (khul'): Divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for compensation paid to the husband.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a husband says to his wife 'You are divorced and upon you is a thousand' and she does not accept?
A) Divorce does not occur because she did not accept.
B) Divorce occurs but she owes nothing.
C) Divorce occurs and she owes the thousand.
D) Nothing occurs at all.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a divorce utterance and a financial demand in the same sentence are treated as independent unless a clear link is established, so no money is owed even upon acceptance.

Talaq — Divorce

The Dual Nature of Khul': Option for Her, Not for Him
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the validity of stipulated options in khul'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the principle that khul' is like a sale for the woman and like an oath for the man, and apply it to determine the validity of stipulated options (khiyar al-shart) for each party.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, khul' is a hybrid: a sale for the woman (allowing options) and an oath for the man (forbidding options).

You know that khul' allows a wife to obtain a divorce in exchange for compensation. But how does the law treat conditions attached to this transaction? The Hanafi school distinguishes sharply between the husband's and wife's positions. This lesson unpacks the foundational principle that khul' is like a sale from her side and like an oath from his side, and shows how this determines the validity of a stipulated option (khiyar al-shart). We will examine the intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on this issue.

Guided Path
1
State the Principle
Identify the core legal maxim: 'Khul' is like a sale from the woman's side and like an oath from the man's side.' Understand that this classifies the contract differently for each party, carrying distinct implications.
This principle is the key to deriving all branch rulings in this lesson.
2
Analyze the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Recognize that Abu Hanifa holds the option valid for the woman and invalid for the man, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold it invalid for both. Identify each side's basis: Abu Hanifa emphasizes the sale nature for her and oath nature for him; the Sahibayn consider the oath side dominant for both.
The dispute is over which aspect (sale or oath) is stronger when the contract involves both elements.
3
Apply to the Husband's Option Scenario
Work through the case where the husband says 'You are divorced for a thousand on condition that I have the option for three days.' According to Abu Hanifa, the option is void because the husband's side is an oath, which does not admit options. The divorce occurs immediately and compensation is due.
The condition does not invalidate the divorce; it is simply nullified.
4
Apply to the Wife's Option Scenario
Now consider the husband saying 'You are divorced for a thousand on condition that you have the option for three days.' According to Abu Hanifa, this is valid: the wife can choose within three days to accept or reject the divorce. If she rejects, divorce is void; if she accepts, it becomes binding.
This mirrors the rules of a sale, where a buyer may have a cooling-off period.
5
Identify the Practical Consequence (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa, the wife has control: she can cancel the divorce during the option period. Under the Sahibayn, she has no such right; the divorce is binding immediately. This difference directly affects the outcome of a khul' agreement.
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that of Imam Abu Hanifa, as declared in the text.

Source Text
