Nikah Does Not Admit Faskh After Completion
Ruling that marriage contract cannot be annulled after valid completion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that a validly completed marriage cannot be annulled (faskh) and explain that separation due to impotence results in talaq ba'in.

Core Idea
A marriage contract that is fully completed cannot be annulled — separation can only be through talaq.

This passage establishes a foundational rule in Hanafi marriage law: once a marriage contract is validly concluded, it cannot be undone by annulment (faskh). This ruling has important practical implications, particularly when a wife seeks separation due to her husband's impotence. The Hanafi school holds that such separation is considered talaq ba'in, not faskh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Extract the core statement: marriage does not admit faskh after the contract is fully completed. This is the key legal principle.
The Arabic phrase 'لا يقبل الفسخ عندنا' is emphatic.
2
Define 'After Completion' (Tamam al-Aqd)
Understand that completion means the contract is fully concluded with all conditions and pillars satisfied, and no outstanding options (like khiyar) that could prevent finality.
Options that prevent completion include khiyar al-bulugh and khiyar al-'itaqa.
3
Recognize Exceptions Before Completion
Before the contract is completed, faskh is possible. Examples given are khiyar al-bulugh (option of puberty for a minor) and khiyar al-'itaqa (option of emancipation for a slave). These prevent the contract from reaching completion.
These options give a party the right to annul before the contract becomes binding.
4
Apply the Principle to Impotence
When a wife seeks separation due to her husband's impotence, the separation is not faskh but talaq ba'in. This is because the marriage is already complete; annulment is not an option.
Talaq ba'in is an irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage permanently.
5
Understand the Rationale
The objective is to remove injustice from the wife. If the separation were not ba'in, she would be left in a suspended state—neither married nor divorced—which would not achieve justice.
The passage says: 'إذا لم تكن بائنة تعود معلقة بالمراجعة'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ النِّكَاحَ لَا يَقْبَلُ الْفَسْخَ عِنْدَنَا بَعْدَ تَمَامِ الْعَقْدِ. لكن النكاح لا يقبل الفسخ عندنا يعني بعد تمام العقد، أما قبل تمام العقد فيقبل ذلك كما في خيار البلوغ وخيار العتاقة، لأن ذلك امتناع من تمام العقد، وإنما تقع): أى الفرقة (بائنة، لأن المقصود وهو رفع الظلم عنها لا يحصل إلا بهاى بالبائنة (لأنها): أى لأن الفرقة (لو لم تكن بائنة تعود معلقة بالمراجعة) وهى التى لا تكون ذات زوج ولا مطلقة. أما الأول فلفوات المقصود، وهو الوطئ وأما الثانى فلأنها تحت زوج فلا يحصل حينئذ رفع الظلم وهو المقصود من فرقة العنين.
Indeed, marriage does not admit annulment according to us after the completion of the contract. But marriage does not admit annulment according to us, meaning after completion of the contract. As for before completion of the contract, it admits that, as in the option of puberty and the option of emancipation, because that is a refusal from completing the contract. And it (i.e., the separation) occurs as ba'in, because the objective, which is removing injustice from her, is not achieved except by ba'in. Because the separation, if it were not ba'in, would return suspended on reconciliation, and she would be neither a wife nor a divorced woman. As for the first, due to loss of the objective, which is intercourse; and as for the second, because she is under a husband, so removal of injustice, which is the objective of separation for an impotent man, would not be achieved.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage states a key Hanafi principle: a marriage contract that has been validly completed cannot be annulled (faskh). Any separation after that point is considered talaq (divorce), not faskh. The principle is illustrated with the case of a husband who is impotent ('anin). When the wife seeks separation due to her husband's impotence, the separation is not faskh but talaq ba'in (irrevocable divorce). This is because faskh would retroactively annul the marriage, which is not permissible after completion. The ruling ensures that the wife is free to remarry (since talaq ba'in ends the marriage permanently) and does not remain suspended without being a wife or a divorced woman. Before the contract is complete, however, faskh is possible, e.g., when a minor who was married by someone else reaches puberty (khiyar al-bulugh) or a slave is emancipated (khiyar al-'itaqa) – these prevent the contract from being completed.

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): Annulment of a contract retroactively, as if it never occurred.
تمام العقد (tamam al-aqd): The completion of a contract, meaning all conditions and pillars are satisfied without any ongoing options to prevent finality.
طلاق بائن (talaq ba'in): An irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage immediately, without right of return during iddah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is separation due to impotence considered talaq ba'in rather than faskh in the Hanafi school?
A) Because the husband's impotence nullifies the marriage retroactively.
B) Because marriage after completion cannot admit faskh.
C) Because the wife has the right to divorce.
D) Because the judge must decide.

Key Takeaway
After valid completion, a marriage contract cannot be annulled (faskh); separation due to defects like impotence results in talaq ba'in.

The Principle of Not Combining Substitute and Substituted in 'Iddah
Ruling statement establishing the maxim of non-combination

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim 'al-jam' bayn al-badal wa al-mubdal la yajuz' and apply it to 'iddah cases, identifying when a woman must switch from counting months to counting menstruation.

Core Idea
A substitute cannot be combined with the original; when the original becomes possible, the substitute becomes invalid.

This lesson presents a foundational maxim in Hanafi fiqh: you cannot simultaneously rely on a substitute and its original. In 'iddah, this rule determines whether a woman observes her waiting period by months or by menstrual cycles. Understanding this principle prevents errors in 'iddah calculation for women whose status changes during the waiting period.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Maxim and Its Terms
Read the maxim: 'al-jam' bayn al-badal wa al-mubdal la yajuz' (combining the substitute and the substituted is not permissible). Identify 'badal' (substitute) and 'mubdal' (the original). In 'iddah, the substitute is months (ashhur) for a woman who has reached menopause (a'isah), and the original is menstrual cycles (hayd).
The maxim is a universal principle in Hanafi law, not limited to 'iddah.
2
Understand the Context of 'Iddah
Recall the default 'iddah for a non-pregnant, menstruating woman: three menstrual cycles (quru'). If a woman is a'isah (past menopause/hopeless of menstruation), the 'iddah becomes three months. Months are the substitute (badal) for hayd.
This substitution is based on Quran 65:4.
3
Apply the Scenario: When Hayd Appears During Monthly 'Iddah
Consider a woman who begins 'iddah by months because she is a'isah. During these months, she experiences a menstrual period. According to the maxim, she cannot combine the months already counted (badal) with this new hayd (the original). The original (hayd) has appeared, so the substitute (months) becomes invalid.
She must now observe 'iddah by hayd for that cycle. The months counted before are disregarded.
4
Derive the Ruling for Continuation
After the hayd episode, if she becomes a'isah again (no further menstruation), she must return to counting months from the beginning? The commentary clarifies: The appearance of hayd invalidates the entire substitute period. She must start a fresh 'iddah by months only if it is confirmed that she has returned to the state of being a'isah. However, if she menstruates only once and then stops, she completes the 'iddah by the remaining months? Actually, the ruling is that she switches to hayd for that cycle, and the months counted before are invalid. The 'iddah then continues by hayd until three cycles are completed, or if she returns to a'isah status, she may again switch to months. The precise details are in longer texts; here we focus on the maxim.
The principle prevents any combination of the two methods in the same 'iddah.
5
Note the Practical Consequence
If a woman attempts to combine by counting both months and hayd, her 'iddah is invalid. She must restart the 'iddah correctly. This maxim protects the integrity of the waiting period.
This ruling is universally agreed upon among Hanafis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ الْجَمْعَ بَيْنَ الْبَدَلِ وَالْمُبْدَلِ لَا يَجُوزُ.
Indeed, combining the substitute and the substituted is not permissible.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage provides a key legal maxim: a substitute (badal) and the original substituted item (mubdal) cannot be combined. In the context of 'iddah, the principle is applied to a woman who has reached menopause (a'isah). For such a woman, the original 'iddah would be by menstrual cycles (hayd), but since she no longer menstruates, months (ashhur) become the substitute. If during her 'iddah by months she suddenly experiences one or two periods and then becomes hopeless of menstruation again, she cannot combine both the months already counted and the newly occurring hayd. Instead, she must revert to counting by months from the beginning? The commentary clarifies that if she was counting by months as a substitute (badal) for hayd, and then hayd occurs, the substitute becomes invalid because the original (hayd) has appeared. She must then observe 'iddah by hayd for those periods, but if she later becomes a'isah again, she must return to months? The full ruling from Al-Hidayah (on page 429) clarifies: if a woman in 'iddah by months experiences hayd, she must switch to hayd for that cycle; the months counted before are not valid because the substitute (ashhur) is no longer applicable when the original (hayd) is possible. This illustrates that combining the two is invalid.

Key Terms
بدل (badal): Substitute; in 'iddah, months (ashhur) are the substitute for menstrual cycles (hayd) for a woman who has reached menopause.
مبدل (mubdal): The original item being substituted; in 'iddah, it is hayd (menstrual cycles).
عدة (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband before remarriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A woman in 'iddah by months suddenly menstruates. According to the principle 'al-jam' bayn al-badal wa al-mubdal la yajuz', what should she do?
A) Continue counting months and add the hayd period.
B) Switch to 'iddah by hayd and disregard the months counted.
C) Count both months and hayd together.
D) Stop 'iddah entirely.

Key Takeaway
Combining a substitute and its original in 'iddah is invalid; when the original appears, the substitute is voided.

intermediate
8 min
The Principle of Not Combining the Substitute and the Substituted in Hanafi Fiqh
States the principle of not combining badal and mubdal with examples from 'iddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that badal and mubdal cannot be combined and apply it to cases where a woman's condition changes during 'iddah.

Core Idea
When the original becomes available, the substitute automatically becomes void — they cannot coexist.

The principle that a substitute (badal) and the original (mubdal) cannot be combined is a key rule in Hanafi fiqh, especially in matters of 'ibadat and family law. This passage from Al-Hidayah illustrates it through the 'iddah of a woman who switches from months to menses. By understanding this principle, you will be able to resolve similar cases where a replacement ruling is superseded by the original.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
Extract the core legal maxim: 'The substitute and the original cannot be combined.' This is stated explicitly in the text.
2
Understand the analogy
The author uses the well-known example of wudu and tayammum: when water (the original) is available, tayammum (the substitute) is invalid. This illustrates the rule that the substitute is void when the original is present.
3
Apply to 'iddah
Consider a menopausal woman (ayisah) observing 'iddah by months (the substitute for menses). If she begins menstruating, the months already counted are nullified because menses (the original) has appeared.
4
Note the consequence
The 'iddah must restart based on the original — three complete menstrual cycles. The past months are not added; they are entirely void.
5
Recognize the broader application
This principle is a clear analogy (qiyas jaliy) used across Hanafi fiqh, not only in 'iddah but also in purification and other acts where a substitute is employed when the original is inaccessible.

فإن كانت تعتد بالشهور لصغر أو إياس فحاضت انتقض ما مضى من عدتها بالشهور وكان عليها ثلاث حيض أما في الآيسة فظاهر لأنها لما حاضت تبين أنها لم تكن آيسة وإنما كانت ممتدا طهرها وأما في الصغيرة إذا حاضت فإنها قدرت على الأصل قبل حصول المقصود بالبدل والقدرة على الأصل تمنع اعتبار البدل ولا يكمل مع الأصل لأنهما لا يلتقيان فلا بد من الاستئناف.

الهداية: كتاب الطلاق، باب العدة، ج ۲ ص ۴۳۰، ط: رحمانیه
The woman will remain hanging and the objective will not be achieved. In the principle, this restriction is that after completion of the contract it is known that before completion it accepts the dissolution, as in the case of khiyar al-bulugh and khiyar al-'itaqah. Chapter of 'Iddah. Indeed, combining the substitute and the substituted is not permissible. Translation: Combining the substitute and the substituted is not permissible. Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that combining the substitute and the substituted is invalid, i.e., it is not correct to combine both at the same time because when the original comes, the substitute is not needed; rather, the substitute automatically becomes void. The author of Al-Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that if a woman in 'iddah who had despaired of menstruation was completing her 'iddah by months, then she menstruates once or twice, then despairs again, she will now complete her 'iddah by months because for a woman who has despaired of menstruation, months are the substitute for menstruation. If menstruation were to be considered in her 'iddah, then it would necessitate combining the substitute and the substituted, which is not permissible, just as combining wudu and tayammum is not correct. But marriage does not accept revocation according to us, meaning after the completion of the contract; before completion it accepts it, as in khiyar al-bulugh and khiyar al-'itaqah, because that is abstention from completing the contract. And the separation is irrevocable (ba'in) because the objective, which is removing injustice from her, is not achieved except by an irrevocable divorce. Because if the separation were not irrevocable, she would remain suspended with the possibility of return, and she would be neither a wife nor divorced. The first is due to loss of the objective, which is intercourse, and the second is because she is under a husband, so removing injustice is not achieved, which is the objective of annulment for impotence. Al-Binayah: Kitab al-Talaq, Bab al-'Inan, etc., vol. 5, p. 585, Dar al-Kutub al-'Ilmiyyah. Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Talaq, Bab al-'Iddah, vol. 2, p. 429, Rahmaniyyah. If she was observing 'iddah by months due to youth or menopause and then menstruated, what has passed of her 'iddah by months is nullified, and she must observe three menstruations. As for the menopausal woman, it is clear because when she menstruates, it becomes apparent that she was not menopausal, but rather her purity was prolonged. As for the young girl, when she menstruates, she becomes capable of the original before achieving the objective by the substitute, and the ability of the original prevents consideration of the substitute, and it is not completed with the original because they do not meet, so she must start anew. Al-Mabsut li al-Sarakhsi: Kitab al-Talaq, Bab al-Raj'ah, vol. 6, p. 27, Dar al-Ma'rifah. Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Talaq, Bab al-'Iddah, vol. 2, p. 430, Rahmaniyyah.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof from Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The text presents the principle that the substitute (badal) and the original (mubdal) cannot coexist. It then applies this to the 'iddah of a woman who was observing 'iddah by months (as a substitute for menses) but then experienced menses. The principle is illustrated with the analogy of wudu and tayammum: when water (original) is available, tayammum (substitute) is invalid. Similarly, during 'iddah, if a woman who was on months-based 'iddah due to menopause or youth begins menstruating, she must switch to menstrual 'iddah, nullifying the months already counted. The text also includes a discussion from Al-Hidayah and Al-Binayah about the case of an impotent husband, where the marriage cannot be dissolved after completion of the contract, but before completion it can, analogously to khiyar al-bulugh and khiyar al-'itaqah. This shows the consistency of the principle: substitution is only valid when the original is inaccessible.

Key Terms
بدل (badal): Substitute that stands in place of the original ruling or act when the original is impossible.
مبدل (mubdal): The original ruling or act that the substitute replaces; the primary act.
آئسہ (ayisah): A woman who has despaired of menstruation due to old age.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the principle in this passage, what happens when a woman observing 'iddah by months due to menopause begins menstruating?
A) She continues the months-based 'iddah and adds the menses.
B) The months already counted are nullified, and she begins a new 'iddah of three menses.
C) She completes the months and then observes three menses.
D) Her 'iddah is complete with a single menses.

Key Takeaway
When the original becomes accessible, the substitute becomes void; they cannot be combined in any circumstance.

Purpose of 'Iddah and the Rule of Tadakhul
Ruling with reasoning explaining the purpose of 'iddah and tadakhul

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the purpose of 'iddah according to the Hanafi school and apply the rule of tadakhul to scenarios involving multiple causes of 'iddah.

Core Idea
The purpose of 'iddah is to clear the womb; once achieved, a second 'iddah interpenetrates.

This text from Al-Hidayah and its commentaries establishes a fundamental principle of Hanafi family law: 'iddah serves a single purpose—to determine that the womb is empty. This principle has a powerful practical consequence: if a woman in 'iddah is approached under shubhah, she does not need to double her waiting period. Instead, the two 'iddahs interpenetrate. The following lesson unpacks this reasoning and shows how leading Hanafi authorities applied it.

Guided Path
1
State the principle
Identify the purpose of 'iddah as stated in the text: to know the emptiness of the womb (istibra' al-rahim).
This is the central legal rationale (ta'lil) that guides all related rulings.
2
Identify the application scenario
Consider a woman already in 'iddah (e.g., from divorce) who is then approached under shubhah by another man. According to the text, a second 'iddah becomes obligatory due to the new cause.
Shubhah means a mistaken belief that intercourse is lawful.
3
Trace the reasoning for tadakhul
Since both 'iddahs aim for the same purpose (clearing the womb), and one 'iddah achieves that purpose, the two 'iddahs interpenetrate. The woman does not need to observe two full separate periods.
The specific ruling: if she menstruates once after the second intercourse, she needs only two more menses to complete both 'iddahs.
4
Examine the nuance on substitute 'iddahs
If the woman's 'iddah was originally based on months (due to youth or menopause) and she then menstruates, the substitute (months) is abandoned and she must start a new 'iddah of three menses. The original (menses) takes precedence once possible.
This underscores that the purpose (menses) is primary; months are only a secondary means.
5
Note the textual evidence
The passage quotes Al-Hidayah, Al-Mabsut, and Al-Ikhtiyar to support the principle and its applications. Each text reaffirms that the purpose governs the ruling.
Al-Hidayah states the principle, Al-Mabsut expands on the substitution rule, and Al-Ikhtiyar details the counting of menses in tadakhul.

ترجمہ: عدت سے مقصو د رحم کی فراغت کو بتلانا ہے۔
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ عورت پر جو عدت ہوتی ہے اس سے مقصود یہ ہوتا ہے کہ عورت کے رحم کی براءت اور فراغت کو معلوم کیا جائے۔
صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلارہے ہیں کہ اگر معتدہ عورت کے ساتھ شبہ کی بنیاد پر وطی ہو جائے تو اب اس پر ایک اور عدت واجب ہوگی ، البتہ دونوں عدتوں میں تداخل ہوگا کیونکہ عدت سے مقصود فراغت رحم کو بتلانا ہے، اور یہ مقصود ایک عدت سے حاصل ہو جاتا ہے، اس لئے بلا وجہ دو عدتیں واجب کر کے عورت کے انتظار میں اضافہ نہیں کیا جائے گا اور دونوں عدتوں میں تداخل مانا جائے گا۔
فَإِنْ كَانَتْ تَعْتَدُّ بِالشُّهُورِ لِصِغَرِ أَوْ إِيَاسِ فَحَاضَتْ انْتَقِضَ مَا مَضَى مِنْ عِدَّتِهَا بِالشُّهُورِ وَكَانَ عَلَيْهَا ثَلاتُ حِيَضِ أَمَّا فِى الْآيِسَةِ فَظَاهِرٌ لأَنَّهَا لَمَّا حَاضَتْ تَبَيَّنَ أَنَّهَا لَمْ تَكُنُ آيَسَةً وَإِنَّمَا كَانَتْ مُمْتَدًّا طُهُرُهَا وَأَمَّنا فِي الصَّغِيرَةِ إِذَا حَاضَتْ فَلَّانَّهَا قَدَرَتْ عَلَى الْأَصْلِ قَبْلَ حُصُولِ الْمَقْصُودِ بِالْبَدَلِ وَالْقُدْرَةُ عَلَى الْأَصْلِ تَمْنَعُ اعْتِبَارَ الْبَدَلِ وَلَا يَكْمُلُ مَعَ الْأَصْلِ لِأَنَّهُمَا لَا يَلْتَقِيَان فَلا بُدَّ مِنْ الاسْتِثْنَافِ.
المبسوط للسرخسى كتاب الطلاق، باب الرجعة، ج ۶ ص ۲۷، ط: دار المعرفة
الهداية: كتاب الطلاق، باب العدة، ج ۲ ص ۴۳۰، ط: رحمانیه
(وَإِذَا وُطِئَتِ الْمُعْتَدَّةُ بِشُبُهَةٍ فَعَلَيْهَا عِدَّةٌ أُخْرَى) لِوُجُودِ السَّبَبِ وَيَتَدَاخَلَانِ، فَإِنْ حَاضَتْ حَيْضَةً ثُمَّ وُطِئَتْ كَمَلَتُهَا بِثَلَاثٍ أَخَرَ ، وَتُحْسَبُ حَيْضَتَانِ مِنَ الْعِدَّتَيْنِ وَتُكَمَّلُ الْأُولَى وَالثَّالِثَةُ تَيَمَّةُ لِلطَّانِيَةِ، لأنَّ الْمَقْصُودَ مِنَ الْعِدَّةِ التَّعَرُفْ عَنْ بَرَاءَةِ الرَّحِمِ، وَأَنَّهُ حَاصِلْ بِالْعِدَّةِ الْوَاحِدَةِ لِأَنَّهُ لَا بُدَّ مِنْ ثَلَاثِ حِيَضِ بَعْدَ الْوَطْئِيِ الثَّانِي وَبِهِ تُتَعَرَّفُ بَرَاءَةُ الرَّحِمِ، وَلِلثَّانِي أَنْ يَتَزَوَّجَهَا بَعْدَ اسْتِكْمَال الْأُولَى لِأَنَّهَا فِي عِدَّتِهِ. الاختيار لتعليل المختار كتاب الطلاق، باب العدة، ج ۳ ص ۱۷۵، ط: مطبعة المجلس القاهرة
(11) Indeed, the purpose of 'iddah is to know the emptiness of the womb. Translation: The purpose of 'iddah is to inform of the emptiness of the womb. Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that the 'iddah observed by a woman aims to know the clearance and emptiness of her womb. The author of Al-Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that if a woman in 'iddah is approached under shubhah (mistake), then another 'iddah becomes obligatory upon her, but the two 'iddahs will interpenetrate because the purpose of 'iddah is to inform of the emptiness of the womb, and this purpose is achieved with one 'iddah. Therefore, two 'iddahs will not be made obligatory without cause, increasing the woman's waiting period, and interpenetration will be accepted. If she was observing 'iddah by months due to youth or menopause and then menstruated, what has passed of her 'iddah by months is nullified, and she must observe three menstruations. As for the menopausal woman, it is clear because when she menstruates, it becomes apparent that she was not menopausal, but rather her purity was prolonged. As for the young girl, when she menstruates, she becomes capable of the original before achieving the objective by the substitute, and the ability of the original prevents consideration of the substitute, and it is not completed with the original because they do not meet, so she must start anew. Al-Mabsut li al-Sarakhsi: Kitab al-Talaq, Bab al-Raj'ah, vol. 6, p. 27, Dar al-Ma'rifah. Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Talaq, Bab al-'Iddah, vol. 2, p. 430, Rahmaniyyah. (And if a woman in 'iddah is approached under shubhah, then another 'iddah is obligatory upon her) due to the existence of the cause, and they interpenetrate. If she menstruates once and then is approached, she completes it with three more; two menstruations are counted from both 'iddahs, and the first and third complete the second. Because the purpose of 'iddah is to know the clearance of the womb, and that is achieved with one 'iddah, because three menses are necessary after the second intercourse, and by it the clearance of the womb is known. And the second man may marry her after the completion of the first because she is in his 'iddah. Al-Ikhtiyar li Ta'lil al-Mukhtar: Kitab al-Talaq, Bab al-'Iddah, vol. 3, p. 175, Matba'ah al-Majlis al-Qahirah.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage presents a rule from Hanafi fiqh regarding the purpose of 'iddah. The text begins by stating the principle: the purpose of 'iddah is to ascertain that the womb is empty of pregnancy. This purpose is central to all 'iddah rulings. The author then applies this to a scenario: if a woman already in 'iddah is approached under shubhah (e.g., a man mistakenly believes she is free to marry), she must observe another 'iddah. However, the two 'iddahs interpenetrate (tadakhul) because the goal of determining the emptiness of the womb is already being achieved through the first 'iddah. The text then quotes from Al-Mabsut and Al-Hidayah to illustrate a related point: if a woman on months-based 'iddah due to menopause or youth starts menstruating, her months count is nullified, and she must observe a new 'iddah of three menses. This is because menses is the original, and months are the substitute. Finally, the passage from Al-Ikhtiyar clarifies that when a woman in 'iddah is approached under shubhah, she must complete a new 'iddah of three menses from that intercourse, but the menses already counted from the first 'iddah count towards the second if they occurred after the second intercourse. This is all based on the purpose of 'iddah being cleared of the womb.

Key Terms
عدة ('iddah): The waiting period observed by a woman after divorce or death of husband to ensure the womb is free of pregnancy.
تداخل (tadakhul): Interpenetration of two 'iddahs, where one waiting period satisfies both obligations.
شبہ (shubhah): A mistaken belief that intercourse is permissible, leading to a valid but mistaken act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens when a woman in 'iddah is approached under shubhah?
A) She must observe two complete, separate 'iddahs sequentially.
B) The two 'iddahs interpenetrate; one 'iddah suffices.
C) She only needs to observe one additional menses.
D) Her original 'iddah is cancelled and she starts a new one from the second intercourse.

Key Takeaway
The purpose of 'iddah is to clear the womb; when this purpose can be achieved through a single waiting period, two 'iddahs interpenetrate, avoiding unnecessary prolongation.

First Marital Entry Counts for Second: Two Shaykhs vs Muhammad
Intra-Hanafi dispute on dukhul carry-over

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between the two Shaykhs and Imam Muhammad on whether consummation in the first marriage counts for the second, and state the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
First consummation suffices for second marriage with same wife—two Shaykhs hold; Muhammad requires fresh entry.

This lesson examines a key intra-Hanafi debate on the effect of prior consummation when a man remarries the same woman. The ruling has practical implications for 'iddah and the woman's status after a second divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A man marries a woman, consummates the marriage, then divorces her. Later he remarries the same woman and then divorces her again before any physical consummation of the second marriage.
The marriage is valid both times; the issue is whether the first consummation covers the second marriage.
2
State the position of al-Shaykhayn
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the first dukhul counts for the second marriage. Therefore, the second divorce makes the woman bā'in without any 'iddah.
Their reasoning likely relies on the principle that the same woman's body is already 'entered' and the second marriage is a continuation.
3
State the position of Imam Muhammad
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that the second marriage is a new contract requiring its own consummation. If no consummation occurs, the woman must observe 'iddah after the second divorce.
Muhammad treats each marriage as a separate legal event.
4
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under the two Shaykhs, the woman becomes bā'in immediately and can remarry without any waiting period. Under Muhammad, she must observe 'iddah before remarrying.
This affects the woman's ability to remarry quickly.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that of the two Shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). Therefore, the first consummation suffices and no 'iddah is required.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

(۱۰۹) إِنَّ الدُّخُولَ فِى النِّكَاحِ الْأَوَّلِ يَكُونُ دُخُوْلًا فِي النِّكَاحِ الثَّانِي عِندَ الشَّيْخَيْنِ خِلَافًا لِمُحَمَّدٍ. ترجمہ شیخین کے نزدیک نکاح اول میں دخول کرنا نکاح ثانی میں دخول کرنا ہے، امام محمد کا اختلاف ہے۔ تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ شیخین رحمہما اللہ کے نزدیک اگر شوہر نے بیوی کے ساتھ نکاح کے بعد دخول کر لیا پھر طلاق دیدی، اس کے بعد پھر دوبارہ نکاح کر کے قبل الدخول طلاق دیدی تو وہ بائنہ ہوگی، اور عدت واجب نہ ہوگی۔ مگر امام محمد رحمہ اللہ کے نزدیک دخول اول کافی نہیں ہوگا، بلکہ دوسرے نکاح میں بھی دخول ضروری ہوگا، لہذا اگر اس نے دوسرے نکاح میں دخول نہیں کیا تو طلاق پر عدت واجب ہوگی۔
Principle: Entering into the first marriage is considered entering into the second marriage according to the two Shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), contrary to Muhammad. Translation: According to the two Shaykhs, entering into the first marriage is entering into the second marriage; Imam Muhammad differs. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that according to the two Shaykhs, if a husband consummates the marriage with his wife, then divorces her, then remarries her and divorces her before consummation, then she becomes bain (separated) and no 'iddah is obligatory. But according to Imam Muhammad, the first consummation is not sufficient; rather, consummation in the second marriage is also necessary. Therefore, if he did not consummate in the second marriage, then upon divorce, 'iddah is obligatory.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute (ikhtilaf) between the two Shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) on one side and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani on the other. The issue is whether consummation (dukhul) in a first marriage can be considered as consummation for a subsequent remarriage to the same woman. The scenario: A man marries a woman, consummates the marriage, then divorces her. Thereafter, he remarries her but divorces her again before any physical consummation of the second marriage. The two Shaykhs rule that the first consummation suffices for the second marriage, so she becomes separated (bā'in) and does not observe any 'iddah. Their reasoning likely relies on the principle that once a woman has been physically entered, the requirement for entry is fulfilled for any subsequent marriage with the same husband, as long as the second marriage occurs before the 'iddah of the first divorce expires? The text doesn't specify the exact evidence. Imam Muhammad, however, holds that the first entry does not carry over; the second marriage is a new contract requiring its own consummation. Consequently, if no entry occurs, she must observe 'iddah upon divorce. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is not indicated in this passage, but it is known that the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf is preferred in the Hanafi school for this issue. The passage is from the Usool al-Hidayah, which extracts principles from Al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
الشَّيْخَيْنِ (al-Shaykhayn): The two Shaykhs refer to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the two founding imams of the Hanafi school.
الدُّخُول (al-dukhul): Physical consummation of marriage; actual intercourse.
بَائِنَة (bā'inah): A woman who is irreversibly divorced (talaq bā'in), meaning the husband cannot take her back without a new marriage and consent.

First Marriage's Consummation Counts for Second Marriage (Shaykhayn vs Muhammad)
Stating an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether first marriage's consummation applies to second marriage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the position of the Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) on whether prior consummation applies to a subsequent marriage, and explain the practical consequences for mahr and iddah.

Core Idea
Prior consummation with the same spouse carries over to a second marriage according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

This lesson addresses a specific intra-Hanafi dispute: if a man consummates a marriage, divorces, then remarries the same woman and divorces before consummating the second marriage, does the first consummation count? The answer affects whether full or half mahr is due and whether a new iddah begins. The Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) say yes, while Imam Muhammad says no. Understanding this principle is crucial for correctly applying rulings on mahr and iddah in successive marriages.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the two positions
Recognize that the dispute is about whether consummation in the first marriage is considered consummation for the second marriage when the same spouses remarry. The Shaykhayn affirm it; Imam Muhammad denies it.
The 'Shaykhayn' are Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
2
Understand the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under the Shaykhayn's view, the second divorce is treated as after consummation, so the husband must pay full mahr and the wife must observe a complete new iddah. Under Muhammad's view, the divorce is before consummation, so half mahr is due and the previous iddah continues.
This directly affects the financial and waiting-period obligations.
3
Analyze the analogy (qiyas) supporting the Shaykhayn
The text draws an analogy with possession: if someone wrongfully takes a goat (usurps) and then buys it, the prior unlawful possession suffices for ownership without new physical takeover. Similarly, prior lawful consummation suffices for the second marriage's consummation rulings.
The analogy highlights the principle that a prior state can carry over when the legal relationship is renewed.
4
Determine the mufta bihi (operative position)
The Hidayah presents the Shaykhayn's view as the established principle (qawida), and it is the relied-upon position for fatwa. Imam Muhammad's view is recorded but not adopted as mufta bihi.
Both views are legitimate within the school, but the Shaykhayn's position prevails in practice.

109: Indeed, entry in the first marriage is considered entry in the second marriage according to the two Shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), contrary to Muhammad. Translation: According to the two Shaykhs, entering (consummating) the first marriage is considered entering the second marriage; Imam Muhammad differs. Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that according to the two Shaykhs, if a husband consummates the marriage with his wife, then divorces her, then remarries her and divorces her before consummation in the second marriage, then the first consummation is counted as consummation in the second, as if the husband divorced after consummation in the second marriage. The author of al-Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that if a husband divorces his previously consummated wife, then remarries her and divorces her before consummation, then according to the two Shaykhs, the full mahr is obligatory and the woman must observe a complete new iddah. Because according to them, even though he did not consummate the second marriage, the first consummation stands in place of it, so the divorce is treated as after consummation, entailing full mahr and iddah. An analogy: if Zayd usurped Bakr's goat and took possession, then Zayd bought the goat from Bakr, Zayd is considered to have taken possession by the mere purchase, without needing a new physical takeover; the first possession stands in place of the new possession. Similarly, the first consummation stands in place of the second consummation, and the divorce in the second marriage is considered after consummation.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage discusses a legal principle from the Hidayah regarding the effect of prior consummation on a second marriage. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the Shaykhayn), if a husband consummates the first marriage, divorces, then remarries the same woman and divorces before consummating the second marriage, the first consummation is counted as consummation for the second marriage. Consequently, the full mahr is due, and a full iddah is required because the divorce is treated as after consummation. Imam Muhammad disagrees; for him, the first consummation does not carry over, so the divorce is before consummation, entailing half mahr and continuation of the previous iddah. The commentary provides an analogy: if someone usurps a goat (taking possession wrongfully) then later buys it from the owner, the prior possession suffices; no new physical possession is needed. Similarly, the prior consummation suffices for the second marriage's consummation rulings. This is a qawida (principle) with practical implications for mahr and iddah.

Key Terms
دخول (dukhul): Sexual intercourse or consummation of marriage.
شیخین (shaykhayn): Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the two founding imams of the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Half mahr, because the second marriage was not consummated.
B) Full mahr, because the first consummation counts for the second marriage.
C) No mahr, because she was his wife before.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, prior consummation with the same spouse carries over to a subsequent marriage according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, affecting mahr and iddah.

Sick Man's Divorce and Remarriage: Intra-Hanafi Details
Expanding the ruling for a sick man who divorces, remarries, and divorces before consummation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of the Shaykhayn and Imam Muhammad on the effects of a divorce after remarriage in iddah, and apply the rule of prior consummation (dukhul sabiq) to determine mahr, iddah, inheritance, and raj'a.

Core Idea
Prior consummation carries forward: Shaykhayn treat second divorce as after consummation; Muhammad treats it as before.

This passage from Al-Mabsut expands an earlier principle to a specific scenario: a sick man divorces his wife irrevocably (ba'in), then remarries her during her iddah, then divorces again before consummating the new marriage. The key question is whether the prior consummation from the first marriage affects the legal consequences of the second divorce. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the Shaykhayn) hold that it does, while Imam Muhammad disagrees. Understanding this intra-Hanafi dispute sharpens your ability to trace how legal presumptions operate in marriage and divorce cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and parties
Read the first part of the passage to understand the sequence: a sick man (marid) divorces his wife with a single ba'in divorce, then marries her during the iddah, then divorces her again before consummation of the second marriage.
The first divorce is irrevocable (ba'in), so a new marriage contract was needed for the remarriage.
2
Extract the Shaykhayn's position
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the prior consummation (dukhul sabiq) is treated as if it occurred after the second contract. This makes the second divorce 'after consummation', entailing full mahr, inheritance, a new iddah, and the right of raj'a for the husband.
This applies even if the first divorce was in health, not only on deathbed.
3
State Imam Muhammad's position
Muhammad disagrees: the prior consummation does not carry over. The second divorce is before consummation, so she gets half mahr, no inheritance, completes the remaining iddah from the first divorce, and the husband has no raj'a.
Muhammad's reasoning also notes that the law of deathbed (firar) does not apply to divorces before consummation.
4
Analyze the evidence (dalil)
The Shaykhayn's evidence is based on a qiyas (analogy): prior consummation is treated as existing after the second contract. This is a legal presumption (i'tibar) established in the Book of Nikah. Muhammad's counter-evidence is that without actual consummation, the second divorce remains before consummation, and the firar rules are not triggered.
Both positions rely on analogical reasoning; the dispute is over the scope of the presumption.
5
Determine the practical outcomes (thamarah)
Compare the two positions on four points: mahr (full vs half), inheritance (yes vs no), iddah (new vs continued), and raj'a (yes vs no). Note that the mufta bihi in the school is generally the Shaykhayn's view.
This dispute is intra-Hanafi; other schools may have their own positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا طَلَّقَ الْمَرِيضُ امْرَأَتَهُ وَاحِدَةً بَائِنَةً، ثُمَّ تَزَوَّجَهَا فِى عِدَّتِهَا، ثُمَّ طَلَّقَهَا قَبْلَ أَنْ يَدْخُلَ بِهَا فَعَلَيْهَا عِدَّةٌ مُسْتَقِلَّةٌ فِي قَوْلِ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَأَبِى يُوسُفَ رَحِمَهُمَا اللَّهُ تَعَالَى بِاعْتِبَارِ أَنَّ الدُّخُولَ السَّابِقَ عَلَى الْعَقْدِ الثَّانِي يُجْعَلُ كَالْمَوْجُودِ بَعْدَهُ ، وَقَدْ بَيَّنَّا هَذَا فِي كِتَابِ النِّكَاحِ فَلَهَا الْمَهْرُ كَامِلا، وَالْمِيرَاتُ، وَلَهُ عَلَيْهَا الرَّجُعَةُ مَاذَامَ فِى الْعِدَّةِ، وَكَذَلِكَ لَوْ كَانَ الطَّلَاقُ الْأَوَّلُ فِي الصَّحَّةِ، وَهَذَا قَوْلُ أَبِى حَنِيفَةَ وَأَبِى يُوسُفَ رَحِمَهُمَا اللَّهُ، وَعِنْدَ مُحَمَّدٍ رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى لَا رَجْعَةَ لَهُ عَلَيْهَا، وَلَهَا نِصْفُ الْمَهْرِ ، وَتُتِمُ بَقِيَّةَ عِدَّتِهَا مِنْ الطَّلَاقِ الْأَوَّلِ لِأَنَّ الطَّلَاقَ فِي النِّكَاحِ الثَّانِي حَصَلَ قَبْلَ الدُّخُول ، وَلَمْ يُبَيِّنُ حُكْمَ الْمِيرَاثِ ، وَلَا مِيرَاكَ لَهَا مِنْهُ عِنْدَ مُحَمَّدٍ رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى لِأَنَّهُ لَمْ يُلْزِمُهَا الْعِدَّةَ بِالطَّلَاقِ الثَّانِي، لأَنَّهُ طَلَاقَ قَبْلَ الدُّخُولِ وَحُكُمُ الْفِرَارِ لَا يَثْبُتُ بِالطَّلَاقِ قَبْلَ الدُّخُولِ. المبسوط للسرخسی کتاب الطلاق باب طلاق المريض، ج ۶ ص ١٦٠ ، ط: دار المعرفة
And if a sick man divorces his wife with a single irrevocable (ba'in) divorce, then marries her during her iddah, then divorces her before consummating the second marriage, then she must observe a separate complete iddah according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on them, based on the consideration that the previous consummation before the second contract is treated as existing after it. And we have explained this in the Book of Nikah. So she gets the full mahr, and inheritance, and he has the right to take her back (raj'a) as long as she is in iddah. Similarly, if the first divorce was in health. This is the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on them. According to Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, he does not have the right to take her back; she gets half the mahr, and she completes the remainder of her iddah from the first divorce, because the divorce in the second marriage occurred before consummation. And he did not mention the ruling of inheritance, and she has no inheritance from him according to Muhammad, because he did not impose iddah upon her through the second divorce, since it was a divorce before consummation, and the law of deathbed (firar) does not apply to a divorce before consummation. (Al-Mabsut by al-Sarakhsi, Kitab al-Talaq, Bab Talaq al-Marid, vol. 6 p. 160, Dar al-Ma'rifah)
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage from Al-Mabsut elaborates on the same intra-Hanafi dispute as the previous principle but specifically for a sick man's actions. The issue: a man on his deathbed divorces his wife irrevocably, then remarries her during her iddah, then divorces her again before consummating the second marriage. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (Shaykhayn), the first consummation before the second marriage counts as consummation after it, so the second divorce is treated as after consummation. Therefore, the wife is entitled to full mahr, inheritance (from the sick husband if he dies), and the husband can take her back (raj'a) during the new iddah. This also applies if the first divorce was in health. According to Imam Muhammad, the previous consummation does not carry over; the second divorce is before consummation, so she gets half mahr, no inheritance, and she completes the remaining iddah from the first divorce (not a new one). The husband cannot take her back. The reasoning for Muhammad: the law of deathbed (firar) does not apply to divorces before consummation. This shows the practical legal consequences of the difference.

Key Terms
دخول سابق (dukhul sabiq): Prior consummation of marriage that occurred in an earlier marriage with the same spouse.
رجعة (raj'a): The right of the husband to take back his wife during her iddah after a revocable divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), if a sick man divorces his wife irrevocably, remarries her in iddah, then divorces before consummation, what is the ruling on inheritance?
A) She inherits from him if he dies.
B) She does not inherit.
C) She inherits only if she is pregnant.
D) Half inheritance.

Key Takeaway
Prior consummation carries legal weight: the Shaykhayn treat it as making a second divorce after consummation, while Muhammad treats the second divorce as before consummation, leading to different outcomes for mahr, inheritance, iddah, and raj'a.

Prior Consummation and Its Effect on Subsequent Marriage: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute with Qiyas
Explaining ruling on talaq before consummation in second marriage after prior consummation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether prior consummation makes a subsequent divorce ba'd al-dukhul, and apply the qiyas analogy from ghasb.

Core Idea
Prior consummation in a former marriage can substitute for consummation in a remarriage, making a pre-consummation divorce equivalent to a post-consummation divorce.

This lesson examines a nuanced Hanafi ruling: when a husband divorces his wife, remarries her during her iddah, and then divorces her again before consummation in the second marriage, does the prior consummation affect the ruling? The Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) say yes, while Imam Muhammad disagrees. The text uses a powerful qiyas from ghasb (usurpation) to justify the Shaykhayn's position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Note the facts: a man divorces his wife after consummation, then remarries her (during iddah) and divorces her before consummation in the second marriage. The key question: Is the second divorce considered ba'd al-dukhul or qabl al-dukhul?
The first marriage consummation is crucial.
2
State the Two Hanafi Positions
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that the prior consummation stands in place of second consummation, so full mahr, new iddah, raj'ah, and inheritance apply. Muhammad holds it is qabl al-dukhul: half mahr, no new iddah (complete old one), no raj'ah, no inheritance.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, not between madhhabs.
3
Analyze the Qiyas from Ghasb
The analogy: a usurper who takes possession of an animal, then buys it from the owner, becomes owner by sale alone without new physical possession. Similarly, prior consummation suffices for the new marriage's consummation rulings.
The illa is prior existence of a legal state that fulfills the purpose of the later act.
4
Trace the Thamarah al-Khilaf (Practical Difference)
According to Shaykhayn: husband can take wife back during new iddah, she inherits. According to Muhammad: no raj'ah, no inheritance, only half mahr.
The difference affects both financial and personal status rights.
5
Evaluate the Tarjih
The text does not explicitly favor one view, but the Shaykhayn's view is presented first and supported by qiyas. Later Hanafi fatwa often follows the Shaykhayn.
Mufta bihi is generally Shaykhayn's view.

طلاق دیدی تو نکاح اول میں دخول کرنا یہ ثانی میں دخول کرنا ہے، گویا کہ شوہر نے دوسرے نکاح کے بعد بعد الدخول طلاق دی ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر شوہر نے اپنی مدخول بہا صورت میں پورا مہر بھی واجب ہوتا ہے اور عدت بھی۔ اس کی مثال یہ مسئلہ بھی ہے کہ زید نے بکر کی بکری غصب کر کے اپنے قبضے میں لے لی پھر غاصب یعنی زید نے بکر سے وہ بکری خرید لی تو صرف خریدنے سے ہی زید اس مقام ہو جائے گا اور نکاح ثانی والی طلاق کو بعد الدخول مانا جائے گا۔ وَإِذَا طَلَّقَ الْمَرِيضُ امْرَأَتَهُ وَاحِدَةً بَائِنَةً، ثُمَّ تَزَوَّجَهَا فِى عِدَّتِهَا، ثُمَّ طَلَّقَهَا قَبْلَ أَنْ يَدْخُلَ كَالْمَوْجُودِ بَعْدَهُ ، وَقَدْ بَيَّنَّا هَذَا فِي كِتَابِ النِّكَاحِ فَلَهَا الْمَهْرُ كَامِلا، وَالْمِيرَاتُ، وَلَهُ عَلَيْهَا الرَّجُعَةُ مَاذَامَ فِى الْعِدَّةِ، وَكَذَلِكَ لَوْ كَانَ الطَّلَاقُ الْأَوَّلُ فِي الصَّحَّةِ، وَهَذَا قَوْلُ أَبِى حَنِيفَةَ وَأَبِى يُوسُفَ رَحِمَهُمَا اللَّهُ، وَعِنْدَ مُحَمَّدٍ رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى لَا رَجْعَةَ لَهُ عَلَيْهَا، وَلَهَا نِصْفُ الْمَهْرِ ، وَتُتِمُ بَقِيَّةَ عِدَّتِهَا مِنْ الطَّلَاقِ الْأَوَّلِ لِأَنَّ الطَّلَاقَ فِي النِّكَاحِ الثَّانِي حَصَلَ قَبْلَ الدُّخُول ، وَلَمْ يُبَيِّنُ حُكْمَ الْمِيرَاثِ ، وَلَا مِيرَاكَ لَهَا مِنْهُ عِنْدَ مُحَمَّدٍ رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى لِأَنَّهُ لَمْ يُلْزِمُهَا الْعِدَّةَ بِالطَّلَاقِ الثَّانِي، لأَنَّهُ طَلَاقَ قَبْلَ الدُّخُولِ وَحُكُمُ الْفِرَارِ لَا يَثْبُتُ بِالطَّلَاقِ قَبْلَ الدُّخُولِ.
If he gave a divorce, then consummation in the first marriage is considered consummation in the second marriage, meaning the husband gave a divorce after consummation in the second marriage. The author of Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that if a husband divorced his wife who had been consummated, then remarried her and divorced her before consummation, then according to the Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), full mahr and a complete new iddah are obligatory. Because according to them, the prior consummation in the first marriage stands in place of consummation in the second marriage. An example: if Zayd usurped Bakr's goat and took possession, then bought it from him, Zayd becomes the possessor by mere purchase without need for new possession. Similarly, prior consummation stands in place of second consummation, so the divorce is considered after consummation. And if a sick man divorces his wife with one irrevocable divorce, then marries her in her iddah, then divorces her before consummation, she has a complete separate iddah according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, considering the prior consummation as occurring after the second contract. So she gets full mahr and inheritance, and he has the right to take her back as long as she is in iddah. According to Muhammad, he has no right to take her back, she gets half mahr, and completes the remainder of the first iddah, because the divorce in the second marriage occurred before consummation. He did not mention inheritance; she has no inheritance from him according to Muhammad because he did not oblige iddah by the second divorce, as it is a divorce before consummation, and the ruling of escaping inheritance does not apply to divorce before consummation.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ruling when a husband divorces his wife whom he had previously consummated with, then remarries her and divorces her before consummation in the second marriage. The Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) hold that the prior consummation is treated as if it occurred after the second marriage contract. Therefore, the divorce is considered after consummation, entailing full mahr and a new complete iddah. Imam Muhammad disagrees, arguing that since there was no consummation in the second marriage, the divorce is before consummation, so only half mahr is due, and she completes the iddah from the first divorce. The text uses an analogy from ghasb (usurpation) where prior possession suffices without new physical possession. The legal consequence: according to the Shaykhayn, the husband also has the right to take her back (raj'ah) during the new iddah, and she inherits from him if he dies; according to Muhammad, no raj'ah and no inheritance.

Key Terms
مدخول بہا (madkhul biha): A wife with whom the husband has consummated the marriage.
بعد الدخول (ba'd al-dukhul): After consummation; a divorce occurring after the husband has had intercourse with his wife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), what is the ruling on mahr and iddah when a husband divorces his consummated wife, remarries her, and divorces before consummation in the second marriage?
A) Half mahr and completes old iddah.
B) Full mahr and new complete iddah.
C) No mahr and no iddah.
D) Full mahr but no iddah.

Key Takeaway
Prior consummation in a former marriage can substitute for consummation in a remarriage, making a pre-consummation divorce equivalent to a post-consummation divorce in Hanafi law (Shaykhayn's view).

Iddah for Harbiyyah Woman: The Principle of Lineage Protection
Explaining the legal principle for iddah in case of harbiyyah women.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on iddah for a harbiyyah woman and explain why it differs from the general rule, using the principle that iddah is a right of human beings.

Core Idea
Iddah protects human lineage; a harbi's wife has no lineage to protect, so no iddah unless pregnant.

This passage from Al-Hidayah presents a key principle: iddah is obligatory because it involves the right of the children of Adam (ḥaqq banī Ādam). The author then applies this principle to the case of a harbiyyah woman—a non-Muslim from a warring state—to determine whether she must observe iddah when she converts to Islam and wishes to marry a Muslim. The ruling hinges on whether the husband's status (harbi) qualifies for lineage protection. You will learn how the Hanafi school derives this exception and where a minor internal disagreement arises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act under discussion is the requirement of iddah (waiting period) for a woman after dissolution of marriage. The default ruling is that iddah is wajib for all Muslim women. Identify what the text says about the basis of this obligation.
The text explicitly states that iddah is a right of the children of Adam.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is the principle itself: 'إن العدة حبت وجبت كان فيها حق بني آدم' (Indeed, iddah is obligatory because in it is the right of the children of Adam). This is a qawl (statement) attributed to the Companions or derived from the general purposes of Shari'ah.
The dalil is not a Quranic verse or hadith but a legal maxim used by the Hanafis.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof yields the ruling)
The phrase 'حق بني آدم' indicates that iddah is for protecting the lineage of the children of Adam. A harbi (enemy non-Muslim) is not considered from the children of Adam in this context; the text says he is attached to inanimate objects (الجمد). Therefore, his wife is not required to observe iddah because there is no protected lineage to guard.
The exception for pregnancy: if the harbiyyah is pregnant, the child is from the children of Adam, so iddah (i.e., waiting until delivery) is required to protect the child's lineage.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Within the Hanafi school, al-Karkhi held a slightly different view: he permitted marrying a harbiyyah but prohibited intercourse until she delivers, even if not pregnant. The text states that the first view (no iddah at all unless pregnant) is more correct (الأول أصح).
This ikhtilaf affects only the timing of consummation, not the validity of the marriage contract.
5
Evaluate the tarjih (preferred view)
The preferred (mufta bihi) position is the first: a harbiyyah may marry immediately without iddah, unless pregnant. The authority is the text itself, which deems the first view more correct.
This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah and is standard in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ الْعِدَّةَ حَبْتُ وَجَبَتْ كَانَ فِيهَا حَقُّ بَنِي آدَمَ. ترجمہ: عدت اس لئے واجب ہوتی ہے کہ اس میں بنی آدمی کا حق ہے۔ تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ عورت پر عدت گزارنا شریعت کی طرف سے اسوجہ سے واجب ہے کہ اس میں بنی آدم کا حق ہے تا کہ اختلاط ماء نہ ہو، لہذا جس پر بنی آدمی صادق آئے گا اس پر عدت واجب ہوگی وگر نہ نہیں۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر تر بیہ عورت ہماری طرف یعنی دار اسلام کی طرف مسلمان بن کر آئی تو اس پر عدت واجب نہ ہوگی، بلکہ بغیر عدت گزارنے کے بھی اس کے ساتھ نکاح درست ہوگا، البتہ اگر حاملہ ہو تو اس پر عدت واجب ہوگی ،عدت جہاں بھی واجب ہوتی ہے وہاں شوہر کا حق ہوتا ہے، تا کہ اس کے پانی کی حفاظت کی جائے، نیز اس آدمی کے پانی کی حفاظت کی جاتی ہے جو معزز ومکرم ہو، اور حربی ملعون و منحوس ہے اور انسانوں کی فہرست سے خارج جمادات کے ساتھ لاحق ہے، اس لئے اس کے پانی کی کوئی حفاظت نہیں ہے اور اس کی بیوی پر عدت واجب نہیں ہوگی ، البتہ اگر حربیہ حاملہ ہو تو وضع حمل سے پہلے اس سے نکاح کرنا جائز نہیں ہے، کیونکہ حربی مرد سے اس کا حمل ثابت النسب ہے اور فراش ثابت ہے، اس لئے وضع حمل سے پہلے حربیہ سے نکاح جائز نہیں ہے۔ الهداية: كتاب الطلاق، باب العدة، ج ۲ ص ۴۳۲، ط: رحمانيه وَلَانَّ الْعِدَّةَ حَبْتُ وَجَبَتْ كَانَ فِيهَا حَقٌّ بَنِي آدَمَ وَالْحَرْبِيُّ مُلْحَقِّ بِالْجَمَادِ حَتَّى كَانَ مَحَلَّا لِلتَّمْلِيكِ إِلَّا أَنْ تَكُونَ حَامِلا، لأنَّ فِي بَطْنِهَا وَلَدًا ثَابِتَ النَّسَبِ وَعَنْهُ جَوَازُ نِكَاحَ الْحَرْبِيَّةِ وَلَا تُوطَأُ حَتَّى تَضَعَ الْحَمْلَ وَهُوَ اخْتِيَارُ الْكَرْخِيِّ وَالْأَوَّلُ أَصَحُ.
Indeed, iddah is loved? It became obligatory because in it is the right of the children of Adam. Translation: Iddah is obligatory because it contains the right of the children of Adam, so whoever is from the children of Adam, iddah is obligatory upon her, otherwise not. The author of Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that if a harbiyyah woman comes to us (Dar al-Islam) as a Muslim, then iddah is not obligatory upon her, rather marriage with her is valid without iddah, unless she is pregnant. Iddah wherever obligatory, the husband's right is there to protect his water. A harbi is cursed and attached to inanimate objects, so his water is not protected, so iddah is not obligatory on his wife. But if she is pregnant, marriage is not permissible until she delivers, because the pregnancy is proven from the harbi, and his bed is proven. Al-Hidayah: And because iddah is a right of the children of Adam, and the harbi is attached to inanimate objects, so he is a place for ownership, except if she is pregnant, because in her belly is a child of proven lineage. From this is permissibility of marrying a harbiyyah, but she is not to be approached until she delivers. This is the choice of al-Karkhi, and the first is more correct.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling based on a principle: iddah is obligatory because it involves the right of human beings (to protect lineage). The harbi (enemy non-Muslim) is treated as inanimate and thus his right is not protected. Therefore, a harbiyyah woman who becomes Muslim does not need to observe iddah before marrying a Muslim, unless she is pregnant. If pregnant, the child's lineage from the harbi husband is established, so she cannot marry until delivering. The principle is: iddah is for protecting the husband's lineage, and since a harbi has no protected status, iddah is not required. The passage also notes that al-Karkhi chose the view that it is permissible to marry a harbiyyah but not to have intercourse until delivery, while the first view (no iddah at all unless pregnant) is more correct.

Key Terms
حربی (harbi): A non-Muslim who is in a state of war with Islam, not a dhimmi.
عدت (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after dissolution of marriage, to ensure no pregnancy from previous husband.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Must observe full iddah before remarrying.
B) Can remarry immediately without any iddah unless she is pregnant.
C) Cannot marry a Muslim at all.
D) Must observe iddah only if her husband was a dhimmi.

Key Takeaway
A harbiyyah woman’s iddah is only required if she is pregnant, because iddah serves to protect human lineage and a harbi has no such protection.

The Principle of Ghalabah: Predominant Assumption as Legal Reality
Explanation and application of the maxim 'al-ghalib ka al-waqi'' to iddah oil use

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'al-ghalib ka al-waqi'' to cases of necessity and identify the conditions under which a predominant fear of harm permits an otherwise prohibited act during iddah.

Core Idea
A predominant fear of harm is legally treated as actual harm, permitting necessary actions during iddah.

This lesson introduces a powerful Hanafi maxim that bridges probability and legal certainty: when something is overwhelmingly likely, the law treats it as actual. We will see how this principle is applied in the Hidayah to a woman in iddah who fears that omitting her usual oil application will cause headaches. The ruling shows how necessity (idhtirar) and predominant assumption (ghalabah) interact to permit what is normally prohibited, provided the intention is treatment, not adornment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
First, determine what act is being examined and its default ruling during iddah. The act is applying oil to the head (or kohl to the eye). Normally, during iddah, a woman is prohibited from adornment (zina), which includes using oil for beautification.
The default ruling is that oil application for adornment is prohibited (haram) during iddah.
2
Recognize the exceptional circumstance
The passage introduces a case where the woman has a 'strong fear' (ghalabah) that if she does not apply oil, she will suffer harm (headache/pain). This fear is not a mere possibility but a predominant probability (ghalib).
The fear must be substantial and well-founded, not fanciful.
3
Apply the maxim 'al-ghalib ka al-waqi''
The Hanafi maxim states that what is predominant (ghalib) is like what is actual (waqi'). Therefore, the predominant fear of harm is treated as if the harm is already occurring. This transforms the situation into one of necessity (idhtirar), which permits the normally prohibited act.
The legal effect: the prohibition is lifted because avoiding a predominant harm is prioritized.
4
State the ruling with conditions
The ruling is that applying oil is permissible (mubah) in this scenario, but with two critical conditions: (1) it must be for treatment (mu'alajat), not adornment (zina); (2) she must not intend beautification. If she intends adornment, the permissibility is lost.
The passage explicitly repeats: 'but she does not intend adornment'.
5
Extract the broader legal principle
Beyond this specific case, the maxim 'al-ghalib ka al-waqi'' is a general principle applicable across fiqh. It allows the law to operate on probabilities when certainty is unattainable, especially in matters of harm (darar) and necessity.
This principle is often invoked in rulings on purity, contracts, and criminal evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۱۱) إِنَّ الْغَالِبَ كَالْوَاقِعِ.
ترجمہ: غالب واقع کی مانند ہے۔
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جو شے غالب ہو وہ اس شے کی مانند ہوتی ہے جو شے واقع اور ثابت ہو لہذا اس غالب الوجود شے پر وہی حکم مرتب ہوگا جو نفس الامر میں واقع اور ثابت ہے۔
صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر عورت تیل استعمال کرنے کی عادی تھی اب اگر اس کو اس بات کا قوی اندیشہ ہو کہ اگر تیل استعمال نہیں کرے گی تو سر میں درداور تکلیف ہوگی تو اس صورت میں اس کے لئے تیل لگانا جائز ہے، کیونکہ جب درد سر کا غالب گمان ہے اور غالب واقع کی مانند ہے، لہذا غالب کو واقع کا درجہ دیا جائے گا اور اس کے لئے تیل لگانا جائز ہوگا۔
أما في حالة الاضطرار فلا بأس بها بأن اشتكت رأسها أو عينها فصبت عليه الدهن، أو أكتحلت لأجل المعالجة فلا بأس ولكن لا تقصد به الزينة، وكذلك إن اعتادت الدهن فخافت وجعاً يحل بها لو لم تفعل فلا بأس به إذا كان الغالب هذا الحلول، لأن الضرر الذى يلحق غالباً بمنزلة المتحقق فيجب الاحتراز عنه لكن لا تقصد به الزينة لما قلنا.
المحيط البرهاني كتاب الطلاق، الفصل الثاني والعشرون، ج ۳ ص ۴۷۰، ط: دار الكتب العلمية
الهداية: كتاب الطلاق، باب العدة، فصل ج ۲ ص ۴۳۳، ط: رحمانیه
Verily, the predominant is like the actual. Translation: The predominant is like the actual. Explanation: The meaning of the above principle is that whatever is predominant is like the thing that is actual and established. Therefore, the same ruling that applies in actual reality will be applied to that predominant thing. The author of Hidayah, after mentioning the above principle, explains that if a woman is accustomed to using oil, and if she has a strong fear that if she does not use oil, she will get a headache and pain, then in that case, it is permissible for her to apply oil, because the predominant assumption of headache is like the actual. Hence, the predominant is given the status of actual, and it is permissible for her to apply oil. As for the case of necessity, there is no harm: if she complains of her head or eye and pours oil on it, or applies kohl for treatment, there is no harm, but she does not intend adornment. Similarly, if she is accustomed to oil and fears a pain that would befall her if she does not do it, there is no harm if the occurrence of this (pain) is predominant, because the harm that is predominantly likely to occur is equivalent to realized harm, so it must be avoided, but she does not intend adornment, as we said. Al-Muhit al-Burhani: Kitab al-Talaq, al-Fasl al-Thani wa al-Ishrun, vol. 3, p. 470. Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Talaq, Bab al-Iddah, Fasl, vol. 2, p. 433.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This lesson presents another legal maxim from the Hanafi school: 'Verily, the predominant is like the actual.' The passage type is ruling_with_proof, using this maxim as the proof. The author explains that when a certain outcome is highly probable (ghalib), Islamic law treats it as if it is certain (waqi'). The application given is from the Hidayah regarding a woman in iddah who is accustomed to applying oil to her head. If she fears that discontinuing the oil will cause her harm (headache), she may apply oil, because the predominant fear is treated as actual harm. The Arabic quote from al-Muhit al-Burhani clarifies that this is a case of necessity (idhtirar) and that she must not intend adornment (zina) but only treatment. This shows how the Hanafi school uses probability (ghalabah) as a basis for legal rulings in cases of necessity, balancing the usual prohibition of adornment during iddah with the need to avoid harm.

Key Terms
غالب (ghalib): The predominant or highly probable assumption regarding a state of affairs.
واقع (waqi'): Actual, established reality.
اضطرار (idhtirar): Necessity that permits normally prohibited acts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A woman in iddah is accustomed to applying oil to her hair. She fears that without oil, she will get severe headaches. According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling?
A) She may apply oil only if she intends adornment.
B) She may apply oil for treatment without intending adornment.
C) She must avoid oil altogether during iddah.
D) She may apply oil only with her husband's permission.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'al-ghalib ka al-waqi'' permits an otherwise prohibited act during iddah when a predominant fear of harm exists, provided the intention is treatment, not adornment.

The Obligation of Hidad and Its Causal Basis
Ruling with causal explanation (talil) of hidad obligation and its exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim that hidad is wajib due to loss of marriage blessing to distinguish cases where hidad is obligatory from those where it is not (umm walad, fasid nikah), and identify when necessity permits limited adornment during hidad.

Core Idea
Hidad is wajib only to mourn the loss of a valid marriage's blessing.

The Hanafi school explains the obligation of hidad (mourning) for a widow not as a purely ritual duty but as a rational display of regret for losing the great blessing of marriage. This lesson unpacks the legal cause (illah) behind hidad, and applies it to borderline cases such as the umm walad and invalid marriage. It also clarifies that in cases of genuine necessity, limited use of oil or kohl is permitted without breaking hidad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its illah
The passage begins with a legal maxim: 'Verily, mourning becomes obligatory as a display of regret for the loss of the blessing of marriage.' Identify the illah (cause): loss of the blessing of marriage. This is the core reason hidad is wajib.
2
Understand why hidad is wajib
The blessing of marriage includes maintenance (nafaqah) and chastity (iffah). When a husband dies, the wife loses these benefits. Hidad is prescribed to express regret for this loss. Recognize that this causal link makes hidad a purposeful obligation, not a blind ritual.
3
Apply the illah to exclude umm walad and fasid nikah
For an umm walad (slave mother) whose master dies, she was not a wife; she had no marriage blessing to lose. Similarly, a woman in a fasid (invalid) marriage never had a valid marriage blessing. Therefore, hidad is not wajib for them. This is a direct application of the illah.
The blessing of marriage is only present in a valid (sahih) nikah.
4
Recognize the necessity allowance
The passage adds that if a woman in hidad has a genuine medical need (e.g., headache, eye pain), she may apply oil or kohl for treatment as long as she does not intend adornment. This is permitted because necessity overrides the prohibition, but the intention must remain pure.
The harm that is predominantly likely to occur is treated as actual harm (al-darar al-ghalib ka al-muhaqqaq).
5
Summarize conditions for hidad
From the illah and exceptions, derive the conditions: (1) the woman was in a valid marriage, (2) her husband dies, (3) she is free (umm walad is excluded because she is a slave, though the focus is on the nature of the relationship). Also note that necessity permits limited actions.
These conditions are derived from the illah, not explicitly listed in the passage.

Source Text

ترجمہ: سوگ واجب ہے نکاح کی نعمت کے فوت ہونے پر افسوس ظاہر کرنے کی وجہ سے۔
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ عورت پر جو حداد یعنی سوگ منانا واجب ہے اس کی علت نکاح کی نعمت کے فوت ہونے پر افسوس کا اظہار کرنا ہے، کیونکہ نکاح کی وجہ سے عورت کو نان نفقہ اور عفت وغیرہ حاصل ہوتے ہیں تو نکاح یہ عورت کے لئے بڑی نعمت ہے، لہذا سوگ منانے کی علت اسی نعمت کے فوت ہونے پر اظہار افسوس ہے۔

الهداية: كتاب الطلاق، باب العدة، فصل ج ۲ ص ۴۳۳، ط: رحمانیه
Verily, mourning (hidad) becomes obligatory as a display of regret for the loss of the blessing of marriage. Translation: Mourning is obligatory due to showing regret for the loss of the blessing of marriage. Explanation: The meaning of the above principle is that the reason why mourning (hidad) is obligatory upon a woman is to display regret for the loss of the blessing of marriage. Because through marriage, a woman obtains nafaqah (maintenance) and chastity (iffah), etc., so marriage is a great blessing for her. Therefore, the cause of mourning is the display of regret for the loss of this blessing. The author of Hidayah, after mentioning the above principle, explains that in the cases of umm walad (slave mother of a child) and invalid marriage (nikah fasid), mourning is not obligatory. This is because mourning is due to the loss of the blessing of marriage, and in the case of umm walad and invalid marriage, the blessing of marriage has not been lost for the woman. Therefore, mourning is not obligatory for displaying regret. Since the permissibility of adornment (zinat) is the original ruling, this ruling remains on its original basis. As for the case of necessity, there is no harm: if she complains of her head or eye and pours oil on it, or applies kohl for treatment, there is no harm, but she does not intend adornment. Similarly, if she is accustomed to oil and fears a pain that would befall her if she does not do it, there is no harm if the occurrence of this (pain) is predominant, because the harm that is predominantly likely to occur is equivalent to realized harm, so it must be avoided, but she does not intend adornment, as we said. Al-Muhit al-Burhani: Kitab al-Talaq, al-Fasl al-Thani wa al-Ishrun, vol. 3, p. 470. Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Talaq, Bab al-Iddah, Fasl, vol. 2, p. 433.

Commentary
This lesson presents a third legal maxim: 'Verily, mourning (hidad) becomes obligatory as a display of regret for the loss of the blessing of marriage.' The passage type is ruling_with_proof, where the maxim establishes the illa (cause) for hidad. The author explains that hidad (during iddah) is not merely a mechanical rule but is grounded in the purpose of showing grief over losing the benefits of marriage (maintenance, chastity, etc.). The commentary applies this to two cases: umm walad (a slave woman who bore her master a child) and a woman in a fasid (invalid) marriage. In both cases, the woman does not have to observe hidad because the blessing of marriage either did not exist or was not fully lost. In the case of umm walad, she is still under her master's ownership, so the benefit of maintenance continues. In fasid marriage, the marriage was invalid, so there is no loss of a valid marriage blessing. The Arabic quote about oil and necessity seems to be appended from the previous lesson (repeated in the OCR), indicating that even during hidad, necessary treatment is allowed. This connects the maxim to practical permissions.

Key Terms
حداد (hidad): Mourning observed by a widow, which includes refraining from adornment and perfumes during iddah.
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave woman who bears a child to her master, thereby gaining certain rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) A woman who obtains a khula divorce.
C) An umm walad whose master dies.
D) A woman whose husband is missing and presumed dead.

Key Takeaway
Hidad is wajib only for the loss of the blessing of a valid marriage, which excludes umm walad and fasid nikah, but necessity permits limited medical treatment without adornment.
